High pressure forecast but hot air wins the day

Author: Crawshaw, Steve

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/high-pressure-forecast-hot-air-wins-day/docview/312328477/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: INSIDE the vast International Congress Centre building, the pressure groups were in their element. At most international conferences, critics of the establishment play little more than a walk-on role. Like ticks on an elephant's back, their bites have little obvious effect. In Berlin last week, things were different - a sign, perhaps, of things to come. At the UN conference on climate change - in effect, the "How to Stop Global Warming" conference - environmental pressure groups played a key role in defining the agenda. There was a sprinkling of truly exotic delegates - like the little group who kept jumping up at press conferences and warning of the coming judgement of the Lord. There were the familiar brand of summit theatricals, too, who organised telegenic stunts outside the conference building, and who invaded the plenary hall.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=High+pressure+forecast+but+hot+air+wins+the+day&amp;author=Crawshaw%2C+Steve&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-04-09&amp;spage=17&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Global warming/ Berlin summit
INSIDE the vast International Congress Centre building, the pressure groups were in their element. At most international conferences, critics of the establishment play little more than a walk-on role. Like ticks on an elephant's back, their bites have little obvious effect. In Berlin last week, things were different - a sign, perhaps, of things to come.
At the UN conference on climate change - in effect, the "How to Stop Global Warming" conference - environmental pressure groups played a key role in defining the agenda.
There was a sprinkling of truly exotic delegates - like the little group who kept jumping up at press conferences and warning of the coming judgement of the Lord. There were the familiar brand of summit theatricals, too, who organised telegenic stunts outside the conference building, and who invaded the plenary hall.
But it was the mainstream environmental groups among the non-governmental organisations, or NGOs, who set the tone. They received fulsome tribute when yesterday's document was adopted.
The NGOs were everywhere in the giant, energy-guzzling halls. Their representatives even outnumbered the government delegates. The German government had even invited representatives of environmental NGOs to form part of the official delegation. The NGOs, worried about losing their credibility, said no.
Not that the green lobby regarded the results of the conference as a success. The conference was widely written off as a disaster before it began. And it was written off as a disaster after it was over. "A historic missed opportunity," in one environmentalist's phrase. In short: another talking shop, which got nowhere.
But the jury is still out as to whether Berlin really got nowhere - or whether it will be seen as a milestone in years to come. The critics point out that the conference did not even attempt to reach an agreement on reducing emissions of carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases - which is, after all, the point. What the conference did do, too, can be seen as puny. It decreed that proposals should be prepared for reduction targets which will be agreed (or not) in 1997. Nothing, in other words, which commits anybody to do anything. What could be measlier?
But such an analysis may leave out a crucial element: the likely pressures, partly created by the Berlin conference, in the next two years. Sometimes hot air (to coin a phrase) can just be hot air. Thus, it was a depressing experience to witness western governments congratulating one another on their "diplomatic triumph" after a huge international conference on Yugoslavia in 1992. The parallels with Berlin are clear: a set of highfalutin declarations which get nowhere and which, by failing to tackle the real issues, pave the way for the ultimate catastrophe.
Representatives of the Alliance of Small Island States, who were active in Berlin, are worried. The very existence of some islands is threatened if global warming continues because of the associated rise in sea level. For them, every delay seems to be playing with disaster. Others, too, are beginning to get worried - though sometimes with more ambivalent feelings. China's size and industrial growth means that its carbon dioxide emissions are, in absolute terms, great - though much smaller, per capita, then in the West. And yet China is also afraid of the implications of global warming of potentially destructive storms or damage to coastal areas. In this respect, China has found an ally in populous India, which has the same ambivalence.
The two-year delay is intended to give an opportunity to work out how reductions will be carried out, and who will carry the burden. That may be dull - but not necessarily useless. In the long term, this process may prove more significant than the razzmatazz pledges made at the earth summit in Rio in 1992.
From now on, whenever major floods or hurricanes occur (insurance companies warned last week that they fear an American hurricane which could cost $50bn), television news is likely to mention global warming as a possible cause. That, in turn, will make voters more aware. Voters' awareness will put pressure on the politicians. And political pressures will make global warming more of a "sexy" issue for the editors of television news. Thus, the circle closes, and the concerns seem likely to spiral.
Of course, the politicians could go back on their commitments. But would the voters accept such an obvious lack of good faith? It was, in any case, interesting to note how many delegates in Berlin seemed almost to hope that they would one day be forced to seize the nettle.
Already, there is what Michael Zammit Cutajar, the UN official with responsibility for the new secretariat devoted to global warming, calls the creation of "a level playing field" between north and south.
"Before, it was always developing countries which were on the mat. Now, it's the other way round. The developed countries are under scrutiny. That's a good thing." these (above) in Shenyang, China, will still belch out greenhouse gases. Chinese factory workers (right) take a cigarette break PHOTOGRAPHS: TOM STODDART/KATZ

No time for world to bask in the sun: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Walker, John

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/no-time-world-bask-sun/docview/252141502/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Economist, in its usual unctuous editorial voice, reassured penguins and people that there was no need to panic if the UN conference on global warming accomplished little in Berlin, because earthly catastrophe was not visible ahead. ``There is still time to bask in the sun.'' You don't have to be a ``panicked'' Green activist to recognize the signs of political hypocrisy and economic coverup in the disarray in Berlin over the first review of the Climate Change Convention, one of the few successes of the Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro in 1992. But there was Sheila Copps, minister of our so-called environment ministry, telling The Ottawa Citizen how sad it was that Canadians were less interested in the environment as she flew off to Berlin to stand with the Americans against firm reduction targets.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=No+time+for+world+to+bask+in+the+sun&amp;author=Walker%2C+John&amp;issn=0839296X&amp;title=Edmonton+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-04-09&amp;spage=C.8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: That environmental charade in Berlin might be the basis for a satirical film were it not about something that could be less than amusing, the planet's future.
The Economist, in its usual unctuous editorial voice, reassured penguins and people that there was no need to panic if the UN conference on global warming accomplished little in Berlin, because earthly catastrophe was not visible ahead. ``There is still time to bask in the sun.''
That is like telling 40,000 Newfoundland fishermen five years ago fish all you like, the cod will always be there.
You don't have to be a ``panicked'' Green activist to recognize the signs of political hypocrisy and economic coverup in the disarray in Berlin over the first review of the Climate Change Convention, one of the few successes of the Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro in 1992.
But there was Sheila Copps, minister of our so-called environment ministry, telling The Ottawa Citizen how sad it was that Canadians were less interested in the environment as she flew off to Berlin to stand with the Americans against firm reduction targets.
Those are the Americans who are so eager to get Brazil to stop burning the rainforest, but emit more ``greenhouse'' gases than any other country in the world. Canada is second. And the U.S. has done less to meet the modest target set at Rio of stabilizing carbon dioxide emissions at 1990 levels by the year 2000.
In Berlin, there was the heartwarming sight of insurance company executives rushing around like tree-huggers urging their fellow capitalists to please reduce those greenhouse gases because we can't afford more California flood claims since El Nino distorted the weather patterns.
There were the guys from those oil and coal corporations, whose annual reports now feature how they love the environment, button-holing delegates to remind them how costly it will be to eliminate those gases or to encourage alternate fuels. While the top gaseous producing countries -- the U.S., Canada, Australia and Japan - fought for the soft and flabby target, Middle Eastern oil-producing states fought for the veto on any change at all.
And the emerging economic giants, China and India, whose energy needs depend largely on coal, urged their fellow Third World developing nations to support tougher targets for the polluters so they can break into the new industrial world at last.
Nobody, however, was listening to the poor island states of the Pacific, the Caribbean or the Indian Ocean which could be flooded or drowned if global warming melts the ice caps and raises the oceans as scientists predict for the next century.
Of course, nobody knows for sure whether this will happen, any more than fish experts know whether the turbot or the salmon will come back. But it ill behooves any of us to avoid dealing collectively with these serious global environmental problems and not merely with platitudinous words rather than achievable actions.
The European Community has been pushing for tougher reduction targets than the U.S. or Canada want to accept. But all of the rich industrialized countries in Berlin have been pointing the finger at the poor Third World countries for doing little or nothing to reach the modest Rio targets.
Yet these countries that have polluted the atmosphere most are the very countries that have exhausted the earth's resources in enriching themselves and whose commercialization of the world's economies has driven Third World countries to deplete their forests and desertify their lands in the pursuit of modernization and wealth.
This does not mean the industrialized West must wear a hair shirt and chant mea culpas. But it should mean their governments ought morally to give serious leadership to activating the Climate Change Convention, rather than hamstringing it.
Tough economic times these may be, though they are much worse outside the rich West. If our governments will be honest with us about the planet's environmental problems, the people will be interested and support needed change. But we can't just bask in the sun, especially when those ozone holes keep growing.

Global Warning

Author: By Mark Hertsgaard

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warning/docview/109398434/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: SAN FRANCISCO Invisibly small, the flecks of volcanic dust have been wafting through our atmosphere for nearly four years now. Ejected by the eruption of Mount Pinatubo in the Philippines in June 1991, the ash dispersed around the planet like a fine mist, shielding the earth's surface from the full force of the sun.

U.S. can turn down heat: [FINAL Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-s-can-turn-down-heat/docview/306778233/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=U.S.+can+turn+down+heat&amp;author=&amp;issn=07347456&amp;title=USA+TODAY+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-04-05&amp;spage=08.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: THE EDITORIAL PAGE; Today's debate: GLOBAL WARMING; OUR VIEW: We've already taken some baby steps. Now the nation should take the lead in thinking globally.
At the start of a two-week, 160-nation conference on global warming now in progress in Berlin, a high-wire artist lost his footing, grabbed the cable and barely saved himself from plummeting 160 feet. The moment provides a useful metaphor for the current state of international action to curb global warming. Like the tightrope walker, it's barely hanging on.
Although everyone admits the science is imperfect, evidence of global warming and surveys of its costs continue to mount.
A World Bank report suggests that by 2050, sea levels could inundate Shanghai, flood almost 35,000 square miles and displace 76 million people. The Institute for International Economics estimates a 5 degree rise in world temperatures would cost the United States $45 billion a year due to drought, rising sea levels and so forth.
Can all this be linked to the billions of tons of greenhouse gases -- mainly carbon dioxide -- we generate each year? No one knows for certain, but in the last 100 years, while CO levels have risen 20%, average temperatures have risen by 1 degree - a full one-third of the world's total temperature rise over the last 10,000 years.
The risk of even greater change, perhaps 6 degrees or more, is why industrialized nations agreed in 1992 to cap their production of greenhouse gases at 1990 levels. Few nations, though, are even close. The United States, like many other nations, merely is encouraging CO producers to cut back.
Opponents say there's no rush to limit emissions, and cutting greenhouse gases will hurt the economy without benefit.
But climate change is not a steady progression. It can jerk and spike with horrific results. This is why the world's insurance industry is re-examining the way it handles storm and flood coverage.
Moreover, you can limit pollution without imposing an undue economic burden.
Since 1990, companies that produce sulfur dioxide -- an acid rain precursor -- have been able to buy and sell pollution rights. Significantly, the cost of those rights has stayed unexpectedly low. Lesson: With market-based incentives and appropriate flexibility, polluters can and will find cost-effective ways to cut SO emissions.
Why not do the same for CO? And because global warming is a global threat, why not create a global market?
In Berlin, where the climate change conference ends Friday, many developing nations hate this idea, which they fear will slow their economic development. They want, first, to see hard new commitments by the industrialized world.
Congress and the White House can answer that demand with tradable CO permits. This would show our resolve, provide a model for similar efforts elsewhere and cut other major air pollutants at home. That's a smart play even if you think global warming is a crock.

Too hot to handle In the week when global warming was at the top of the world's agenda, newspapers chose to cast their eyes elsewhere

Author: Russel-Jones, Robin

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/too-hot-handle-week-when-global-warming-was-at/docview/294862486/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: LAST week the international community met in Berlin to determine what further measures are needed to combat global warming. On Thursday it was announced that ozone depletion over the northern hemisphere has reached a record low due to high levels of chlorine monoxide and low temperatures in the stratosphere. These two issues are closely linked. They are both worsening atmospheric casualties of man's industrial activities, and they are both preventable. Indeed the CFCs that destroy ozone are also potent greenhouse gases. Warming of the lower atmosphere leads to cooling of the stratosphere, the formation of polar stratospheric clouds and accelerated ozone loss. To those who ask: "Which is the most important environmental issue?" They are both issues of critical importance to our society.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Too+hot+to+handle+In+the+week+when+global+warming+was+at+the+top+of+the+world%27s+agenda%2C+newspapers+chose+to+cast+their+eyes+elsewhere&amp;author=Russel-Jones%2C+Robin&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-04-05&amp;spage=T.005&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: LAST week the international community met in Berlin to determine what further measures are needed to combat global warming. On Thursday it was announced that ozone depletion over the northern hemisphere has reached a record low due to high levels of chlorine monoxide and low temperatures in the stratosphere. These two issues are closely linked. They are both worsening atmospheric casualties of man's industrial activities, and they are both preventable.
Indeed the CFCs that destroy ozone are also potent greenhouse gases. Warming of the lower atmosphere leads to cooling of the stratosphere, the formation of polar stratospheric clouds and accelerated ozone loss. To those who ask: "Which is the most important environmental issue?" They are both issues of critical importance to our society.
However society's response could not be more different. Whilst the causes and remedies for ozone depletion are widely recognised, there is an extraordinary reluctance to accept the implications of climate change.
Newspaper editors either ignore the issue altogether, or ask journalists such as Matt Ridley and Richard North who are making a career out of criticising the environmental movement and who write articles which seem unbelievably complacent about the effects of global warming.
The Daily Express, Daily Mail, Daily Mirror and the Today newspaper carried no coverage whatsoever. Only a brief mention appeared in the Daily Telegraph and nothing in the Times. However the Times had published a scientifically confused article by Matt Ridley (March 25), and a further piece on March 27 which cast doubt on the reliability of temperature measurements! The Independent had also published a fatuous article by its economic correspondent stating that the solution to global warming was to ignore it, get rich and then pay for the consequences. At least the Independent allowed the chairman of Friends of the Earth to reply (March 29).
Only the Guardian and the Financial Times demonstrated a coherent policy towards the issue. Several articles appeared in both papers, but perhaps the most surprising was an FT piece on the response of insurance companies to climate change (March 31). The world's six largest storm catastrophes between 1987 and 1993 cost the insurance sector $36 billion in compensation. By setting premiums according to customer's environmental record, the insurance companies hope to influence industrial practice in a way that governments seem unwilling to do. A further article on this theme appeared in the Sunday Telegraph: "Banks catch a chill on global warming". How revealing that the most conservative of institutions should be the ones to take global warming seriously.
During the four days that five newspapers could not find space for any mention of global warming or ozone depletion, those same papers devoted 163 pages to sport and an incredible 19 pages to the funeral of Ronnie Kray. Do people think that the burial of a malevolent old gangster from the East End is of greater importance than the integrity of the ecosystems that sustain life on this planet?

Pakistan's Prime Minister Is Sending Contradictory Messages to the US: [ALL 04/05/95 Edition 1]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/pakistans-prime-minister-is-sending-contradictory/docview/291228383/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Benazir Bhutto, Prime Minister of Pakistan, has criticized the United States and the West for having trained warriors to fight the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan in the name of Islamic holy war, and then having withdrawn hastily when the Soviet forces withdrew from Afghanistan ("Two Shootings in Karachi," March 22). The same Ms. Bhutto and other Pakistanis have repeatedly criticized the regime of Mohammed Zia ul-Haq for having sought American help during the Afghan war. Now Bhutto, only a few days after her criticism of the American policy of arming the so-called Muslim militants during the Afghan war, turns to the Americans and pleads with them "to carry out the entire cleansing operation" in Pakistan. Here is the prime minister of the Islamic Republic of Pakistan, who took a solemn oath when she became prime minister to promote the Islamic ideology in Pakistan, pleading with the Americans to help her deal with the problem of Islamic militancy.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Pakistan%27s+Prime+Minister+Is+Sending+Contradictory+Messages+to+the+US&amp;author=&amp;issn=08827729&amp;title=The+Christian+Science+Monitor+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-04-05&amp;spage=18&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Pakistan's Prime Minister Is Sending Contradictory Messages to the US
Benazir Bhutto, Prime Minister of Pakistan, has criticized the United States and the West for having trained warriors to fight the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan in the name of Islamic holy war, and then having withdrawn hastily when the Soviet forces withdrew from Afghanistan ("Two Shootings in Karachi," March 22). The same Ms. Bhutto and other Pakistanis have repeatedly criticized the regime of Mohammed Zia ul-Haq for having sought American help during the Afghan war.
Now Bhutto, only a few days after her criticism of the American policy of arming the so-called Muslim militants during the Afghan war, turns to the Americans and pleads with them "to carry out the entire cleansing operation" in Pakistan. Here is the prime minister of the Islamic Republic of Pakistan, who took a solemn oath when she became prime minister to promote the Islamic ideology in Pakistan, pleading with the Americans to help her deal with the problem of Islamic militancy.
As a democratic leader with the support of the Pakistan People's Party behind her, is it not her duty to use the party as a means to bring about constructive change among her people so that they focus their energies on community-building projects instead of indulging in senseless sectarian killings?
Khalid Bin Sayeed, Kingston, Canada
Professor Emeritus, Political Studies
Queen's University
It's demographic change, not climatic
The article "Insurance Firms Ask If Global Warming Swells Disaster Rate," March 29, suggests that climatic change is the primary culprit in the escalating insurance losses associated with hurricanes. The primary cause, however, of multibillion dollar losses is not a change of climate, but a change of demography. Hurricane Andrew, which devastated southern Dade County, Fla., in 1992, was the most expensive hurricane to strike the United States (though not the most deadly). The $17 billion of insured losses had nothing to do with climate change, but everything to do with the urban development in Dade County.
The insurance industry should look at its business practices, as opposed to focusing on the academic scientific discussion of climate change. Although industry representatives may be tempted to resign themselves to the thesis of global warming, they will save their institutions' money only by coming to terms with the overdevelopment and overinsurance of dangerous coastal properties.
Dean D. ChurchillMiami
Assistant Professor of Meteorology
and Physical Oceanography
University of Miami
The article fails to mention another important reason for the increase in severity of recent disasters. As the article points out, 21 out of the 25 largest catastrophes in the United States, in insured terms, have occurred in the last decade. What is missed is that our own human population is also exploding at an unprecedented rate. This has myriad effects that increase the severity of a natural disaster, from simple increases in population densities to building on land once considered unsuitable for habitation. The continued loss and destruction of our natural environment only exacerbates this trend when we replace it by acres of man-made structures.
Besides the obvious connection with an increase in global warming, the population explosion ensures that when natural disasters do occur, they will continue to be the most costly, deadly disasters.
Ted Tanner, Paradise, Calif. ### 30

On the endangered list: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Spears, Tom

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/on-endangered-list/docview/239935450/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: FACADE: Since it was set up in the early 1970s to bring environmental protection -- air, water, wildlife, toxic chemicals and the rest -- under one roof, Environment Canada has failed to protect the country's environment. Its plans to deal with endangered species offers an example of this shrinking role. Environment Canada still wants to stop international smuggling of endangered animals, publish studies and help provinces discuss the issues. But it will do less to protect rare plants and animals in Canada. Since it was set up in the early 1970s to bring environmental protection (air, water, wildlife, toxic chemicals and the rest) under one roof, Environment Canada has steadily lost clout and the will to protect the country's environment. It has never replaced the provinces as the main protector of the physical area we call Canada.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=On+the+endangered+list&amp;author=Spears%2C+Tom&amp;issn=08393222&amp;title=The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-04-01&amp;spage=B.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: FACADE: Since it was set up in the early 1970s to bring environmental protection -- air, water, wildlife, toxic chemicals and the rest -- under one roof, Environment Canada has failed to protect the country's environment.
Who needs Environment Canada?
The troubled department recently responded to major federal budget cuts by doing what it does best: Giving more work to the provinces, and admitting it will have trouble doing what's left.
The federal department has little influence on anything you eat, drink or breathe. And it doesn't enforce its own laws. Now it proposes reducing its role further, shifting more responsibilities to the provinces, private businesses and universities.
Its plans to deal with endangered species offers an example of this shrinking role. Environment Canada still wants to stop international smuggling of endangered animals, publish studies and help provinces discuss the issues. But it will do less to protect rare plants and animals in Canada.
The dwindling effectiveness of Environment Canada raises a question: Should it be allowed to retain its departmental status as it fades into irrelevance? Or should it be broken into little chunks that other departments -- which have already taken over most of Environment Canada's role anyway -- could salvage?
``Somebody will soon have to ask, Why do you want Environment Canada to exist at all?'' said Paul Muldoon, a prominent environmental lawyer. The key is its shrinking role and growing reluctance to regulate industries, he said.
Since it was set up in the early 1970s to bring environmental protection (air, water, wildlife, toxic chemicals and the rest) under one roof, Environment Canada has steadily lost clout and the will to protect the country's environment. It has never replaced the provinces as the main protector of the physical area we call Canada.
It looks solid enough on the outside, but on closer inspection it looks like the building facade from a movie set.
Its legacy for Canada is haphazard environmental protection under a ragged patchwork of provincial environment regimes.
The U.S. Environmental Protection Agency is much stronger. It has assigned states the duty of cleaning the air in polluted areas, with an ultimatum: Make progress or lose federal grants for building roads. It can also issue orders to cities.
The crucial U.S. law is the federal Clean Air Act, which lays out air quality standards for the country. There's no such law in Canada.
And if U.S. citizens think the EPA is shirking, they can, and sometimes do, sue -- not for money, but for a court order forcing the EPA to follow the law to the letter. Individual Canadians don't have such extensive rights.
In Canada, the provinces are the real environmental cops. But the patchwork of environmental laws differ -- and sometimes conflict -- from province to province.
For instance, Ontario law says towns and cities must treat their sewage. Quebec has no such law, which means people on opposite banks of the Ottawa River live by very different rules.
Another example: Alberta has outlawed the sale of gall bladders taken from bears. Demand for the gall bladders, which are used in traditional Oriental medicines, has stimulated poaching and threatens bear populations in many parts of Canada.
``But it's legal to trade in bear gall bladders in Saskatchewan, which is right next door (to Alberta),'' said Judy Levita of the World Wildlife Fund. ``So you get people poaching bears in Alberta and taking the gall bladders across the (Saskatchewan) border to sell.''
Closer to home, Quebec has banned the sale of a tiny endangered plant, the wild leek. But the edible plant, which looks a little like a baby onion, can be legally picked and sold in Ontario. It's not endangered here. Officials believe many of the wild leeks sold in Ottawa each summer come illegally from Quebec.
And environmental taxes, soft drink deposits, garbage dumping fees and other economic measures vary widely from province to province.
Budget night brought the issue into sharp focus.
Canada's usually vocal environmental activists remained silent as federal cuts wiped out 1,400 of 5,700 Environment Canada jobs, and slashed the department's financing to $503 million from $737 million.
More than that, they complimented the Liberal government on a budget that would help the environment by giving fewer subsidies to energy megaprojects.
Their lack of concern over such drastic cuts is the most obvious evidence the department matters less and less.
There's certainly a role for a single national body that provides an overview for Canada. That's what the department was supposed to do since 1971, when the federal government announced plans to create Environment Canada. (The promise of the day was to ``put ecology ahead of economics whenever a choice has to be made between the two.'')
At the very least, Canada needs a body to do research on national and global issues, and to run a national wildlife service. And we need someone to represent us internationally because pollution knows no boundaries. We keep signing international agreements -- on protecting plant and animal species, on acid rain, on climate change, on the thinning ozone layer. Once signed, they need enforcement at home.
But anyone who honestly believes Environment Canada fills a national leadership role also believes those no-name strikebreakers playing baseball in Florida are real Montreal Expos and real Toronto Blue Jays.
Yet the image that Environment Canada is the main environmental agency in this country persists with the public. If we have 5,700 people (for now) under the Environment Canada banner, doesn't that mean it's the national leader in making this country a better place to live?
No.
The air you breathe is one example of Environment Canada's irrelevance.
Air pollutants have become almost the exclusive territory of the provinces. British Columbia has ordered car owners to test tailpipe emissions once a year and get repairs if the engine is too dirty. Ontario may follow. But there's no nationwide rule.
What about the water?
Municipal governments handle the basic stuff, sewage and drinking water. Provinces regulate industrial waste water. Environment Canada shares responsibility for some waterways, such as the St. Lawrence River.
It likes to brag about the $3-million fine it won against an industry east of Montreal, but it doesn't tell people it watched the daily flushing of tonnes of pollutants into the river for 30 years before laying charges.
In the Great Lakes, which hold more than one-fifth of the world's fresh water, Environment Canada says it's still committed to spending $110 million on conservation and cleanups. But it will spread the spending over a longer period.
And it says it may not be able to achieve some of its targets because of the budget.
Which targets? Cleaning sites of major contamination, destroying PCBs, protecting wetlands and studying the impact of climate change in the ``basin'' of the lakes. Other target dates have been extended a year or two: cleaning tainted sediments, reducing toxic pollution, and protecting wildlife.
Remember, this is an international issue; our commitment to protect the lakes dates to a Canada-U.S. treaty signed in 1909.
Canada's weak environmental performance has prompted international criticism. By the early 1990s, at the height of the acid rain debate, U.S. regulators often pointed out that their country had 150 power plants equipped with ``scrubbers'' to clean acid gases out of their smoke, while Canada, for all its tree-hugging rhetoric, had none.
The department is earning a reputation for admitting it can't handle its job. Last fall, its scientists decided Environment Canada had neither the jurisdiction nor the scientific know-how to protect endangered species. It does a darn good job of placing the animals on a list. Beyond that, one hopes the dying beluga whales are impressed by arguments over jurisdiction.
On land, the provinces do most of the real work in regulating forestry: That's why British Columbia regulates forest protection to a greater extent than the neighboring Yukon, where forests are federally regulated.
And other federal departments do a huge proportion of environmental work.
Environment Canada doesn't even enforce its own laws. Last June's annual report was typical. In the previous 12 months, Environment Canada had caught 136 people or companies breaking environmental laws, and prosecuted just eight of them. The other 128 got off with warnings.
By contrast, Ontario prosecuted more than 400 environment cases last year, winning convictions in 345 of them, with a similar-sized enforcement staff.
``If you have no enforcement, you might as well have no regulations,'' says Heather Hamilton, head of the Canadian Biodiversity Network, a group dedicated to protecting the full range of plants and animals.
Just a few years ago, Environment Canada was claiming it had the toughest pollution law in the western world. It no longer makes that claim.
So what's left? Great scientific research. Environment Canada is a world leader in understanding our endangered ozone layer, global warming, the long-range drift of toxic chemicals, threats to wildlife, and a lot of other topics. If Environment Canada goes, someone must pick up the pieces.
But the department must be more than a research institute. Canadians keep telling pollsters they want someone in authority to be waving the green flag, while Environment Canada, anxious not to offend, bogs down in endless ``multistakeholder consultations.'' If it flies a flag at all, it's pure white.
Environment Minister Sheila Copps disagrees sharply with most of this assessment.
The fact environmental powers are spread over different jurisdictions ``is a plus, not a minus,'' she said in an interview, because it involves everyone in the decisions rather than leaving all the power to one department to wield ``in a command-and-control mode.''
And she said we need a central body that will represent us internationally. Canada's federal-provincial position on climate change, set for last week's conference in Berlin, may be imperfect, she said, but we would have far less if the provinces were left on their own.
``As weak as our position is, it certainly would be far weaker if we did not have a department that was pushing us forward.''
That much is certainly true. Provinces can't deal internationally, and Canada keeps signing international agreements. But Environment Canada doesn't fill this role very strongly, anyway.
Seven years after Canada hosted the granddaddy of climate change conferences (Our Changing Atmosphere in 1988), plans to reduce our output of ``greenhouse'' gases are still somewhere off in the future.
As David Runnalls of the Institute for Research on Public Policy says: ``Canada is good at signing all sorts of things that the federal government has no chance of enforcing,'' usually because, as with climate change and endangered species, the provinces have all the power that matters.
He favors keeping Environment Canada in some form, but adds that questioning its right to exist ``is a legitimate question'' because of its chronic weakness.
A second danger is that busting up the department would leave the valuable fragments such as its researchers as vassals without an overlord. They wouldn't last long under a cost-cutting government. One remedy might involve breaking them off into separate research institutes associated with universities, where they would be far safer from political censorship.
Abolishing the department would be admitting defeat to the world, admitting that we have little national will protecting the physical body that we call Canada. But it would be a starting point for building something better.
Environment Canada good and dead might achieve more than Environment Canada on life support.
Who's in control
Environmental responsibilities:
Local government: Sewage treatment, garbage disposal, recycling, composting, land use planning.
Provinces: Industrial pollutants (including major spills); standards for water and air quality, use of pesticides, most wildlife and freshwater fish protection, forestry; oversees local government's roles.
Other federal departments: Oversee forestry, mining, nuclear industry, national parks, ocean fisheries, health studies in Great Lakes, pesticide registration.
Environment Canada: Weather forecasting, science research, Canada's position under international agreements (such as climate change convention); shares responsibility with provinces on endangered species and regional issues such as Great Lakes and St. Lawrence; can set some national standards; recently said it will help different provinces ``harmonize'' their laws and regulations; environmental assessment of major projects on federal property (such as rivers).
Update
The issue: The greenhouse effect is the name given to the rise in the earth's average temperature since the industrial revolution. Scientists blame the increasing concentration of gases such as methane, carbon dioxide and nitrous oxide in the atmosphere for trapping heat.
What's new: Delegates from almost 130 nations are in Berlin this week for a 10-day United Nations conference on global warming. The meeting is the first follow-up to the 1992 Rio earth summit, where it was decided that countries should monitor greenhouse gases, specifically carbon dioxide.
The evidence: Although the complexity of the earth's climate system makes it difficult to predict the consequences accurately, a catalogue of anecdotal evidence is growing.
The oceans: There has been unprecedented bleaching of corals in Tahiti, a feature linked to an increase in ocean temperatures. Some meteorologists believe freak weather patterns in recent years, including the floods that swamped Europe, are consistent with global warming.
Weather patterns: The global insurance industry has recorded a 40-fold rise in claims arising from hurricanes and other extreme weather over the past 25 years. The formation of an iceberg the size of Cyprus in the Antarctic last month is also consistent with warming.

Hot Politics of Global Warming; U.N. Debates Earth's Fate, Who's to Blame, What to Do: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Atkinson, Rick

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/hot-politics-global-warming-u-n-debates-earths/docview/307855301/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Delegates to the United Nations climate conference here want to know if humans have caused global warming. But first, humans being humans, the conference itself has gotten hot with politics. The profound climatic questions of the day have been largely overshadowed by procedural disputes, electoral wrangling and stem-winding oratory. Oil-producing nations, for example, remained adamant on day three of the 11-day, $16 million conference that any action plan for reducing "greenhouse gases" -- believed responsible for the Earth's increasing temperature -- should require unanimous rather than majority approval. That would permit any one of more than 120 participating nations to veto emission targets for the 21st century. As delegates worked behind closed doors to find a compromise, lobbyists -- particularly environmentalists and industry representatives -- worked to promote their contrary interpretations of the scientific data on global warming and thus two different views on the Earth's fate.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Hot+Politics+of+Global+Warming%3B+U.N.+Debates+Earth%27s+Fate%2C+Who%27s+to+Blame%2C+What+to+Do&amp;author=Atkinson%2C+Rick&amp;issn=01908286&amp;title=The+Washington+Post+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-03-31&amp;spage=A.35&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Delegates to the United Nations climate conference here want to know if humans have caused global warming. But first, humans being humans, the conference itself has gotten hot with politics. The profound climatic questions of the day have been largely overshadowed by procedural disputes, electoral wrangling and stem-winding oratory.
Oil-producing nations, for example, remained adamant on day three of the 11-day, $16 million conference that any action plan for reducing "greenhouse gases" -- believed responsible for the Earth's increasing temperature -- should require unanimous rather than majority approval. That would permit any one of more than 120 participating nations to veto emission targets for the 21st century.
As delegates worked behind closed doors to find a compromise, lobbyists -- particularly environmentalists and industry representatives -- worked to promote their contrary interpretations of the scientific data on global warming and thus two different views on the Earth's fate.
Certain fundamentals are conceded by all concerned. A natural blanket of atmospheric gases helps keep the planet warm by trapping solar radiation as it bounces off Earth's surface back toward space. Without this natural "greenhouse" effect, our world would, on average, be about 60 degrees colder than it is now.
But for centuries humans have added additional greenhouse gases to the mix, particularly carbon dioxide through the burning of coal, oil and gas.
Atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide have increased nearly 30 percent in the past two centuries, while methane and nitrous oxide -- two other greenhouse gases -- have increased by 100 percent and 15 percent, respectively. Nearly half of greenhouse gas emissions come from three dozen industrialized countries, including the United States.
Scientists see signs suggesting that global warming and its consequences have accompanied this accumulation of gases: a several-inch rise in sea level this century, possibly from polar and glacial melting; a decline in Northern Hemisphere snow cover; an increase in global surface temperatures of about 1 degree in the past 100 years; the fact that the nine warmest years this century have all occurred since 1980.
The U.N. Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, in research often cited by the U.S. government, has projected that temperatures could rise 2 to 8 degrees in the next century if greenhouse gas emissions are not curbed. That, in turn, could lead to calamitous coastal and island flooding, dramatic climate changes and sweeping socioeconomic consequences.
Not so fast, say industry representatives. Where's the proof?
"The {climate change panel} has not determined that man-made emissions are having an impact on climate. There's only been a small increase in temperatures in the last 100 years, and most of that was before 1940," said John Schlaes, executive director of the Global Climate Coalition, which describes itself as the "leading U.S. business voice on climate change."
Founded in 1989, the coalition represents 55 companies and trade organizations, from the Aluminum Association to Union Pacific Railroad, with Exxon, Chevron and Texaco sandwiched in between. For these companies, curbing emissions from fossil fuel combustion could mean a loss of jobs, profits and the competitive advantages available to U.S. industry from relatively cheap energy supplies.
"There are many economic and scientific analyses that remain to be done so countries can consider the impact of their decisions on their economies and competitiveness," Schlaes added in an interview.
In a similar vein, the United Mineworkers of America circulated a statement this week urging conference delegates to reject "tough targets and timetables until essential scientific uncertainties are resolved." A Kuwaiti climatologist, reflecting an OPEC viewpoint, suggested that global warming is correlated more to "sunspot cycles and volcanic eruptions" than to carbon dioxide emissions from oil and coal use.
"All they need to do is throw in the monkey wrench of doubt," Liz Barratt-Brown, senior attorney for the Washington-based Natural Resources Defense Council, said of skeptics. "When you're dealing with a difficult subject like this, that's enough to stop progress."
To counterattack along economic lines, Greenpeace this week allied itself with the insurance industry to warn that weather run amok from global warming could cause multibillion-dollar storms that will hurt consumers by causing higher insurance costs.
The Environmental Defense Fund sought to promote the link between global warming and greenhouse gas emissions by arranging interviews with Bert Bolin, chairman of the climate change panel and a prominent climatologist at the University of Stockholm. Bolin said another huge U.N. study, due for release later this year, will show more clearly than ever the links between man-made pollutants, climate and the probable consequences.
"There are accumulating signs that there really is an ongoing climate change, the magnitude of which is difficult to assess," Bolin said in an interview. "To turn around developments in the field of energy is like trying to stop an oil tanker; it takes decades. . . . Nobody argues for draconian steps. What's important is to find a strategy, an approach, some first steps."
Credit: Washington Post Foreign Service

Subject: Global warming

Company / organization: Name: United Nations--UN; NAICS: 928120

Title: Hot Politics of Global Warming; U.N. Debates Earth's Fate, Who's to Blame, What to Do:   [FINAL Edition ]

Publication title: The Washington Post (pre-1997 Fulltext); Washington, D.C.

Pages: A.35

Number of pages: 0

Publication year: 1995

Publication date: Mar 31, 1995

Dateline: BERLIN, Ma rch 30

Section: A SECTION

Publisher: WP Company LLC d/b/a The Washington Post

The World Environmentalists find powerful ally

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-environmentalists-find-powerful-ally/docview/385023343/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Berlin GERMANY -- BERLIN Insurance executives were making the rounds of leading delegations yesterday at the United Nations conference on climate change.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+World+Environmentalists+find+powerful+ally&amp;author=&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-03-30&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   The World Environmentalists find powerful ally
Thursday, March 30, 1995
Berlin GERMANY -- BERLIN Insurance executives were making the rounds of leading delegations yesterday at the United Nations conference on climate change. Fearing environmental catastrophes will bankrupt them if governments do not halt global warming by reducing emissions of carbon dioxide, they are taking on a new role as friends of the Earth. According to a chief Lloyd's of London underwriter, Richard Keeling, insurers have suffered losses to every major storm since 1987 - $20-billion (U.S.) alone to Hurricane Andrew in 1992.

____________________________________________________________

Global warming: economists don't know best: [1]

Author: Berkoff, Jeremy

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-economists-dont-know-best/docview/312303717/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+warming%3A+economists+don%27t+know+best&amp;author=Berkoff%2C+Jeremy&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-03-30&amp;spage=18&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: From Mr Jeremy Berkoff
Sir: Frances Cairncross may be right that the most rational course is to adapt to climate change when it happens, but the operative word is "may". Nowhere in her article does she address the risks involved.
What happens if global warming greatly accelerates? To quote William Cline to the effect that the benefits of taking action on global warming do not overtake the costs until about 2150 implies that he actually knows. He doesn't, because no one knows - for certain.
Moreover, to state that the world has only so much wealth to devote to solving environmental problems is nonsense. It may decide to devote only so much, but that is quite another matter. The choice is not merely, or even significantly, between resolving water pollution problems and anticipating global warming, but in the proportion of world wealth that is devoted to any particular purpose in competition with all others. And, given the dangers involved, I would have thought that reducing the risks associated with global warming should by any standards be a priority.
The insurance industry makes its living by distributing known risks among many. Unfortunately, there is only one earth and the risks of global warming are unknown. Moreover, those who cause the problem do not share equally in the risks, and those that take action benefit those who don't. Thus, the option of leaving it to the market (however defined) is unavailable. Since the risk premium will be to some extent arbitrary, and since some consideration should presumably be given to equity, the only possible mechanism is "the laborious negotiation of complex international treaties" which Ms Cairncross so derides.
Yours faithfully,
JEREMY BERKOFF
Cambridge
28 March

____________________________________________________________

What if the globe is warming? This week's UN conference can ensure we're ready if scientists prove the threat is real: [Final Edition]

Author: Mathews, Jessica

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/what-if-globe-is-warming-this-weeks-un-conference/docview/269760104/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In this environment, not even die-hard greens expect the Berlin meeting to negotiate binding targets and timetables for cutting emissions. It is unlikely that it will ever prove possible to do so until there is conclusive evidence of warming. As long as a vigorous research and global monitoring effort is maintained -- budget cutters, take note -- that's a reasonable risk to run. Some 130 countries are attending the two-week UN conference on global warming that began Tuesday in Berlin. The meeting is a follow- up to the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro. There, delegates adopted the Convention on Climate Change, which says industrialized countries must reduce carbon dioxide emissions to 1990 levels by 2000. Few of the 127 countries that have ratified the accord can tell the Berlin conference that they can meet the goal. Environment Minister Sheila Copps said that, in an effort to break an impasse between developed and developing countries on how to stabilize emissions of heat-trapping greenhouse gases, Canada will propose international trading in pollution permits. Ms Copps will push the emissions-trading idea at ministerial-level talks at the conference next week.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=What+if+the+globe+is+warming%3F+This+week%27s+UN+conference+can+ensure+we%27re+ready+if+scientists+prove+the+threat+is+real&amp;author=Mathews%2C+Jessica&amp;issn=11899417&amp;title=The+Spectator&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-03-30&amp;spage=A.15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: As the more than 100 nations that have signed the global climate treaty are gathered in Berlin this week for their first meeting, some recent news bulletins from the planet set the stage:
* Warming is changing the shape of the Antarctic Peninsula. Last month an ice shelf disintegrated, breaking off an iceberg on which the state of Rhode Island would fit comfortably. Said one scientist: "Looking out of the aircraft window I was utterly amazed. . . . In 25 years of Antarctic fieldwork I have never seen anything like it."
* A 50,000-square-mile region of the Pacific Ocean has lost 80 per cent of its zooplankton, the microscopic organisms near the base of the food chain. The loss coincides with a four-decade rise in water temperature. One of the scientists reporting the results two weeks ago described himself as "flabbergasted" at how few fish and birds are left. "It's already pretty dead out there."
* Coral reefs from the Caribbean to the South Pacific are sick, apparently from warmer water. Glaciers are in full retreat. Snow cover and Arctic sea ice are declining. The weather pattern known as El Nino (which is causing California's current misery) is occurring more frequently and lasting longer.
None of these proves that global warming is under way. Each of them could be due to natural fluctuations. But the accumulation of suggestive evidence leaves fewer doubts.
Scientists will say on the record only what they can prove, but like everyone else they have gut feelings. For more and more of them the question is not if, but when and in what form, the greenhouse smoking gun will appear.
Though major uncertainties remain, greenhouse science has made steady strides since the convention was signed three years ago. Computer models of global climate can now produce a regional pattern that accounts for hitherto confounding patches of warming and cooling. The advance gives climatologists far greater confidence in the models' long-term predictions.
Possible impacts
Meanwhile, governments and a few industries (the insurance industry in particular) have been getting a taste of the possible costs of climate change.
The last 10 years in the United States have seen a steady stream of multibillion-dollar weather disasters, beginning with the southeastern drought in 1986, the worst in 287 years, and including the drought that closed the Mississippi in 1988, its flood five years later, California's six-year drought and its current floods, and a series of record-breaking hurricanes.
The point is not that these are proof of climate change, though more frequent floods and more severe droughts are what the models predict. What they drive home in a way that no study can is that neither wealth nor high technology is protection against weather extremes.
While they may recognize a greater vulnerability, governments have also learned that reducing carbon dioxide emissions is a lot harder than they had supposed so long as energy prices remain low. The climate convention called on the industrialized countries to bring their emissions back to 1990 levels by 2000. Of those that have submitted plans for doing so, nine of 15 countries expect to miss the goal.
Because of higher economic growth and lower oil prices than were projected, and the defeat of the Clinton administration's Btu tax, it appears that the United States will exceed the voluntary target by a whopping 6 per cent.
The political climate is also a good deal less welcoming to ambitious international undertakings than it was at the Rio Summit. As the time nears when painful adjustments may have to be made, opposition stiffens from industries and countries that will be hurt. The leaders of the coalition trying to block action are oil- dependent Saudi Arabia and Kuwait and coal-dependent China.
In this environment, not even die-hard greens expect the Berlin meeting to negotiate binding targets and timetables for cutting emissions. It is unlikely that it will ever prove possible to do so until there is conclusive evidence of warming. As long as a vigorous research and global monitoring effort is maintained -- budget cutters, take note -- that's a reasonable risk to run.
For now, success in Berlin has three elements. The conference must set up a system that will be able to act when it needs to. A mechanism to track and evaluate each country's emissions and voting rules that require large majorities but not consensus are crucial. A veto by each nation prevents action.
It must also agree to begin thinking beyond 2000 even though the modest goals for that year will not be met. Given that emissions make themselves felt not just years or decades but centuries after they are produced, a five-year horizon is ludicrously inapt. Looking ahead means that reduction plans will have to include the developing countries where most future energy growth will occur.
Finally, the conference needs to agree on the principle (known as joint implementation) that emissions reductions should be sought wherever they are cheapest, whether that is in the country that is paying for them (probably a developed country) or somewhere else (likely in the developing world).
Though the idea is politically explosive because it suggests that rich societies won't have to change their lifestyles while poor ones will, no other approach makes economic or atmospheric sense.
Greenhouse science doesn't today provide the smoking gun necessary for substantial emissions reductions. A growing number of signals from the planet suggests, however, that the time for action may not be far off.
Berlin's vital task is global readiness.
Jessica Mathews is a senior fellow at the Council on Foreign Relations in Washington D.C.
The UN conference
Some 130 countries are attending the two-week UN conference on global warming that began Tuesday in Berlin. The meeting is a follow- up to the 1992 Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro. There, delegates adopted the Convention on Climate Change, which says industrialized countries must reduce carbon dioxide emissions to 1990 levels by 2000. Few of the 127 countries that have ratified the accord can tell the Berlin conference that they can meet the goal.
Environment Minister Sheila Copps said that, in an effort to break an impasse between developed and developing countries on how to stabilize emissions of heat-trapping greenhouse gases, Canada will propose international trading in pollution permits. Ms Copps will push the emissions-trading idea at ministerial-level talks at the conference next week.
Primer
A primer on global warming:
* Effects could include a rise in global sea level which would threaten coastal cities, an increase in the frequency of heat waves and droughts, extinction of many plants and animals.
* There is uncertainty about the timing, magnitude and regional patterns of climate change.
* The world's average temperature has been gradually rising since the Industrial Revolution of the late 18th and early 19th centuries.
* Concentrations of carbon dioxide, largely from burning coal and oil, have increased substantially during the same period.
* The heat-trapping properties of carbon dioxide and some other industrial gases are well known.
* Earth's warmest years since 1861 have been 1981, 1983, 1987, 1988, 1989, 1990 and 1991.
Credit: FROM THE WASHINGTON POST

FORGET CHICKEN LITTLE; GLOBAL WARMING PRACTICALLY A FACT

____________________________________________________________

JOHN Major told MPs yesterday that former Tory deputy chairman,

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/john-major-told-mps-yesterday-that-former-tory/docview/294954504/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: JOHN Major told MPs yesterday that former Tory deputy chairman, Lord Archer, was and would remain his friend, as a Labour MP pressed him over the law on insider dealing. Dale Campbell-Savours (Workington), who was forced to quit the Commons on a previous occasion after making allegations against Lord Archer, said at question time that Commons rules "prevent me from raising aspects of the trading activities of Lord Archer, your friend . . . But in the light of the fact that the prosecuting authorities have repeatedly failed to prosecute successfully people who have committed the offence of insider trading - and we all know who we are talking about - isn't it about time there was a full review of the law?" Mr Major confirmed "that Lord Archer is my friend, has been my friend and will remain my friend in the future . . . If the law officers feel something is going amiss they will approach me and the necessary action will be taken."

Full text: JOHN Major told MPs yesterday that former Tory deputy chairman, Lord Archer, was and would remain his friend, as a Labour MP pressed him over the law on insider dealing. Dale Campbell-Savours (Workington), who was forced to quit the Commons on a previous occasion after making allegations against Lord Archer, said at question time that Commons rules "prevent me from raising aspects of the trading activities of Lord Archer, your friend . . . But in the light of the fact that the prosecuting authorities have repeatedly failed to prosecute successfully people who have committed the offence of insider trading - and we all know who we are talking about - isn't it about time there was a full review of the law?" Mr Major confirmed "that Lord Archer is my friend, has been my friend and will remain my friend in the future . . . If the law officers feel something is going amiss they will approach me and the necessary action will be taken."
* A PERSISTENT Tory critic of the Government's package of measures for the unemployed intervened as it cleared the Commons last night. Former education minister Alan Howarth (Stratford-on-Avon) asked Employment Minister Ann Widdecombe: "Do you personally look forward to a world of benefit cuts and boot camps?" Miss Widdecombe replied that she looked forward to a world of focused, targeted benefits for those in need. The Jobseekers Bill, which cuts entitlement to non means-tested benefit from one year to six months, reduces help for the jobless with mortgage repayments, and introduces measures to help people find work, was given a third reading by 269 to 239 (Government majority 30) and now goes to the Lords.

Global warming; Berlin conference must ready the world for change: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Mathews, Jessica

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-berlin-conference-must-ready-world/docview/432809958/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: * Warming is changing the shape of the Antarctic Peninsula. Last month an ice shelf disintegrated, breaking off an iceberg on which the state of Rhode Island would fit comfortably. Said one scientist: "Looking out of the aircraft window I was utterly amazed. . . . In 25 years of Antarctic fieldwork I have never seen anything like it." * Coral reefs from the Caribbean to the South Pacific are sick, apparently from warmer water. Glaciers are in full retreat. Snow cover and Arctic sea ice are declining. The weather pattern known as El Nino (which is causing California's current misery and a warmer than normal spring in parts of North America) is occurring more frequently and lasting longer. Meanwhile, governments and a few industries (the insurance industry in particular) have been getting a taste of the possible costs of climate change. The last 10 years in the United States have seen a steady stream of multibillion-dollar weather disasters, beginning with the southeastern drought in 1986, the worst in 287 years, and including the drought that closed the Mississippi River in 1988, its flood five years later, California's six-year drought and its current floods, and a series of record-breaking hurricanes. The point is not that these are proof of climate change, though more frequent floods and more severe droughts are what the models predict. What they drive home in a way that no study can is that neither wealth nor high technology is protection against weather extremes.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+warming%3B+Berlin+conference+must+ready+the+world+for+change&amp;author=Mathews%2C+Jessica&amp;issn=03841294&amp;title=The+Gazette&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-03-22&amp;spage=B.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   WASHINGTON - As the more than 100 nations that have signed the global climate treaty prepare to gather in Berlin next week for their first meeting, some recent news bulletins from the planet set the stage:
* Warming is changing the shape of the Antarctic Peninsula. Last month an ice shelf disintegrated, breaking off an iceberg on which the state of Rhode Island would fit comfortably. Said one scientist: "Looking out of the aircraft window I was utterly amazed. . . . In 25 years of Antarctic fieldwork I have never seen anything like it."
* A 50,000-square-mile region of the Pacific Ocean has lost 80 per cent of its zooplankton, the microscopic organisms near the base of the food chain. The loss coincides with a four-decade rise in water temperature. One of the scientists reporting the results two weeks ago described himself as "flabbergasted" at how few fish and birds are left. "It's already pretty dead out there."
* Coral reefs from the Caribbean to the South Pacific are sick, apparently from warmer water. Glaciers are in full retreat. Snow cover and Arctic sea ice are declining. The weather pattern known as El Nino (which is causing California's current misery and a warmer than normal spring in parts of North America) is occurring more frequently and lasting longer.
None of these proves that global warming is under way. Each of them could be due to natural fluctuations. But the accumulation of suggestive evidence leaves fewer doubts. Scientists will say on the record only what they can prove, but like everyone else they have gut feelings. For more and more of them the question is not if, but when and in what form, the greenhouse smoking gun will appear.
Long-term predictions
Though major uncertainties remain, greenhouse science has made steady strides since the convention was signed three years ago. Computer models of global climate can now produce a regional pattern that accounts for hitherto confounding patches of warming and cooling. The advance gives climatologists far greater confidence in the models' long-term predictions.
Meanwhile, governments and a few industries (the insurance industry in particular) have been getting a taste of the possible costs of climate change. The last 10 years in the United States have seen a steady stream of multibillion-dollar weather disasters, beginning with the southeastern drought in 1986, the worst in 287 years, and including the drought that closed the Mississippi River in 1988, its flood five years later, California's six-year drought and its current floods, and a series of record-breaking hurricanes. The point is not that these are proof of climate change, though more frequent floods and more severe droughts are what the models predict. What they drive home in a way that no study can is that neither wealth nor high technology is protection against weather extremes.
While they may recognize a greater vulnerability, governments have also learned that reducing carbon-dioxide emissions is a lot harder than they had supposed, so long as energy prices remain low. The climate convention called on the industrialized countries to bring their emissions back to 1990 levels by 2000. Of those that have submitted plans for doing so, nine of 15 countries expect to miss the goal. Because of higher economic growth and lower oil prices than were projected, it appears that the United States will exceed the voluntary target by a whopping 6 per cent.
Painful adjustments
The political climate is also a good deal less welcoming to ambitious international undertakings than it was at the Rio Summit in 1992. As the time nears when painful adjustments may have to be made, opposition stiffens from industries and countries that will be hurt.
In this environment, not even die-hard greens expect the Berlin meeting to negotiate binding targets and timetables for cutting emissions.
For now, success in Berlin has three elements. The conference must set up a system that will be able to act when it needs to. A mechanism to track and evaluate each country's emissions and voting rules that require large majorities but not consensus are crucial. It must also agree to begin thinking beyond 2000 even though the modest goals for that year will not be met. Given that emissions make themselves felt not just years or decades but centuries after they are produced, a five-year horizon is ludicrously inapt. Reduction plans will have to include developing countries.
Finally, the conference needs to agree on the principle that emissions reductions should be sought wherever they are cheapest, whether that is in the country that is paying for them (probably a developed country) or somewhere else (likely in the developing world). Though the idea is politically explosive because it suggest that rich societies won't have to change their lifestyles while poor ones will, no other approach makes economic or atmospheric sense.
Greenhouse science doesn't today provide the smoking gun necessary for substantial emissions reductions. A growing number of signals from the planet suggests, however, that the time for action may not be far off. Berlin's vital task is global readiness.
*** Infomart-Online ***

Signals from Earth substantiate global warming: [Final Edition]

Author: Mathews, Jessica

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/signals-earth-substantiate-global-warming/docview/275405886/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Meanwhile, governments and a few industries (the insurance industry in particular) have been getting a taste of the possible costs of climate change. The last 10 years in the United States have seen a steady stream of multibillion-dollar weather disasters, beginning with the southeastern drought in 1986, the worst in 287 years, and including the drought that closed the Mississippi in 1988, its flood five years later, California's six-year drought and its current floods, and a series of record-breaking hurricanes. For now, success in Berlin has three elements. The conference must set up a system that will be able to act when it needs to. A mechanism to track and evaluate each country's emissions and voting rules that require large majorities but not consensus are crucial. It must also agree to begin thinking beyond 2000 even though the modest goals for that year will not be met. Given that emissions make themselves felt not just years or decades but centuries after they are produced, a five-year horizon is ludicrously inapt. Looking ahead means that reduction plans will have to include the developing countries where most future energy growth will occur. News bulletins from the planet show that the environment must be protected with a global timetable to cut carbon dioxide emissions. These bulletins have appeared in many forms, includ- ing the flooding devastating California this month (below), Hurricane Hugo which tore through Charleston, S.C., in 1989 and droughts which wasted farms in North Dakota and other areas for a decade.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Signals+from+Earth+substantiate+global+warming&amp;author=Mathews%2C+Jessica&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Record&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-03-22&amp;spage=A.9&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Note
As the more than 100 nations that have signed the global climate treaty prepare to gather in Berlin next week for their first meeting, some recent news bulletins from the planet set the stage.
n Warming is changing the shape of the Antarctic Peninsula. Last month an ice shelf disintegrated, breaking off an iceberg on which the state of Rhode Island would fit comfortably. Said one scientist: "Looking out of the aircraft window I was utterly amazed. . . . . In 25 years of Antarctic fieldwork I have never seen anything like it."
n A 50,000-square-mile region of the Pacific Ocean has lost 80 per cent of its zooplankton, the microscopic organisms near the base of the food chain. The loss coincides with a four-decade rise in water temperature. One of the scientists reporting the results two weeks ago described himself as "flabbergasted" at how few fish and birds are left. "It's already pretty dead out there."
n Coral reefs from the Caribbean to the South Pacific are sick, apparently from warmer water. Glaciers are in full retreat. Snow cover and arctic sea ice are declining. The weather pattern known as El Nino (which is causing California's current misery) is occurring more frequently and lasting longer.
None of these proves that global warming is under way. Each of them could be due to natural fluctuations. But the accumulation of suggestive evidence leaves fewer doubts. Scientists will say on the record only what they can prove, but like everyone else they have gut feelings. For more and more of them the question is not if, but when and in what form, the greenhouse smoking gun will appear.
Though major uncertainties remain, greenhouse science has made steady strides since the convention was signed three years ago. Computer models of global climate can now produce a regional pattern that accounts for hitherto confounding patches of warming and cooling. The advance gives climatologists far greater confidence in the models' long-term predictions.
Meanwhile, governments and a few industries (the insurance industry in particular) have been getting a taste of the possible costs of climate change. The last 10 years in the United States have seen a steady stream of multibillion-dollar weather disasters, beginning with the southeastern drought in 1986, the worst in 287 years, and including the drought that closed the Mississippi in 1988, its flood five years later, California's six-year drought and its current floods, and a series of record-breaking hurricanes.
The point is not that these are proof of climate change, though more frequent floods and more severe droughts are what the models predict. What they drive home in a way that no study can is that neither wealth nor high technology is protection against weather extremes.
While they may recognize a greater vulnerability, governments have also learned that reducing carbon dioxide emissions is a lot harder than they had supposed so long as energy prices remain low. The climate convention called on the industrialized countries to bring their emissions back to 1990 levels by 2000.
Of those that have submitted plans for doing so, nine of 15 countries expect to miss the goal. Because of higher economic growth and lower oil prices than were projected, and the defeat of the U.S. administration's BTU tax, it appears that the United States will exceed the voluntary target by a whopping six per cent.
The political climate is also less welcoming to ambitious international undertakings than it was at the Rio Summit. As the time nears when painful adjustments may have to be made, opposition stiffens from industries and countries that will be hurt. The leaders of the coalition trying to block action are oil-dependent Saudi Arabia and Kuwait and coal-dependent China.
In this environment, not even die-hard greens expect the Berlin meeting to negotiate binding targets and timetables for cutting emissions. It is unlikely that it will ever prove possible to do so until there is conclusive evidence of warming. As long as a vigorous research and global monitoring effort is maintained that's a reasonable risk to run.
For now, success in Berlin has three elements. The conference must set up a system that will be able to act when it needs to. A mechanism to track and evaluate each country's emissions and voting rules that require large majorities but not consensus are crucial. It must also agree to begin thinking beyond 2000 even though the modest goals for that year will not be met. Given that emissions make themselves felt not just years or decades but centuries after they are produced, a five-year horizon is ludicrously inapt. Looking ahead means that reduction plans will have to include the developing countries where most future energy growth will occur.
Finally, the conference needs to agree on the principle (known as joint implementation) that emissions reductions should be sought wherever they are cheapest, whether that is in the country that is paying for them (probably a developed country) or somewhere else (likely in the developing world). Though the idea is politically explosive because it suggests that rich societies won't have to change their lifestyles while poor ones will, no other approach makes economic or atmospheric sense.
Greenhouse science doesn't today provide the smoking gun necessary for substantial emissions reductions. But a growing number of signals from Earth suggests that the time for action may not be far off. Berlin's vital task is global readiness.
Jessica Mathews is a senior fellow at the U. S. Council on Foreign Relations.
