History and science show extreme weather not on the rise: [FOURTH Edition]

Author: NORMAN MACDONALD AND JOSEPH SOBEL

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/history-science-show-extreme-weather-not-on-rise/docview/270380331/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: IT HAS BECOME fashionable (and therefore predictable) for advocates of global warming theories to pronounce every severe weather-related disaster as evidence that the Earth is heating up and on course with catastrophe. Such claims are false. The new scientific evidence that disputes these assertions is found in a recent Accu-Weather report: "Changing Weather? Facts and Fallacies about Climate Change." The slight increase in average climate temperature that has occurred during the past century falls well within the limits of natural climate variability and does not signal a man-made "greenhouse" crisis.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=History+and+science+show+extreme+weather+not+on+the+rise&amp;author=NORMAN+MACDONALD+AND+JOSEPH+SOBEL&amp;issn=2574593X&amp;title=San+Francisco+Examiner&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-03-14&amp;spage=A.15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: IT HAS BECOME fashionable (and therefore predictable) for advocates of global warming theories to pronounce every severe weather-related disaster as evidence that the Earth is heating up and on course with catastrophe. Such claims are false.
The new scientific evidence that disputes these assertions is found in a recent Accu-Weather report: "Changing Weather? Facts and Fallacies about Climate Change."
Based on an analysis of weather during the past 100 years, Accu-Weather found that:
The slight increase in average climate temperature that has occurred during the past century falls well within the limits of natural climate variability and does not signal a man-made "greenhouse" crisis.
Severe storms such as tornadoes and hurricanes have not shown any tendency to occur more frequently today than they have in the past.
The heavy January rains in Southern California are not unprecedented.
Record high temperatures are not occurring more frequently now than they have at other times in the past 100 years.
Data collected from scientists and the National Weather Service confirm that hurricanes and typhoons have not increased in frequency.
In fact, the number of tropical storms that eventually became hurricanes varies widely throughout the 109 years of record-keeping. For example, there were four hurricanes in 1992 (the year Hurricane Andrew struck Florida and Louisiana), compared with 10 hurricanes in 1887, and 11 in 1916.
In addition, concerns that tornadoes are becoming more frequent and severe are not well founded.
The data show that tornadoes in the strong (winds from 113 to 206 mph) and violent (winds between 207 and 318 mph) classifications have not increased from 1953 to 1993, the last year data are available.
Conversely, the reported number of weak tornadoes (winds less than 112 mph) has increased. Undoubtedly many small tornadoes sighted today would have gone unreported in years past. America's increased population, urban sprawl and greater public awareness have resulted in better detection. So, while we are spotting and reporting more small tornadoes, it is not clear that occurrences actually have increased.
January's 12 inches of rainfall and flooding in Southern California have led some to question whether those events are unusual and produced by global warming. Rainfall records in Los Angeles, dating back to 1878, provide the answer to both questions: No.
There were five monthly record rainfalls before this century. Los Angeles received 15.8 inches of rain in December 1889. This superseded the record-setting month of February 1884, when the city received 13.37 inches of rainfall.
Interestingly, the recent January rains, despite their drenching, were well below the record for that month. Los Angeles received 14.94 inches of rain in January 1969.
Recent independent analyses of temperature records by university meteorologists have confirmed Accu-Weather's conclusions that high temperatures in the past decade across the entire nation are not unique.
For example, the severe Midwestern heat wave and drought of the late 1980s was more than matched by the "dust bowl" of the 1930s. In fact, record high summer temperatures have been set many times in the Midwest, Northeast and Southeast during the past 100 years.
Average global air temperatures have increased by about one-half degree Celsius during the past century, but this increase is well within the range of natural variation.
It is significant that most of the increase occurred between 1916 and the mid-1940s - before the rapid increase of manmade carbon dioxide emissions that global warming theorists claim is responsible for increased temperatures.
A natural question arises: If weather events today are no worse than they were throughout the past century, why does it seem weather has gotten more volatile?
First, there are the advances in technologies used to detect and record weather data, not the least of which are global satellite systems.
Second, the electronic media efficiently gathers and reports dramatic weather information from around the world. In the age of instant communications, enterprising television networks can bring severe storms right into our living room, virtually as they are happening. Less than two decades ago this kind of instant access to dramatic events was not available to television audiences.
Yet another factor in the perceived increase in catastrophic weather is the fact that more people are living in coastal areas. This increases the likelihood that when storms strike, property damage will be great. Increasing losses may be of significant concern to the insurance industry, but they do not signal climate change or global warming.
At the same time property damages have increased, the number of American deaths caused by natural weather disasters has declined during the latter part of this century. The same technology that contributes to the perception that modern-day weather is more severe also provides a much better early warning system that gives people in high-risk areas more time to evacuate, thereby saving lives.
We are much more aware of hurricanes, tornadoes, floods and droughts but there is no evidence that they are increasing in intensity and frequency. Moreover, there is no evidence that extreme weather events are linked to greenhouse gas emissions in the environment.
The point is that weather data should not be misused to imply that global warming catastrophe lies just over the horizon.
Norman Macdonald and Joseph Sobel are meteorologists for Accu-Weather, Inc., in State College, Pa. They are the authors of the study, "Changing Weather? Facts and Fallacies about Climate Change."

EVIDENCE : Hurricanes from the petrol pumps

Author: Nicholson-Lord, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/evidence-hurricanes-petrol-pumps/docview/313318008/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: This has been a good week for coincidences. Just as a Commons select committee was being warned that the Lloyd's of London insurance market was insolvent, much of northern Europe was dis-appearing under water. The earth's unstable climate, in short, was back on the agenda. The flooding in Europe, the worst in some areas for a century, has raised a question by now depressingly familiar to climatologists. Is global warming - specifically the increased turbulence which, according to most models, will result from pumping gas into the atmosphere - to blame? The proper scientific answer is no. The reality is more complex. Since scientists at the Toronto convention in 1988 suggested a 60 per cent cut in the world's emissions of greenhouse gases - mainly carbon dioxide - little has occurred to upset forecasts of a potentially catastrophic rise of up to 3C in global temperature by the second half of the next century.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=EVIDENCE+%3A+Hurricanes+from+the+petrol+pumps&amp;author=Nicholson-Lord%2C+David&amp;issn=09519467&amp;title=The+Independent&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-02-03&amp;spage=19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Global warming is indeed to blame for the floods, says David Nicholson- Lord
This has been a good week for coincidences. Just as a Commons select committee was being warned that the Lloyd's of London insurance market was insolvent, much of northern Europe was dis-appearing under water. The earth's unstable climate, in short, was back on the agenda.
The flooding in Europe, the worst in some areas for a century, has raised a question by now depressingly familiar to climatologists. Is global warming - specifically the increased turbulence which, according to most models, will result from pumping gas into the atmosphere - to blame? The proper scientific answer is no. The reality is more complex.
Since scientists at the Toronto convention in 1988 suggested a 60 per cent cut in the world's emissions of greenhouse gases - mainly carbon dioxide - little has occurred to upset forecasts of a potentially catastrophic rise of up to 3C in global temperature by the second half of the next century.
Global temperatures continue to rise and the storms, floods, heatwaves and drought consistent with the greenhouse hypothesis still amaze us.
But it is impossible to point to one storm or flood as the consequence of global warming. It probably always will be. This does not prevent you from saying that the longer the turbulence lasts, the shorter the odds - and the greater the need to recalculate them.
The European floods may help in that recalculation. Next month the Climate Change Convention, launched at the Rio Earth Summit in 1992 and which commits its signatory nations to reduce emissions to 1990 levels by 2000, holds its first assessment meeting in Berlin. It will survey a world much changed since 1988.
First, there is widespread acknowledgement, particularly within the insurance industry, that "natural" disasters are increasingly man-made and that global warming is high on the list of suspects: the huge reinsurance bill resulting from storms such as Hurricane Hugo are at least partly responsible for the Lloyd's debacle. Second, most climatologists agree that nothing like enough is being done to curb carbon emissions. And third, the cost benefit assessment of global warming has changed dramatically.
Governments have trodden lightly in trying to curb our appetite for fossil fuels. Voters, it was argued, would not put up with dearer petrol; industrial competitiveness would be damaged. But with the bill for a single hurricane, Andrew in 1992, running at pounds 10bn, such arguments look increasingly threadbare. One recent study concluded that measures to halt global warming would cost 2 per cent of US gross domestic product, but that failure to halt it would cost between 6 per cent and 20 per cent of GDP.
Equally important, policies to curb global warming make growing political as well as economic sense. They create jobs, for example - in anything from planting trees (which soak up carbon) to wind and solar power and energy conservation. In the US the netemployment gain has been put at 3 million jobs. EU estimates suggest that taxes on pollution, instead of taxes on labour, would create 2.7 million new jobs. Merely insulating homes in Britain would generate 50,000 jobs a year.
The row over VAT on fuel gave the impression that energy efficiency means higher fuel bills and not much else. With a Government bent on boosting Treasury receipts at the expense of pensioners this was a reasonable conclusion to draw, but it was the wrong one. Cutting our carbon emissions is not only emerging as the safest and cheapest option for the future. It also means more and better work - not least improving homes instead of mopping them up after the damage.

Stormy times ahead for weather watchers: [Final Edition]

Author: By Trish Crawford Toronto Star

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/stormy-times-ahead-weather-watchers/docview/437176571/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In the past few weeks, Metro has weathered snow, fog, sleet, rain, flooding, even lightning. We've had freezes and thaws, and near-record highs like Jan. 14's balmy 13.1C. ``The risk is real we will see more frequent and intense hurricanes reaching into North America and our region,'' says Henry Hengeveld, senior adviser on climate change for Environment Canada. David Phillips, senior climatologist for Environment Canada, reports that 155 Canadians die each year from weather, with the majority dying from extreme cold. Another 500 die in road accidents in which weather conditions were the major factor, and 77 drownings are related directly to hypothermia from being immersed in cold water.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Stormy+times+ahead+for+weather+watchers&amp;author=By+Trish+Crawford+Toronto+Star&amp;issn=03190781&amp;title=Toronto+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-01-23&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: It's been a wild climatic ride.
In the past few weeks, Metro has weathered snow, fog, sleet, rain, flooding, even lightning. We've had freezes and thaws, and near-record highs like Jan. 14's balmy 13.1C.
And, after 11 days of rain, the snows came. By late yesterday, Toronto was blanketed with 9.4 centimetres of snow, and forecasters were confidently predicting more to come.
What's going on? Is it global warming, or just another cyclical blip in weather patterns?
Scientists are divided. But there's one thing they agree on: there's stormy weather ahead.
And that includes an increasing risk of hurricanes.
``The risk is real we will see more frequent and intense hurricanes reaching into North America and our region,'' says Henry Hengeveld, senior adviser on climate change for Environment Canada.
He points to several reasons:
* Increased sea temperatures, which mean greater opportunities for hurricanes to build and grow. Hurricanes need water temperatures of 27C.
* After a 30-year lull, the U.S. coast has been lashed by a series of hurricanes, 11 causing more than $1 billion each in damages in the past five years.
* An international $1.2 billion NASA weather tracking system, which includes numerous Canadian scientists, has discovered sea current and wind changes that indicate a growing area of ocean storms.
Meanwhile, Canada's climate has warmed 1C during the past century, Hengeveld says.
While the chances of another Hurricane Hazel - which caused 81 deaths and displaced 1,868 southern Ontario families in 1954 - remain relatively remote, Metro won't be caught flat-footed should a hurricane arrive.
Metro's Emergency Measures Office, which co-ordinates activities of 26 agencies in the event of a natural disaster, has been on hurricane alert twice in recent years - for Hugo in 1989 and Andrew in 1992 - says Warren Leonard, who heads the office.
``We can't control what happens but we can control our response,'' adds Wayne Brocklehurst, information director for Emergency Preparedness Canada, a branch of the solicitor-general's ministry.
Weather forecasting is a federal responsibility. Roughly 7,000 weather alerts are issued each year throughout the country, and a special disaster relief fund for uninsured damage provides roughly $12 million a year in assistance to provinces coping with severe financial loss in a storm aftermath.
More than 1 million Canadians, mainly farmers and fishermen, have special radio receivers to receive weather warnings directly.
If a severe weather disaster threatens, the 26 Metro agencies ranging from hospitals to utilities to transportation systems that participate in our disaster plan are alerted.
People are warned to refrain from any unnecessary outdoor activity, power and medical emergency staff are assembled, hotels open their doors to stranded travellers and insurers prepare make-shift offices to handle emergency claims.
David Phillips, senior climatologist for Environment Canada, reports that 155 Canadians die each year from weather, with the majority dying from extreme cold. Another 500 die in road accidents in which weather conditions were the major factor, and 77 drownings are related directly to hypothermia from being immersed in cold water.
That might sound like a lot, but Phillips says Canadians should thank their lucky stars. ``We are probably one of the safest countries in the world for weather disasters.''
Canadians have adapted well to their climate, he says, staying indoors or off water during severe weather warnings, not building on flood plains and constructing buildings to withstand strong winds.
Canada has about 80 tornadoes a year and the prime tornado zone, in the southwest Ontario corridor of Windsor-Sarnia, experiences a large storm once every five years. Of the nine most severe tornadoes recorded in Canada, seven occurred in southern Ontario, but only minor ones have touched Metro.
We feel the effects of a tropical hurricane about once every two years, Phillips says.
Meanwhile, scientists around the world are taking a much closer look at how human-caused environmental changes are affecting climate.
Jim Bruce, a former assistant deputy minister of the environment and chairman of the United Nations Committee on Natural Disaster Reduction, points out that the '90s have been declared a decade for reducing the damage caused by nature.
His analysis has shown an increase in North Atlantic storms affecting Europe, and the storm surge has resulted in Germany buttressing its dikes.
Climate-related disasters are damaging people and property at three times the rate of other natural disasters, such as earthquakes, he says.
Hengeveld of Environment Canada says hurricanes can't be blamed totally on greenhouse gasses and the warming of the globe, since we've had periods of warming and periods of ice ages before that had nothing to do with pollution.
But when ``we talk in terms of risk - the risk of increasing hurricanes in Canada - the answer is yes,'' he says.
Mike Mikitiuk, a civil engineer at the University of Western Ontario's boundary layer wind tunnel laboratory, says Toronto buildings can probably handle any wind a hurricane would be able to throw at them.
When you look at the rest of the world, Canada is doing well, says Stanley Griffin, a vice- president of the Insurance Bureau of Canada, ``We've had mild exposure to natural disasters.''
But the ones we've had in the past decade have been costly and deadly:
* The 1985 Barrie tornado, which resulted in the deaths of 12 people and $71 million in claims.
* The 1987 Edmonton tornado, which swept through a trailer park. Twenty-seven people died and 300 were injured, while damage claims reached $115 million.
* A 1991 hail storm in Calgary resulted in more than $330 million worth of insurance claims, including crops and cars that were severely dented as they sat in driveways.
* A 1993 flood in Winnipeg caused $184 million damage over a three-week period.
Still, the Canadian experience pales in comparison to the $1 billion-a-year price tag for hurricanes in the United States during the past five years.
That, along with other changes in worldwide weather patterns, has scientists scrambling to determine just how much of the problem is being caused by pollution.
The International Mission to Planet Earth project - a $1.2 billion project involving 800 scientists from around the world, run through NASA - is compiling satellite information to analyze weather patterns.
In the first two years of satellite study, says program scientist Ghassen Asrar, scientists have noticed a shifting in ocean currents from the usual tropics- to-pole motion. He adds that it appears winds are turning the warm water back from its northward pattern.
Anomalies in weather have resulted, with normally rainy areas getting drought and others getting torrential rain.
``The arguments we used to justify the expense of this project is that these events can cause damage that is tremendous,'' he says.
Frank Nutter, president of the Reinsurance Association of America, says ``Whether it's cyclical or permanent caused by global warming, we have a changed environment. Nobody in the industry questions we are in for a greater frequency of hurricanes.''
With files from Nicolaas van Rijn

GLOBAL WARMING: PLAYING IT COOL WON'T CALM THE STORM: [3 STAR EDITION]

Author: Bidgood, Lee, Jr

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-playing-cool-wont-calm-storm/docview/278596199/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: It is no wonder that in the past decade, insured U.S. property losses from natural disasters have risen alarmingly, punctuated by peak losses from hurricanes Hugo and Andrew. Through August 1994, before Tropical Storm Gordon struck, insured property losses had already exceeded those of 1989, the year of Hugo.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=GLOBAL+WARMING%3A+PLAYING+IT+COOL+WON%27T+CALM+THE+STORM&amp;author=Bidgood%2C+Lee%2C+Jr&amp;issn=&amp;title=Orlando+Sentinel&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1995-01-11&amp;spage=A.15&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Lee Bidgood Jr. is a retired chemist and an environmental activist. He lives in New Smyrna Beach.
Everyone talks about the weather, but nobody is doing anything about it. Right? Wrong!
People are adding to the atmospheric gases that keep our planet warm. We are turning up nature's thermostat by burning fossil fuels and destroying forests. Unfortunately, nature's thermostat has no readable scale, and it works after an unknown circuit delay.
As we discharge ever-larger tonnages of greenhouse gas, we can't be sure when nature's furnace will respond, or how hot planet Earth will become, but virtually all scientists expect some warming. Uncertainties about the timing, magnitude and local effects of global warming should not delay stringent measures to cut greenhouse gas emissions, despite the confusion spread by coal and petroleum interests who want to do business as usual. A confused public has failed to pressure its leaders into acting decisively.
One predicted effect of global warming is that the earth's weather will become more extreme - more floods, droughts and storms. Are we seeing the beginning of such a climate? Some rather conservative business people think so.
Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurance firm, recently urged Western nations to meet their 1992 Earth Summit commitments to reduce carbon dioxide emissions. Wolf Bauer, the German firm's spokesman, said his company is "convinced that the trend towards more frequent and more severe natural disasters will continue in the future." Bauer indicated that his company intends to reduce its risks in areas vulnerable to climate change.
It is no wonder that in the past decade, insured U.S. property losses from natural disasters have risen alarmingly, punctuated by peak losses from hurricanes Hugo and Andrew. Through August 1994, before Tropical Storm Gordon struck, insured property losses had already exceeded those of 1989, the year of Hugo.
Bear in mind that insured losses are only part of the story.
Last July, flood damage in Georgia alone reached $1 billion, most of it uninsured. And human death and suffering has no price.
Munich Re isn't the only insurance firm to be hard-hit by recent disasters. Several have gone bankrupt, and now, world bankers are becoming concerned. Two European conferences of bankers were held recently to consider the investment implications of environmental problems, especially climate change.
When hardheaded insurance executives and bankers get the message about pending climate changes, Floridians should wake up. A NASA satellite measured an increased rate of rise in sea level in the past two years. Climatologists project about a 2- or 3-foot sea level rise in the next century. Visualize what that higher ocean, with more frequent and severe storms and hurricanes, would do to our coastal cities and economy. It's a grim picture.
Reducing the threat of global warming by conserving energy and developing renewable sources makes sense. It just takes a grass-roots effort to convince the politicians.

Subject: Global warming

Insurance Firms May Join Pollution Fight The industry fears potential catastrophes, such as floods, brought on by smog-induced changes in the climate.: [Ventura West Edition]

Author: Kahlenberg, Richard

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurance-firms-may-join-pollution-fight-industry/docview/293021379/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: This spring, the United Nations is convening a major powwow on climate change, with large delegations expected from both the fossil fuel and insurance industries. The only tribe as powerful as the coal and oil interests, insurers are weighing the possibility that recent weather catastrophes are the first real results of human-induced climate change. "It's quite easy to change the climate. A volcanic eruption, a lot of rain altering oceanic salinity, increased carbon emissions," explained UCSB's [James Kennett]. So far, according to his latest calculations, the planet's climate has "flickered," as he termed it, 21 times over the eons, going from hot to cold, from ice to typhoon. Only the most recent flood has been recorded by eyewitnesses: Both the ancient Hebrews and the Chumash put out news releases 11,000 years ago. You know the one preserved in Genesis 7, 8 and 9, but perhaps not the one attributed to the 19th-Century tribal chronicler, Maria Solares.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Insurance+Firms+May+Join+Pollution+Fight+The+industry+fears+potential+catastrophes%2C+such+as+floods%2C+brought+on+by+smog-induced+changes+in+the+climate.&amp;author=Kahlenberg%2C+Richard&amp;issn=04583035&amp;title=Los+Angeles+Times+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-12-29&amp;spage=24&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Before Christmas, Earthwatch reported that the ocean off our coast commences a process of "upwelling" water from its deepest reaches to take on oxygen before flowing back around the globe.
Over eons, this process has left sediment on the sea bottom that is now being studied as a long-term record of the planet's health.
The new data suggests that global climate can frequently "turn on a button," changed by little things-even human activity-according to Dr. James Kennett, leader of the UC Santa Barbara Marine Science Institute.
There's more to the story than last week's column could cover. Here's the rest:
In about 90 days, the sun and the sea are soon going to be linked in a big environmental story because of the global-warming issue. Historic climate and weather changes, recorded in the sea bottom, along with recent episodes of severe air pollution trapping the sun's light, may be impacting a major world industry: insurance.
This spring, the United Nations is convening a major powwow on climate change, with large delegations expected from both the fossil fuel and insurance industries. The only tribe as powerful as the coal and oil interests, insurers are weighing the possibility that recent weather catastrophes are the first real results of human-induced climate change.
Fearing that the worst is yet to come, they see their interests conflicting directly with those of the fossil fuel industry in the controversy over carbon emissions, that cause smog.
The president of the Reinsurance Assn. of America, Franklin Nutter, said recently, "The insurance business is first in line to be affected by climate change . . . it could bankrupt the industry."
Last time something like this happened, the issue was shoddy, dangerous building practices. So the insurance companies fought for strict building codes. They might have to stand up for strict pollution standards this time. This month they convened a National Hazards Group. We will see its handiwork in March.
Meanwhile, while the titans square off, ordinary California homeowners are already finding notifications of flood-insurance rate hikes in their mail. In some cases, mercifully not yet in our county, it's a 50% increase.
In south Los Angeles County, along the Los Angeles and San Gabriel rivers, whole towns are having rates increased and the locals are fighting it because it's affecting real estate values.
Insurance actuaries are recalculating risk factors that had been stable for decades. This has also triggered new, complex federal requirements that flood insurance be obtained prior to mortgage transactions.
Purchases and sales are being impeded. It's like a reverse neutron bomb-an environmental problem that, regardless of whether it might harm people's health, is affecting their property. This sort of thing could spawn legions of anti-smog activists.
"It's quite easy to change the climate. A volcanic eruption, a lot of rain altering oceanic salinity, increased carbon emissions," explained UCSB's Kennett. So far, according to his latest calculations, the planet's climate has "flickered," as he termed it, 21 times over the eons, going from hot to cold, from ice to typhoon.
Only the most recent flood has been recorded by eyewitnesses: Both the ancient Hebrews and the Chumash put out news releases 11,000 years ago. You know the one preserved in Genesis 7, 8 and 9, but perhaps not the one attributed to the 19th-Century tribal chronicler, Maria Solares.
Since the unveiling of data such as Kennett's, and the arrival of the insurance industry in the middle of these environmental goings on, Solares' version no longer seems merely quaint. Now, it evokes a sensibility more and more people share as we all enter the 25th Earth Year.
Here's what the Chumash chronicler says: "Spotted Woodpecker, Sun's nephew, was the only one saved in the flood. We don't know why the flood came or how it started, but it kept raining and the water kept rising higher and higher until even the mountains were covered.
"All the people drowned except Woodpecker, who found refuge on top of the tallest tree in the world. The water kept rising until it touched his feet. He cried out to Sun, `Help me, Uncle! I am drowning! Save me!' Sun held his torch down low and the water began to go down again."
That was when the world was smogless-and all power was solar. There now seems to be hope this can happen again. Happy New Year.

____________________________________________________________

`Trading' Pollutants Is a Big First Step Toward Cleaner Air: [All 11/22/94 Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/trading-pollutants-is-big-first-step-toward/docview/291215470/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Niagara Mohawk will donate the sulfur dioxide allowances (which have a market value of about $3.8 million) to a nonprofit organization that will "retire" them. The utility thus will get a tax break of about $1 million to invest in further carbon-dioxide-reduction programs. This is good news for the Grand Canyon (which has been filling up with smog) and for the northern forests of New York (which have suffered from acid rain). It's working in New York because Niagara Mohawk is burning cleaner fuels and promoting energy conservation. It's working in the Southwest because Arizona Public Service is investing in new scrubbers at the Navajo Generating Station. Meeting in the Netherlands recently, the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (a United Nations-sponsored group of scientists) declared that "even if global emissions could be stabilized, atmospheric concentrations {of carbon dioxide} would continue to rise for at least 200 years."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=%60Trading%27+Pollutants+Is+a+Big+First+Step+Toward+Cleaner+Air&amp;author=&amp;issn=08827729&amp;title=The+Christian+Science+Monitor+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-11-22&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Much as we might wish for it, there is no easy way to get rid of air pollution - no simple answer to fume-spewing cars or fossil-fuel-burning power plants. It will take political compromise, a change in public attitudes and lifestyles, and, most of all, innovative ideas.
A groundbreaking agreement between electric utilities in Arizona and New York is one such idea. It's an important practical step toward reducing pollutants associated with global warming and acid rain.
It sounds like a game-board maneuver and works like this:
The Arizona Public Service Company is swapping 25,000 tons of sulfur dioxide with the Niagara Mohawk Power Company in exchange for 1.75 million tons of carbon dioxide.
The utilities aren't actually trucking this stuff back and forth but trading "allowances" - the amounts they would have been able to release under the Clean Air Act. By doing better than they had to in reducing emissions, they have built up credits that are marketable under 1990 amendments to the law.
Niagara Mohawk will donate the sulfur dioxide allowances (which have a market value of about $3.8 million) to a nonprofit organization that will "retire" them. The utility thus will get a tax break of about $1 million to invest in further carbon-dioxide-reduction programs.
Arizona Public Service will use its newly acquired CO2 allowance as a kind of insurance against its own carbon-reduction program in an area of rapid growth. But company officials say they intend to meet their commitment without the credits and suggest they may "retire" them as well.
This is good news for the Grand Canyon (which has been filling up with smog) and for the northern forests of New York (which have suffered from acid rain). It's working in New York because Niagara Mohawk is burning cleaner fuels and promoting energy conservation. It's working in the Southwest because Arizona Public Service is investing in new scrubbers at the Navajo Generating Station.
"This is a gutsy, innovative solution that cleans the air without breaking corporate or household budgets," US Energy Secretary Hazel O'Leary said in announcing the agreement. It proves that "market-based solutions will always yield the most cost-effective outcomes while achieving the greatest environmental benefit," she said.
A key player in the deal has been the Environmental Defense Fund (EDF), a private organization that specializes in linking science, economics, and law to find reasonable solutions to environmental problems. EDF has been advising both utilities and the Energy Department.
This first-of-its-kind agreement is good, but there will have to be a lot more to meet clean-air goals. The Clean Air Network (a coalition of groups) reported last week that "more than 100 million Americans still live in areas with unhealthful air quality." And it noted that many states have yet to come up with plans required under the Clean Air Act to meet federal health standards.
Under the Climate Change Convention agreed to at the Earth Summit in Brazil two years ago, 160 countries have agreed to reduce "greenhouse gases" to 1990 levels by the year 2000. But it's a pretty weak agreement, and many countries have done little.
Meeting in the Netherlands recently, the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (a United Nations-sponsored group of scientists) declared that "even if global emissions could be stabilized, atmospheric concentrations {of carbon dioxide} would continue to rise for at least 200 years."
This means vigorous ways to reduce polluting gases below 1990 levels will have to be found - in other words, not producing them in the first place through energy conservation and pioneering efforts like the ones that utilities in Arizona and New York have just launched.

Subject: Agreements; Air pollution; Energy conservation; Pollutants; Environmental impact; Air cleanliness; Carbon; Climate change; Industrial plant emissions; Outdoor air quality; Emission standards

THREAT OF NATURAL DISASTERS POSES HUGE THREAT TO INSURANCE INDUSTRY

Author: Ken Miller GANNETT NEWS SERVICE

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/threat-natural-disasters-poses-huge-insurance/docview/288611651/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: [Ann Deering], who last fall sponsored a conference that brought environmentalists and the insurance industry together to discuss the industry's rising exposure to disasters, said there may be disagreements over climate change -- but there is no dispute that losses to disasters are soaring. The problem facing the insurance industry is a sharp increase in the amount of insured property in vulnerable areas such as on the coasts and in earthquake-prone locations. Combined with that is an increase in the severity of storms, a phenomenon some say is merely cyclical and others say is attributable to human-caused climate disruptions such as the buildup of greenhouse gases from the burning of fossil fuels. Deering said European and Japanese insurance companies have drawn a stronger link between their huge losses and global climate change, although she said many U.S. insurers also now believe there may be a connection. Regardless, the buildup of coastal populations and property presents a vulnerability that did not exist four decades ago when coastal areas where sparsely populated.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=THREAT+OF+NATURAL+DISASTERS+POSES+HUGE+THREAT+TO+INSURANCE+INDUSTRY&amp;author=Ken+Miller+GANNETT+NEWS+SERVICE&amp;issn=07463502&amp;title=The+Salt+Lake+Tribune&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-11-01&amp;spage=D.9&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   WASHINGTON -- The U.S. insurance industry, reeling from mounting catastrophic losses and exposed to record levels of risk from storms, floods and fires, is becoming a true believer in global climate-change theories.
The threats to the industry are becoming so severe that some white-knuckled insurance executives are joining the likes of Greenpeace in lobbying for government policies to reduce global warming by searching for alternative energy sources.
"I have talked to very senior people in all major markets," said Jeremy Leggett, scientific director of Greenpeace International's Climate Campaign. "And they are unanimous that the threat is huge."
Leggett said the Caribbean insurance industry "is in a full-scale crisis," due to spiraling losses from severe weather, while insurance giants in Japan, such as Tokyo Marine and Fire, could be wiped out by a major typhoon.
In the United States, where government climate experts are predicting an increasing hurricane cycle, a possible return next year of another disastrous El Nino weather system, and years of worsening coastal erosion, the insurance industry is taking an urgent look at climate-change issues.
"Whether or not climate change is happening, the forecast is that hurricane activity will increase," said Ann Deering, president of New York-based Environmental Technology & Telecommunications Ltd., a risk and environmental consultant to insurance and other industries.
Deering, who last fall sponsored a conference that brought environmentalists and the insurance industry together to discuss the industry's rising exposure to disasters, said there may be disagreements over climate change -- but there is no dispute that losses to disasters are soaring.
Some unsettling statistics:
-- According to the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA), the nation's coastal population will increase from 112 million in 1990 to 127 million, or nearly 400 people per square mile, in 2010. In its past two annual reviews of the nation's weather, NOAA dubbed 1992 "a year of notable weather extremes" and said 1993 was marked by "highly anomalous weather."
-- According to the Reinsurance Association of America, the total insured value of homes and businesses in those coastal communities in the Atlantic and Gulf Coasts alone was $1.1 trillion in 1980. By 1993, it had risen to more than $2 trillion.
-- Through June, the more than $8.5 billion paid out in catastrophic losses this year makes 1994 the second worst year on record, following 1992's $23 billion in losses.
-- The ability for insurance companies worldwide to pay claims for catastrophic losses in a given year is estimated at $160 billion. For perspective, Hurricane Andrew in south Florida caused $16.5 billion in damages. And had it hit Miami, the total would have been closer to $50 billion. A worst-case hurricane hitting New York, according to insurance models, could cause up to $100 billion in damages.
The problem facing the insurance industry is a sharp increase in the amount of insured property in vulnerable areas such as on the coasts and in earthquake-prone locations. Combined with that is an increase in the severity of storms, a phenomenon some say is merely cyclical and others say is attributable to human-caused climate disruptions such as the buildup of greenhouse gases from the burning of fossil fuels.
"If the question is whether natural disasters pose a huge threat to the insurance industry, the answer is, absolutely," said Jack Webber, executive director of the Natural Disaster Coalition, a group formed by insurers, emergency planners, lenders and others to reduce property losses from disasters.
"Of the 20 largest insured natural catastrophes we've had in the United States, 16 have occurred since 1989," said Frank Nutter, president of the Reinsurance Association of America. "If you look at the period from the 1940s to today, 45 percent of all claim dollars have been paid since 1990. What you see is a concentration of insured losses that is extraordinary in historical context."
If, as most climate researchers now project, global warming occurs, and if it increases the numbers and intensity of hurricanes, "we are on a collision course," Nutter said.
"The amount of exposure has increased exponentially in disaster areas," said Webber. "It's becoming increasingly obvious that the danger signs are there."
Deering said European and Japanese insurance companies have drawn a stronger link between their huge losses and global climate change, although she said many U.S. insurers also now believe there may be a connection. Regardless, the buildup of coastal populations and property presents a vulnerability that did not exist four decades ago when coastal areas where sparsely populated.
"Those two trends -- shifts in populations and higher value properties in the high-risk areas -- mean you are going to experience higher insured losses," said Nutter. "That, together with the climate, is a combination of trends the industry is very much concerned about."
Deering said many insurers are concerned that if ocean temperatures are warming, the hurricane-incubation area in the Atlantic will reach farther north, increasing the odds of hurricanes hitting more populous northern cities.
"Camille was a Category 5," she said of the 1969 hurricane that killed 256 in the Gulf of Mexico. "If it hit today, forget it."
If things continue as they are, and the anticipated "big one" hits, experts say insurers will be hard-pressed to pay claims. Solutions, besides increasing rates and deductibles, include mitigation measures such as adhering to building codes and, environmentalists argue, ending construction on the shore.
"After Andrew, six or seven insurance companies in Florida went under," said Beth Millman, executive director of the Coast Alliance, a leading critic of taxpayer-subsidized insurance in coastal areas in the form of flood insurance.
"The insurance industry has underwritten development in the surf zone," Millman said.
"Andrew{in 1992} and Hugo {in 1989} woke up the industry," she said. Millman said insurance companies correctly worry that a more severe climate could ruin the business, but the reality is the industry has insured structures that could be destroyed.
"Sea levels are going up and we are going to have storms," she said. "It makes bad financial sense for the houses to be there, period. It's an untenable risk."
Illustration Gannett Graphic: Insuring against natural disaster

People: Deering, Ann; Nutter, Frank; Millman, Beth

Cleaner Cars Can Also Be Killer Cars: [NASSAU AND SUFFOLK Edition]

Author: Diana Steed. Diane Steed, the former administrator of

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/cleaner-cars-can-also-be-killer/docview/278805300/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Harvard University's Injury Control Center has also studied the safety question. The center's director, Dr. John Graham, has said in the short term, CAFE induces manufacturers to promote smaller cars; in the long run, it encourages them to reduce the weight and size of all vehicles. As a result, according to a recent study by Graham, a 40percent increase in fuel economy would cause an additional 1,650 deaths and 8,500 serious injuries every year. The analysis puts the rhetoric into perspective, and shatters the case for CAFE as a meaningful response to global warming. In fact, the government's own data show that U.S. cars and light trucks (the only vehicles subject to CAFE) are responsible for only 1.5 percent of all manmade greenhouse gas emissions. All new cars must meet the same strict anti-pollution standards, regardless of their fuel economy ratings. So, a full-sized model is just as clean as a sub-compact. U.S. air quality has improved dramtically over the past two decades. Today, the major auto-related contributors to smog are older, poorly maintained vehicles, and traffic congestion - not clean-burning, late model cars and light trucks.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Cleaner+Cars+Can+Also+Be+Killer+Cars&amp;author=Diana+Steed.+Diane+Steed%2C+the+former+administrator+of&amp;issn=&amp;title=Newsday&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-10-23&amp;spage=A.42&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   PROTECTING the environment has always meant trade-offs. Saving the Pacific Northwest's old-growth forests versus saving the jobs of thousands of loggers. Preserving fragile wetlands versus preserving private property rights. Protecting endangered species living in Southern California's dry brushes versus eliminating the brush to protect homes vulnerable to wildfires.
But if divisive questions spring up when economic interests are at stake, what about enviromental protections that put our own health and safety at risk?
This scenario describes precisely the risk of drastic increases in auto gas mileage requirements - known as CAFE standards - which many activists and some federal officials now claim would reduce the threat of global warming.
With no affordable technologies available that would allow dramatic fuel economy improvements (on the order of 40 percent) in midand full-size vehicles, the lion's share of fuel economy increases would have to flow from significant cuts in the vehicle size and weight.
And there's the rub - those are the two factors that contribute most to safety.
According to the Insurance Institute for Highway Safety, the death rates in the smallest, lightest cars are double those in large, heavy vehicles.
Similarly, the National Academy of Sciences recognizes that, in spite of safety improvements in cars, smaller vehicles continue to provide less occupant protection than larger cars. Safety and fuel economy are linked, says the academy, because one of the most direct methods of increasing gas mileage is reducing size and weight.
As a result, the academy admits, it may be "inevitable" that significant increases in fuel economy can occur only with some negative safety consequences.
But just how negative is "negative"? According to statements by the administrator of the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration (NHTSA) in 1991, the consequences would be dire. Studies by the safety agency found that fuel economy gains in the last 1970s and early 1980s had come largely from a 1,000-pound reduction in average vehicle weight. NHTSA also said 2,000 fatalities and 20,000 serious injuries occur annually as a result of the downsizing of the passenger car fleet during that period.
Harvard University's Injury Control Center has also studied the safety question. The center's director, Dr. John Graham, has said in the short term, CAFE induces manufacturers to promote smaller cars; in the long run, it encourages them to reduce the weight and size of all vehicles. As a result, according to a recent study by Graham, a 40percent increase in fuel economy would cause an additional 1,650 deaths and 8,500 serious injuries every year.
Of course, no technological innovation, let alone policy change is without certain costs.
The question is whether the safety costs of a dramatic increase in CAFE standards are significant enough to outweigh the potential environmental benefits of a higher CAFE standard?
Some activists argue that higher CAFE standards are needed to reduce vehicle emissions of "greenhouse gases" and the risk of potential climate change from excessive accumulation of those gases.
CAFE supporters say higher fuel economy will reduce gasoline consumption and cut carbon emissions. One group, the Sierra Club, even claims that it's the "single biggest step" the U.S. can take to avoid climate disaster. But simple analysis shows such claims don't hold up.
Global warming theories are controversial, but it's clear that U.S. autos have only a marginal impact at most.
All fossil fuel-burning activities (driving cars, trucks, buses; flying planes; operating factories and utilities) generate carbon dioxide, a harmless gas that is also found extensively in nature.
Simply put, global warming (if it is a reality) is a global problem, calling for global responses. That makes it very different from air pollution problems, such as smog, that are regional in nature.
But for greenhouse gases, it's not important how or where the gases are generated; what matters is the total in the global atmosphere. And in fact, the fastest-growing sources of greenhouse gases are in developing countries, such as China and India, not the United States.
The analysis puts the rhetoric into perspective, and shatters the case for CAFE as a meaningful response to global warming. In fact, the government's own data show that U.S. cars and light trucks (the only vehicles subject to CAFE) are responsible for only 1.5 percent of all manmade greenhouse gas emissions.
So even if a drastic 40percent increase in fuel economy standards actually reduce gasoline consumption substantially, the impact on global greenhouse gases would be less than four-tenths of one percent. That's hardly an effective response.
Also, contrary to what many people seem to believe, getting more miles to the gallon does not help to reduce air pollution.
All new cars must meet the same strict anti-pollution standards, regardless of their fuel economy ratings. So, a full-sized model is just as clean as a sub-compact. U.S. air quality has improved dramtically over the past two decades. Today, the major auto-related contributors to smog are older, poorly maintained vehicles, and traffic congestion - not clean-burning, late model cars and light trucks.
Nonetheless, the U.S. Department of Transportation has already set in motion a process to tighten standards for new pickup trucks, vans and sport utility vehicles over the next decade. And a global warming presidential advisory panel, which met this past week, is considering further increases, including higher mandates for new cars.
It's nearly impossible not to recognize the ironies that are shaping up around the debate over a drastic CAFE measure. Environmental activists, who regularly fight for extremely strict health and safety standards, regardless of cost, are now endorsing a measure that will kill and injure thousands of Americans each year.
And the Clinton administration, which has talked about containing health care costs, hasn't considered that auto crashes already cost $14.2 billion per year in health care costs, $4 billion of which is paid with public funds. Yet higher CAFE standards will only add to the number of motorists whose travels end up in hospital emergency rooms.
The bottom line is that the new CAFE increases are not a policy that works for America or for our environment.
The only real question is whether Americans will reject a measure that endangers lives and does almost nothing to protect the environment. **** Deadly Cars? The number of occupant deaths in 1993 per million registered vehicles that are 1-3 years old.

Updates: [Final Edition]

Abstract: Ottawa will dip into a $300-million fund to buy the licences of thousands of East Coast fishermen left without work because of the collapse of some stocks. Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Updates&amp;author=&amp;issn=11974397&amp;title=Kingston+Whig+-+Standard&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-10-15&amp;spage=21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Gas Tax Only One Option: Minister
Canada's natural resources minister said it's premature to say a new gasoline tax is being considered to help pay for anti-pollution measures. Anne McLellan said yesterday a two-cent-a-litre tax on gasoline at the pump is one of 88 recommendations now being considered by a joint industry-government task force. The recommendation, which says the tax money would go into a special environmental fund, is contained in a draft report from the Climate Change Task Force. The final report is expected soon. The minister conceded a final plan must be complete within six months - in time for an international meeting on so-called greenhouse gas emissions.
Confed Life Subject Of U.S. Suit
A jurisdictional fight has broken out over a class action suit launched in the United States against fallen Confederation Life Insurance Co. for, among other things, misrepresenting its financial health. A spokesman for Confederation Life's U.S. operations said yesterday that lawyers for court-appointed rehabilitator Palmieri and Co., will deal with the matter in due course. However, the spokesman added the Michigan court that put the U.S. operations into rehabilitation stayed all litigation against the company during the rehabilitation period. The company was seized by regulators in Canada and the United States on Aug. 11. But the lawyer for a New Jersey doctor who initiated the class action last June said that since the action was filed in U.S. Federal Court, it will be up to this court to decide whether the action will proceed. The plaintiffs, Dr. Allen Glushakow and his wife Shelley Glushakow, are alleging Confederation Life and a number of related parties, including the company's auditors, misrepresented the state of its financial health to them and other U.S. purchasers of its whole- life insurance policies. The suit also questions the company's sales practices.
Ottawa Plans Smaller Fishery
Ottawa will dip into a $300-million fund to buy the licences of thousands of East Coast fishermen left without work because of the collapse of some stocks. Ottawa is aiming for a 50 per cent cut in the industry's capacity for catching fish. Cuts will be engineered by four newly created boards, including one in Quebec, federal Fisheries Minister Brian Tobin said yesterday. Richard Cashin, head of Newfoundland's board, said fishermen could be paid a minimum of $100,000 to forever surrender papers that provide access to fish.
"It would be hard to imagine any under $100,000," said Cashin. Over the next few months, the boards will determine exactly how the scheme will work, but Ottawa will finalize the buyouts. Tobin promised to set up the boards last April within weeks of announcing a $1.9-billion aid program for about 30,000 fishermen and plant workers affected by declines in bottom-dwelling groundfish like northern cod. The $300 million was set aside then.

Company / organization: Name: Confederation Life Insurance Co; NAICS: 524113; DUNS: 20-640-2778

Healthy NDP remains vital for building equitable society: [SA2 Edition]

Author: David Crane Toronto Star

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/healthy-ndp-remains-vital-building-equitable/docview/437072733/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Where the NDP has gone off course, [Lars Osberg] argues in his paper that will be distributed at the NDP conference, is that it has strayed from the need to create wealth as well as to distrib-ute it: "Much of the original Progressive/CCF agenda was about eliminating the irrationalities of capitalism and improving economic efficiency - about generating wealth as well as sharing it." This original agenda also emphasized building communities, not simply pursuing individualism, and developing the idea of "a common future of shared rights and responsibilities." In recent years, Osberg argues, "the NDP has not tended to emphasize efficiency or responsibility. It has talked of how it will share wealth more equitably, rather than how its policies will create wealth - in the public mind, it has become identified more with policies that are fair than with policies that are practical." If, as it is widely believed, the market economy has won the day, the need for a credible and forward-looking NDP is even more important to protect Canadians from all the insecurity and inequality that flows from unrestrained capitalism. Left to its own devices, the free market won't solve the tragic problem of child poverty, save the world's environment or provide full employment. The job of the NDP is to bring humanity and fairness to the marketplace - in the process improving both equity and efficiency.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Healthy+NDP+remains+vital+for+building+equitable+society&amp;author=David+Crane+Toronto+Star&amp;issn=03190781&amp;title=Toronto+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-08-27&amp;spage=C.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: THIS weekend, members of the New Democratic Party are gathering in Ottawa as part of the process of rethinking their party's future.
What they decide is important for all Canadians because we need a political party that pushes from the left if we are to continue to build an equitable and humane country.
Indeed, Canadians will suffer from the lack of an effective NDP presence in the current Parliament as MPs debate the future direction of the economy, the reform of our social safety net, how to deal with the pension system, what kind of information highway we should have, the type of training and retraining systems we need, Canadian cultural policies, the reshaping of government, the jobs of the future and a host of other issues.
In the absence of a strong NDP, the Liberals are likely to be pulled increasingly to the right by Reform, erasing many of the differences between themselves and the Mulroney government they replaced - and giving ordinary Canadians another lesson in political disillusionment in the process.
To be sure, the plight of the NDP is largely its own fault. It has spent the past decade or longer spending too much time trying to be all things to all people and too little time actually thinking about its principles and priorities in a much different new, global economy.
Yet what the NDP does this weekend is not just an internal matter.
"Policy renewal for the NDP is crucial for Canada because the New Democratic Party is the custodian of a political heritage, dating back to the CCF and the Progressive movement, which has been crucially important in making Canada a better place in which to live," argues Lars Osberg, an economist from Dalhousie University.
While many people proclaim the victory of capitalism and the market economy, Osberg responds that "an unrestrained capitalist market system generates too much exploitation and unfairness, too much poverty and inequality and too much economic insecurity for the majority of families."
A key role of the NDP is to remind us, as Osberg contends, that "this is not inevitable" in a world in which capitalism, left to itself, "generates great wealth at the cost of great irrationalities and vast inequities."
A comparison of social conditions in Canada and the United States underlines the importance of policies to reduce inequality and enhance the social community.
Similarly, health studies in Sweden showing that the over-all health of Swedish children at the bottom 10 per cent of family income levels is the same as children at the top 10 per cent is a remarkable contrast to the free market system of the United States.
Likewise, the market system is not good at dealing with environmental problems, especially truly long-term issues such as global warming and climate change. The role of government intervention, especially through economic instruments such as tradable emission permits or carbon taxes, is to alter relative prices so that needed changes take place.
Where the NDP has gone off course, Osberg argues in his paper that will be distributed at the NDP conference, is that it has strayed from the need to create wealth as well as to distrib-ute it: "Much of the original Progressive/CCF agenda was about eliminating the irrationalities of capitalism and improving economic efficiency - about generating wealth as well as sharing it." This original agenda also emphasized building communities, not simply pursuing individualism, and developing the idea of "a common future of shared rights and responsibilities."
In fact, one of the greatest mistakes the NDP has made is not to underline the key fact that efficiency and equity go together.
Quality education and child care available to all children has a huge economic payoff. Medicare provides better health for Canadians and at lower cost than the U.S. private health insurance system, with economic benefits for Canada in addition to the lower costs. Effective retraining programs reduce social welfare costs and create a more productive work force.
Ontario Premier Bob Rae has shown some recognition of the linkages between the social environment and economic growth by merging the Premier's councils on the economy and on social policy into one council.
But in recent years, Osberg argues, "the NDP has not tended to emphasize efficiency or responsibility. It has talked of how it will share wealth more equitably, rather than how its policies will create wealth - in the public mind, it has become identified more with policies that are fair than with policies that are practical."
Perhaps even more than other political parties, the NDP should have a huge stake in wealth creation, to provide the means to support its social ambitions; in individual responsibility, to create the sense of community that should be the basis of Canadian society; and in government efficiency, to give credibility to the idea that government can do things well.
If, as it is widely believed, the market economy has won the day, the need for a credible and forward-looking NDP is even more important to protect Canadians from all the insecurity and inequality that flows from unrestrained capitalism. Left to its own devices, the free market won't solve the tragic problem of child poverty, save the world's environment or provide full employment. The job of the NDP is to bring humanity and fairness to the marketplace - in the process improving both equity and efficiency.
David Crane is The Star's economics editor.

People: Osberg, Lars

Extreme Waves: Surf's up Jon Reeds asks why oceanographers are all at sea about those rare freak extreme waves

Author: Reeds, Jon

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/extreme-waves-surfs-up-jon-reeds-asks-why/docview/293497151/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Extreme waves are a product of storms like those which raged for three months in the North Atlantic in the autumn of 1934. Sailors who survived them brought home tales of 100ft waves, perhaps like the one which hit the collier Ainderby 500 miles off Newfoundland. After six days of struggling west through gathering storms, she had hove to, only to be run down by a mast high wave which stoved in a bulkhead and a hatch, and washed away most of her bridge. One hundred and fifty miles to the east, the grain ship Millpool could not answer the Ainderby's distress calls. Already damaged by huge waves and running before the storm, she too was smashed by a huge wave and gradually her own distress calls faded. Although the Ainderby survived the terrible night and limped back to Swansea, the Millpool and her 26 crew vanished without trace. So too did three other ships that autumn, and two more lost their superstructures to huge waves. Both problems bedevil extreme wave study. The very long-term data needed is mostly lacking and the statistical framework for prediction is shaky. Swell is less important in extreme wave creation, which depends more on extreme wind speed. It all pointed the need for urgent research on waves and storms.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Extreme+Waves%3A+Surf%27s+up+Jon+Reeds+asks+why+oceanographers+are+all+at+sea+about+those+rare+freak+extreme+waves&amp;author=Reeds%2C+Jon&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-08-04&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: FREAK WAVES are the real monsters of the deep. Towering up to 30m above a storm-tossed sea, they have all too often meant the end for seafarers down the centuries. Because they are by definition rare and unpredictable, oceanographers (who prefer the term `extreme waves') are cautious in theorising about them. But a slowly flowing tide of evidence from the North Atlantic suggests they are becoming more of a threat.
If this is true, it is bad news for sailors. But through the ocean's sharpest teeth oceanographers may hear answers to some crucial questions - how waves, storms and storm surges are related, how they have changed and, most excitingly, whether there are clear signs that global warming is having demonstrable local effects.
The European Union is backing its interest with funds for major new research which aims to answer these key questions.
Deep sea sailors rightly fear extreme waves. Yachtsmen have faced this doorway to Davy Jones's locker down the centuries, and even today large vessels vanish without trace, and piracy, sabotage and structural failure cannot explain them all.
Extreme waves are a product of storms like those which raged for three months in the North Atlantic in the autumn of 1934. Sailors who survived them brought home tales of 100ft waves, perhaps like the one which hit the collier Ainderby 500 miles off Newfoundland. After six days of struggling west through gathering storms, she had hove to, only to be run down by a mast high wave which stoved in a bulkhead and a hatch, and washed away most of her bridge.
One hundred and fifty miles to the east, the grain ship Millpool could not answer the Ainderby's distress calls. Already damaged by huge waves and running before the storm, she too was smashed by a huge wave and gradually her own distress calls faded. Although the Ainderby survived the terrible night and limped back to Swansea, the Millpool and her 26 crew vanished without trace. So too did three other ships that autumn, and two more lost their superstructures to huge waves.
That year was exceptional, but there are signs that the north-east Atlantic is getting rougher. Britain's Institute of Oceanographic Science has gathered clear evidence that its average wave heights have increased over the last 30 years.
Scientists are divided over the reason. Some blame the greenhouse effect; others say it is a cyclical change. A third group cites chaos theory, and says that wave climates are the products of a rich interconnection of forces, which are too complex to permit simple causal explanations.
Waves are created by the wind. Yet although North Atlantic waves are showing a clear increase in average height, long term wind statistics show no corresponding increase in speed. Marine consultant Neil Hogben believes the answer lies in the swell, the term oceanographers use to describe ocean waves which can travel hundreds of miles beyond the wind field which created them.
`Wind speeds have not increased, but it seems storm frequencies have,' says Hogben. `Therefore the time interval allowing swells to decay has been reduced.' Storms are a key challenge for climate change theory. At mid-latitudes they are driven by the difference in temperature between equatorial and polar regions. The early work of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) suggested that, as the poles would warm faster than the equator, this gradient would be reduced and storms with it. The theory is proving controversial.
`Storminess is such a variable parameter one is hard put to say whether it has changed,' says wave statistician David Carter.
Whatever the long-term statistics, the last five years have seen a relentless procession of storms advance into northern waters. Norway, at the cutting edge of Atlantic gales and economically dependent on off-shore oil, has logged some dreadful conditions in the Barents and Norwegian seas. Between 1989 and 1993, these inhospitable waters came under unprecedented attack. Record low atmospheric pressures in early 1989 saw significant wave heights (the scientific measure of the average) up to 13m.
A storm the following December saw significant wave heights of 16m recorded at the Frigg oil field and a monster 26.4m extreme wave logged. October 1991 brought 13m significant waves to Haltenbanken in central Norway, a shocked light- house keeper describing them as the worst conditions he had seen in 40 years. Then January 1992 brought hurricane force winds recalling England's 1987 Great Storm, whipping up 14m seas.
There was worse to come. The three months following Christmas 1992 washed away all the records.
In mid-Norway waves averaged 5.2m for the whole three months, never falling below 2m. For five January days, the angry seas averaged over 8m, peaking at 13.6m in central Norway and bringing records of 14m and 16m off thenorth coast. Meanwhile a low pressure area deepened to 916 hPa off north west Scotland, a record for the northern hemisphere.
But however clear the upward trend in average heights, extreme waves are more elusive. Yet with the oil industry getting jumpy and a nervous insurance industry facing extreme weather of its own making, questions are being asked.
Last year Norway's environment ministry brought oceanographers from both sides of the Atlantic to a conference in Reykjavik to ask if extreme waves are getting bigger. Ominously, the scientists from both sides of the ocean compared notes and agreed that they are. In many places `one in one hundred years' estimated wave levels have been exceeded by several metres, and not just once. They concluded this is a genuine trend and not just the difficulties of prediction or the lack of long-term statistics.
Both problems bedevil extreme wave study. The very long-term data needed is mostly lacking and the statistical framework for prediction is shaky. Swell is less important in extreme wave creation, which depends more on extreme wind speed. It all pointed the need for urgent research on waves and storms.
A new study backed by the EU's Environment Programme is just getting underway. Research institutes in eight countries will examine the storm climate in the north-east Atlantic over the past 100 years to see if storms are becoming more frequent or intense. It will seek to establish causal links between storms, waves and storm surges and link the data in with existing models of climate change to begin to unlock some of the key questions about the future.
The two-year project will be coordinated by the Max Planck Institute for Meteorology in Hamburg. Norway, outside the EU, will be paying its own way into the project. The UK will be represented by the Proudman Oceanographic Laboratory at Bidston, which will contribute its expertise on storm surges. Along with waves and storms, these complex interactions of sea and atmosphere, such as the one which produced the devastating east coast floods in 1993, are a vital element of the study.
Collecting long-term data presents a number of problems. The climate undergoes complex changes naturally and man-made effects may be hard to distinguish. Meteorological data sets going back decades or even centuries are available but changes in observational techniques may make them useless for long term comparison.
Some of the best data are of sea levels themselves and of air pressure. Wind speed data is rarely available and, where wave climate figures exist, they tend to be short-term, so statistical reconstruction is needed.
`The project will try to see whether there have been changes in trends, particularly in wave conditions over the last hundred years,' says Graham Alcock, a principal scientific officer at the Proudman Laboratory.
The most tantalising opportunity offered by the project will be hooking this long-term data into the Max Planck Institute's climate model already used to produce some of the IPCC predictions on climate change. As well as improving the model, it may allow the possibility of sub-global predictions. The results should help inform the next round IPCC reports due late next year.
A separate project, Neptune, backed by a different EU programme is also looking at the interaction of waves and storminess. By concentrating on more local results, it should help inform designers of coastal structures, where wave climates are very different to the deep seas.
If storm and wave parameters really are moving out of the natural variations it could provide powerful evidence of the effect mankind is having on the environment.
If storm frequency and intensity are both increasing, it is bad news for all who sail or work on the sea. Terrifying as extreme waves are, it is the demonstrable reality of higher average waves which provides the real challenge for designers of off-shore structures or ships. Just a 10 per cent increase in average stress can halve the fatigue life of such structures.
But it will be the bigger questions that the world at large will watch. Recent work in America suggests the IPCC's optimism about mid latitiude storms may be premature. Cloud reflectivity may fall with temperature, allowing the tropics to warm as fast as the poles.
It is too early yet to make storm warnings a permanent feature of ocean climatology. But a rougher future now looks inevitable.

Title: Extreme Waves: Surf's up Jon Reeds asks why oceanographers are all at sea about those rare freak extreme waves
