Study Bolsters Value Of Species Diversity

Author: Stevens, William K

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/study-bolsters-value-species-diversity/docview/429431551/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The more species an ecosystem has, Dr. [David Tilman] explains, the more likely it is that some will be resistant to drought, disease, insect plague or any other stress that can threaten it. These resistant species spread and take the place of those harmed or killed by the stress. "This is a process that could apply to any ecosystem," he said, which makes it important to preserve biological diversity "because it's nature's insurance policy against catastrophes." The work of Dr. Tilman and Dr. [John A. Downing], reported in the current issue of the British journal Nature, "shows that a variety of species tends to buffer an ecosystem against extreme events," said Professor [John Lawton], "and that's a very important finding." On top of that, he said, his own work, carried out in a controlled laboratory setting, is also revealing that species diversity is important: even on an everyday basis, with no catastrophe looming, species-rich ecosystems tend to function more robustly than species-poor ones. The Tilman-Downing study is "quite sound," said Dr. Edward O. Wilson of Harvard University, an authority on biodiversity and an advocate for its conservation. Not least, he said, it helps point the way to understanding a "gray area" that has been "relatively intractable to experimentation or definitive field study and therefore not easily penetrated." The strategy followed by the two researchers was to combine the experimental approach of the laboratory with the real-world authenticity of a natural ecosystem.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Study+Bolsters+Value+Of+Species+Diversity&amp;author=Stevens%2C+William+K&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-02-01&amp;spage=C.4&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: CONSERVATIONISTS widely believe that the more species an ecosystem contains, the more robust and stable it will be. If that is true, they say, the steady reduction of biological diversity now taking place around the world could in time undermine the ecological foundation of human existence.
A counterargument holds that richness of species is unimportant to ecosystem stability as long as one or two species exist to carry out each of a number of vital functions like photosynthesis, pollination, plant-eating, predation and decomposition of waste. If that is true, the case for preserving biological diversity, the touchstone of conservation in the 1990's, loses one of its major props.
For a long time there was little or no scientific evidence on the question. But now scientists have produced some, and it supports the view that biological diversity is indeed important to the functioning and stability of ecosystems.
Dr. David Tilman of the University of Minnesota in St. Paul and Dr. John A. Downing of the University of Montreal studied the behavior of prairie ecosystems for seven years. They found that the grassland plots with the greatest diversity of plant species lost far less of their vegetative cover as a result of the worst drought in 50 years, and recovered much more quickly, than the plots with the fewest species. The loss of each additional species resulted in a sharper reduction of the ecosystem's resistance to drought, the study suggests.
The more species an ecosystem has, Dr. Tilman explains, the more likely it is that some will be resistant to drought, disease, insect plague or any other stress that can threaten it. These resistant species spread and take the place of those harmed or killed by the stress. "This is a process that could apply to any ecosystem," he said, which makes it important to preserve biological diversity "because it's nature's insurance policy against catastrophes."
The evidence runs directly counter to the rival hypothesis, advanced by Prof. John Lawton, a British ecologist, not out of personal conviction but as a devil's-advocate scientific test. As a result of the Tilman-Downing findings and his own experimental work, Professor Lawton appears to be gravitating to the view of those who say species diversity matters. 'A Very Important Finding'
The work of Dr. Tilman and Dr. Downing, reported in the current issue of the British journal Nature, "shows that a variety of species tends to buffer an ecosystem against extreme events," said Professor Lawton, "and that's a very important finding." On top of that, he said, his own work, carried out in a controlled laboratory setting, is also revealing that species diversity is important: even on an everyday basis, with no catastrophe looming, species-rich ecosystems tend to function more robustly than species-poor ones.
The emerging evidence is bringing a measure of clarity to a subject largely characterized until now by hazy speculation. Many scientists are convinced that if whole classes of vital organisms -- invertebrates, for example -- were somehow eliminated, the global ecosystem would collapse and most other species would disappear, humans included.
Many researchers have believed that species losses short of this extreme case could undercut ecosystem functioning, and that if enough ecosystems are affected the functioning of the biosphere at large -- humanity's life-support system -- could be seriously impaired. It is not necessary for species to go extinct globally for an ecosystem to crash, they fear, but only for enough species to disappear within that ecosystem. And while these scientists have been convinced that an ecosystem will collapse if certain keystone species disappear, they have had no clear idea of when an ecosystem begins to disintegrate.
What they do know is that humans are rapidly eliminating habitats and introducing exotic species that choke out native ones, both of which reduce the diversity of ecosystems.
The Tilman-Downing findings emerged from a long-term study of the factors affecting species composition, dynamics and diversity in Minnesota grasslands that began in 1982. The drought of 1987-88, the fourth worst since records have been kept in Minnesota, provided a fortuitous test of resistance to and recovery from stress among native plant species growing on the study's 207 plots.
For each plot, the researchers calculated the rate of change in biomass, or dry weight of plant material above the ground. They found that during the drought, the plots with the most species produced about half their predrought biomass, while those with the fewest species produced only about one-eighth. By 1992 the species-rich plots had recovered their predrought biomass, while those poorest in species still produced significantly less than before. Help for Ecological Restoration
The researchers found that species-rich plots were more likely to contain some drought-resistant species. These plants stepped up their growth to compensate partly for the decreased growth of other species.
"We're greatly surprised by the clarity of the response we saw," said Dr. Tilman. "We expected there to be a relationship between biodiversity and stability, but we did not expect the relationship to be as strong or as clear as it turned out to be."
The findings could have special meaning for the emerging art and practice of ecological restoration. Reviving only a handful of species may not be sufficient to restore an ecosystem's essential functions. "It doesn't say how many you need, but it does say you better put some variety in there if you're going to cope with the normal run of extremes or perturbations," said Dr. Peter Vitousek, an ecologist at Stanford University.
Is there a threshold above which an increasing number of species no longer makes much difference to ecosystem stability?
Dr. Tilman and Dr. Downing found that once there were 9 or 10 plant species, there was no longer a significant effect on ecosystem functioning. But since the researchers did not have many more than 10 species in their samplings, they do not know whether this constitutes a threshold. That question, which may have different answers for different ecosystems, is still to be investigated.
While there is at present "no better data set than ours" addressing the the relationship of biodiversity to ecosystem stability, Dr. Tilman said, he and Dr. Downing are undertaking more rigorous studies aimed at producing a "very definitive answer" to the question. Support for Conservation
And although "we do not yet know how the stability of other ecosystems depends on biodiversity, these results lend further urgency to pleas for the conservation of biodiversity," the two ecologists wrote in Nature.
The Tilman-Downing study is "quite sound," said Dr. Edward O. Wilson of Harvard University, an authority on biodiversity and an advocate for its conservation. Not least, he said, it helps point the way to understanding a "gray area" that has been "relatively intractable to experimentation or definitive field study and therefore not easily penetrated." The strategy followed by the two researchers was to combine the experimental approach of the laboratory with the real-world authenticity of a natural ecosystem.
Professor Lawton said the study "carries an important message: that species matter in the ability of ecosystems to recover from extreme events."
These events, he said, might be naturally occurring or caused by humans, like the climate change that some scientists believe is occurring as a result of emissions of industrial gases that trap heat in the atmosphere. As human activity reduces the number and distribution of species, he said, "we begin to put large areas of the planet into a double whammy: global climate change and fewer species as well."
Professor Lawton runs experiments in ecosystem behavior at a controlled environmental facility at the British Natural Environment Research Council's Center for Population Biology at Imperial College in Silwood Park, England. "We've created a series of little communities with varying complexity, and can run them through a series of plant variations and speed time up."
Through this approach, he said, he and his colleagues have found that species matter in ecosystem functioning. For instance, he said, "we showed that the most species-rich ecosystems were the ones most able to absorb carbon dioxide" and that "a whole set of ecosystem processes changed as species changed." He said the findings are now being reviewed for publication.
Because many people were writing about the relationship of biodiversity to the functioning of ecosystems "without any evidence at all," Professor Lawton said, he proposed the alternative hypothesis that one or two species representing each important ecosystem function are sufficient. Speaking as a scientist, he said he has been "completely neutral about which way the answer comes out; that's how science proceeds, by testing alternative hypotheses."
But speaking personally, he said he was "delighted by these results" because they "strengthen the argument for conserving species and intact ecosystems."
Photograph Species-rich prairie plot, left, at Cedar Creek Natural History Area has flowering blazing stars and more than 20 other species. Species-poor plot has under five. (Dr. David Tilman/University of Minnesota)
Graph "Species Richness and Survival" shows percentage of initial vegetation in plots, and the number of plant species in a plot. (Source: Dr. David Tilman/University of Minnesota)

Climate Change for Health Reform Polls show crime is top concern: [NASSAU AND SUFFOLK Edition]

Author: By Marilyn Milloy. WASHINGTON BUREAU

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-change-health-reform-polls-show-crime-is/docview/278738457/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: For starters, polls now show that the public's most gnawing worries have shifted away from health care to crime. Conservative Republicans have begun to promote feverishly the idea that there really is no health crisis anyway. And government statistics show national health spending declining - a blip on the trend scale, but a boon to those who argue the system can largely fix itself. Nevertheless, some members see ways to tinker privately. For instance, employer mandates could be softened to require much less than the 80-percent contribution from smaller businesses. Alliances could be eliminated, or scaled back. Premium caps could be eliminated for a year or so, and the phase-in for universal coverage could be extended by several years. Even if such hoops are jumped through - and that's a big "if," says Marilyn Moon of the Washington-based Urban Institute, since "for every problem you solve you create new ones" - it still may not be good enough for some. The reason? A growing bipartisan group of lawmakers simply believes Congress should shoot for a less ambitious bill - say, one that prohibits health-insurance companies from discriminating against the really sick, the elderly or people with preexisting medical conditions. "I would think the people of America would be grateful if we can pass reforms that solve three or four problems - not all of them," said Sen. Bob Kerrey (D-Neb.), who, though a fervid supporter of universal coverage, thinks a broad-based tax is the more "honest" way to fund it.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Climate+Change+for+Health+Reform+Polls+show+crime+is+top+concern&amp;author=By+Marilyn+Milloy.+WASHINGTON+BUREAU&amp;issn=&amp;title=Newsday&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1994-01-23&amp;spage=19&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Washington - It has been four months since President Bill Clinton delivered to the nation an impassioned speech on his vision for a better national health-care system; months, too, since Hillary Rodham Clinton swept through Capitol Hill with her own sparkling pitch for what would become the administration's 1,342-page plan for change.
Theirs was, by every measure, autumn's Big Splash. And yet, owing to a crowded White House agenda and lately, the irksome distraction of the Whitewater case, only now is the actual, dogged swim about to get under way.
On Tuesday night, when the president delivers his first State of the Union address to a joint session of Congress, he is expected to refire the starting gun on health-care policy. But this time he will do it in a political environment that's decidedly different - far more complicated, perhaps less genteel - than it was even a few months ago.
If the going was predicted to be choppy then, observers say it is sure to be outright treacherous now.
"It's just that a lot of events have interceded since September, and so what you've been hearing is a kind of slow hissing sound as the gas has been escaping from the health-care balloon," said Henry Aaron of the Brookings Institution, a Washington think tank. "Things have changed."
For starters, polls now show that the public's most gnawing worries have shifted away from health care to crime. Conservative Republicans have begun to promote feverishly the idea that there really is no health crisis anyway. And government statistics show national health spending declining - a blip on the trend scale, but a boon to those who argue the system can largely fix itself.
Meanwhile, whatever luster the Clinton plan once had has been wiped clean by a determined army of interest-group opponents with big lobbying and advertising war chests. In addition, many members of Congress are declaring so much wrong with the White House proposal these days that major surgery appears inevitable.
And just this weekend, Senate Minority Leader Bob Dole of Kansas urged Republican leaders meeting in Washington to unite on their approach against Clinton and his health-care reform.
Even Sen. Daniel Patrick Moynihan (D-N.Y.), who introduced the Clinton bill in the Senate, appears hard-pressed to embrace any of its features, choosing instead to peck away critically at its financing, its Medicaid formulas, its complexity, even its raison d'etre - that the current system is a mess.
Whether any of this signals disaster for the Clintons' "bottom-line" goal of passing legislation this year, comprehensive health benefits for all Americans is impossible for anybody to guarantee so soon - and most Democrats dare not. "It's within our reach," said Sen. Harris Wofford (D-Pa.) whose own election two years ago set off a national recognition of how angry Americans were about health care.
Indeed, key House committee leaders ambitiously have set an early spring deadline to complete hearings and a markup on the Clinton bill, which members will judge in comparison to several other strongly competing bills - including one by moderate Republicans, another by a bipartisan group that includes conservative Democrats and one by a sizable contingent of liberal Democrats which supports a Canadian-style system. The Senate's timetable appears to be slower-paced and may well jeopardize hopes for an early summer floor debate.
But even Wofford concedes that some masterful negotiations will have to take place for Clinton to salvage the integrity, if not the specifics, of his plan.
Just how that can happen, many analysts agree, is precisely the crux of the matter. The linchpins of the Clinton plan happen to be its most controversial parts: employer mandates, Clinton's primary scheme for making sure everybody is covered; health purchasing alliances, which would bargain with networks of doctors and hospitals for low prices, and government limits on the cost of insurance premiums, a backup if market competition doesn't work.
Nevertheless, some members see ways to tinker privately. For instance, employer mandates could be softened to require much less than the 80-percent contribution from smaller businesses. Alliances could be eliminated, or scaled back. Premium caps could be eliminated for a year or so, and the phase-in for universal coverage could be extended by several years.
But even if such hoops are jumped through - and that's a big "if," says Marilyn Moon of the Washington-based Urban Institute, since "for every problem you solve you create new ones" - it still may not be good enough for some. The reason? A growing bipartisan group of lawmakers simply believes Congress should shoot for a less ambitious bill - say, one that prohibits health-insurance companies from discriminating against the really sick, the elderly or people with preexisting medical conditions. "I would think the people of America would be grateful if we can pass reforms that solve three or four problems - not all of them," said Sen. Bob Kerrey (D-Neb.), who, though a fervid supporter of universal coverage, thinks a broad-based tax is the more "honest" way to fund it.
For its part, the White House wants nothing to do with talk of bargains and deals. "This compromise stuff just gets under my skin," said White House spokesman Jeff Eller. "We've said all along there'd be changes in the bill." It is universal coverage with broad benefits, he said, that cannot be sacrificed. And he argues that the White House should just be allowed to make its case, its way, in time.
Only in the last week or so has the administration been able to begin doing that in earnest, however. Its new health-care campaign manager, New York lawyer Harold Ickes, has just begun plotting a full-blown strategy for wooing Congress and the public. Amid a major reorganization of staff who will be enlisted for the effort, public forums are being planned, as are numerous appearances by the administration officials and the first lady and lots of behind-the-scenes meetings with key players.
Meanwhile, an outside group backing the Clinton plan - of which organized labor is a part - this week launches a mutimillion-dollar media campaign to respond in part to the one being run by the insurance and pharmaceutical industries.
Still, analysts say, it will be the president who potentially will have the greatest sway with the public and with Congress.
"It's a long-odds proposition," said Aaron of Brookings. "But if the stars alight themselves appropriately - which means he has the beneficiary of good luck and not be a perpetrator of missteps like in the first year - then this thing could fly."

The Case Of the Met's Empty Galleries

Author: Riding, Alan

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/case-mets-empty-galleries/docview/429408190/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: In his statement, Mr. de Montebello cited the Spanish elections: "A lengthy period of governmental transition occurred during which the crucial approval process for loans came to a virtual halt. The time lost simply could not be recouped." He thanked officials at the Culture Ministry for their "heroic efforts" but said it was "inappropriate" to rush the shipment to New York. "Our primary concern and responsibility is the safety of works of art." For Ms. Tarapor, however, the paperwork demanded by the Culture Ministry came as a complete surprise. "Since 1987, we have never been asked to provide anything beyond a list with the insurance evaluation of the lenders," she said. "If the rules changed somewhere along the way, the ministry had a responsibility to inform us." Mr. [Jesus Macarron] said he too was puzzled by the requirement. "I've never before had to give loan forms to the junta," he said. And in Mr. de Montebello's view, this time the Met faced "an extremely conservative junta, many of whose members were not well disposed to loans." Despite all the misunderstandings surrounding this affair, both the Met and Spain's Culture Ministry seem eager to resume good relations. Noting that the two sides have a long record of close collaboration, Mr. [Jose Guirao] said Spain is "always interested in participating in any exhibition at an institution like the Met." And in a letter to Mrs. [Carmen Alborch Bataller] thanking her for her help, Mr. de Montebello said that 1995, when the Met celebrates its 125th anniversary, would be a "propitious occasion" to hold the medieval art exhibition "on a scale that the subject and the works deserve."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Case+Of+the+Met%27s+Empty+Galleries&amp;author=Riding%2C+Alan&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-12-19&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: IT WAS AT A PRIVATE LUNCHeon in New York on Oct. 8, 1991, that Philippe de Montebello, director of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, first asked a senior official from Spain's Culture Ministry to support a major exhibition on the art of medieval Spain. Delighted by the Spaniard's enthusiastic response, Mr. de Montebello gave his team the go-ahead for what promised to be one of the museum's showpiece exhibitions of the 1990's.
The Met and Spain already had an impeccable record of working together, starting in 1987, on major exhibitions of such figures as Zurbaran, Goya, Velazquez and Ribera, as well as of the Islamic art of Spain. In the two years that followed the meeting with Jose Maria Luzon Nogue, then Spain's Director General of Fine Arts, Met officials combed Spain for the 80 or so works -- from Visigothic marble tombs to Romanesque wooden carvings -- they would need from that country. In all, the show was to include some 160 works.
"It was to be the most ambitious Spanish exhibition to date," said Mahrukh Tarapor, the Met's associate director for exhibitions, who played a crucial role in organizing earlier Spanish shows. "It was to be the Met's tribute to Spain, examining the most critical and formative period of Spanish history."
Then, barely two weeks before the official opening on Nov. 15, "The Art of Medieval Spain, A.D. 500-1200" was abruptly called off. Many in the art world were stunned. Exhibitions are sometimes delayed, even canceled, but the Met had never before postponed a major show this late in the day -- with catalogues already in print and invitations in the mail.
The museum felt it had no choice. At that moment, the pieces that had been chosen and approved for loan to the Met were still scattered around Spain. Since it was unclear when they would arrive in New York, Mr. de Montebello said in a press release on Oct. 29 that the museum's own calender of events "precluded our accommodating even a brief delay in 'Medieval Spain.' " But he said he hoped the show could still be held "sometime in 1995."
So what happened? Why did time run out? The story tells much about the complexity of mounting ambitious international loan exhibitions and how and why minor details can derail them -- although rarely so dramatically as in this case. Yet while there is some finger-pointing
between Spain and the Met, there seems to be no single explanation for why these longtime associates failed to come together on so important an occasion.
Clearly, this was a complicated exhibition to organize, with about half the pieces coming from Spain and the rest from elsewhere in Europe and the United States. In Spain, it involved borrowing from independent institutions all over the country. Spanish elections in late June brought Cabinet changes that delayed the approval of loans. And poor communications between Spain and the Met seem to have plagued the negotiations.
The final bottleneck was bureaucratic. Before approving the temporary export of the works, the Culture Ministry's rules required a mountain of documents. The Met says it was unaware of this requirement until late September. And by the time the documents were rounded up in a last-minute scramble in which ministry officials lent a hand, the art should already have been in New York and ready for installation.
Yet if any culprit can be identified, it is perhaps the cultural gap that still separates Americans and Spaniards. For the Met, the show could only heighten Spain's image in the United States -- and it took Spanish cooperation for granted. But in Spain, politics, religious sentiments and regional pride all emerged as complicating factors when it came to deciding what should be lent. An Ominous Sign Provincials Say Madrid Has Doubts
The problems surrounding this show had not arisen in the Met's previous dealings with Spain because the shows of Spanish masters like Goya had involved borrowing paintings from the Prado and a few other Government-run museums. In the case of last year's Islamic art show, the Spanish Government was a participant and a Spanish bank the main sponsor. But with "The Art of Medieval Spain," the Met was the sole organizer. Even more important, the priceless objects it wished to display were the property of dozens of churches, cathedrals, Catholic schools and regional museums, each with a right of veto.
Aware of this, Ms. Tarapor, along with Charles T. Little, curator of the exhibition, and other experts made numerous trips to Spain, going from institution to institution, inspecting objects and persuading their owners that nothing untoward would happen to them. Most owners agreed to lend their pieces, which ranged from wooden and stone statues, parts of altars and ancient Bibles to paintings, textiles, and marble and ivory sculptures. But Ms. Tarapor said some owners told her that the central Government in Spain was not enthusiastic about the exhibition. In Spain, that was an ominous sign.
The view from Madrid, on the other hand, was that public opposition to lending certain pieces of great religious significance was growing in some regions. In a June 28 letter to Mr. de Montebello, Mr. Luzon reminded him that each loan had to be approved by the owner, technical experts and the Culture Ministry's 22-member Junta of Qualification, Evaluation and Exportation. He also warned of "growing sensitivity expressed in different media in the sense that some works of art should not be moved from the places where they are habitually kept."
By then, the loan of 12 medieval objects had already been denied -- 6 by the cultural authorities of Catalonia, who argued that the "exceptional" paintings on wood could be damaged by "possible vibrations and climate change," 3 by the Museum of the Cathedral of Gerona and 3 by the Archeological Museum of Oviedo. Gerona and Oviedo gave no reason for their refusals.
The Met was even more disappointed when the Culture Ministry refused to approve the loan of two other pieces the museum had avidly sought. In the case of the 12th-century marble Sarcophagus of Dona Sancha, the ministry appeared to respond to local opposition, although its owner, the Benedictine Monastery of Santa Cruz at Jaca in the Pyrenees, had approved the loan. In the case of the Reliquary of San Isidoro from Leon, an 11th-century silver gilt box, the loan of which had been approved by the Bishop of Leon, the ministry's evaluation committee judged it too fragile to travel. The Final Blow One Requirement Too Many
Nonetheless, plans for the exhibition were moving ahead. On July 30, Herbert Moskowitz, the registrar of the Met, informed Jesus Macarron that his Madrid shipping company had been chosen to pack and transport the pieces to New York and promised to send "an updated list of loans" within two weeks. It finally arrived on Sept. 3. On Sept. 23, Mr. Macarron presented the evaluation committee with a final list of 82 pieces for temporary export, confident that approval would be a formality.
The Met, too, was beginning to relax. In mid-September, Ms. Tarapor met the new Culture Minister, Carmen Alborch Bataller, who agreed to write a brief introduction to the 358-page catalogue, which by then was ready to go to the printers.
"From the start, we planned the catalogue as a definitive book on Spanish medieval art," she said, "so we included many works that we knew we might not get. But we also avoided listing lenders to the exhibition because we didn't know what we'd have. In fact, Philippe de Montebello only wrote his foreword after we received Mrs. Alborch's text at the end of September. At that point, we were confident that our deadline would be met." (The catalogue, published by Abrams, is now for sale at the museum.)
But it was around this time that things began to go badly wrong. When the evaluation committee met on Sept. 24, it approved the loan of 38 pieces belonging to the central Government but said it could not act on the remaining objects without letters from their 19 different owners confirming the loans, plus technical reports from experts certifying that the pieces could travel. Both Mr. Macarron and Culture Ministry officials then began contacting owners, but Ms. Tarapor said she only learned of the delay on Oct. 14. By then time was running out.
The committee met again on Oct. 22 and approved all but seven pieces sought by the Met. Three days earlier, Mr. Luzon had been replaced as Director General of Fine Arts by Jose Guirao, an Andalusian art expert. With Mr. de Montebello in contact with the Culture Minister, Ms. Tarapor urged Mr. Guirao to reconsider some of the denials. The ministry stood firm in refusing the Sarcophagus of Dona Sancha and the Reliquary of San Isidoro, but five more pieces were approved, including the Urraca Chalice from Leon that decorates the back of the catalogue. On Oct. 28, Mr. de Montebello and Mr. Guirao agreed on a final list of 80 objects.
At that point, the pieces were still languishing all over Spain. Mr. Macarron had hoped to start packing on Oct. 25 but wasn't given the green light by the Met until Oct. 28. The next day he was told that every crate had to be brought to Madrid, unpacked, inspected by a curator from the National Archeological Museum and repacked before being shipped. On top of that, he said, the assigned curator was taking an extended weekend break and would not be available until Nov. 3.
"With that additional requirement, which no one had known anything about, he told us he could not make the deadline," Ms. Tarapor said. That same day, Oct. 29, Mr. de Montebello called off the exhibition. The Explanations Nobody Nudging Things Along
In his statement, Mr. de Montebello cited the Spanish elections: "A lengthy period of governmental transition occurred during which the crucial approval process for loans came to a virtual halt. The time lost simply could not be recouped." He thanked officials at the Culture Ministry for their "heroic efforts" but said it was "inappropriate" to rush the shipment to New York. "Our primary concern and responsibility is the safety of works of art."
It is true that Mrs. Alborch had taken over only at the end of July -- and that Spain traditionally closes down during the vacation month of August. But others, like Mr. Luzon, say the real problem was the Met's failure to present papers to the evaluations committee.
"It is unjust to blame the change of government or bureaucracy," he said. "They should have had someone in Spain handling all the paperwork for a very complex exhibition like this. That's what happened with the Islamic art exhibition. You can't replace permits from the owners with high-level representations in the ministry."
Mr. Luzon, who has returned to teaching archeology at a Madrid university, insisted that he had intervened personally to obtain the approval of some owners and had even appointed an official to handle the paperwork. Clearly bitter over suggestions that his office had not fully backed the exhibition, he said: "The Met may have had other difficulties, but it is not fair that they should seek a scapegoat. They've had to postpone an exhibition, and they're trying to save face."
His replacement, Mr. Guirao, agreed that the Met should have appointed a representative in Spain to nudge things along. "Administration in Spain is very complicated," he said, "and everything takes time. In this case, the pieces needed the approval of the owners, the regional governments and the junta. If the borrower only presents a list, the ministry has to do the querying."
For Ms. Tarapor, however, the paperwork demanded by the Culture Ministry came as a complete surprise. "Since 1987, we have never been asked to provide anything beyond a list with the insurance evaluation of the lenders," she said. "If the rules changed somewhere along the way, the ministry had a responsibility to inform us." Mr. Macarron said he too was puzzled by the requirement. "I've never before had to give loan forms to the junta," he said. And in Mr. de Montebello's view, this time the Met faced "an extremely conservative junta, many of whose members were not well disposed to loans."
Before postponing the exhibition, Mr. de Montebello met with a dozen aides to discuss whether the opening could be delayed. But it could not, Ms. Tarapor said, because the museum's calendar was packed for November and December, including openings of "The Gold of Meroe" on Nov. 23, the Florence Gould galleries on Dec. 1, "Diana Vreeland: Immoderate Style" on Dec. 9 and a Lucian Freud retrospective on Dec. 16. And the galleries where the Spanish medieval show was to be held were needed in early May for an exhibition of American Impressionism. The Future Morale Is Down, But Wait Till 1995
The postponement was clearly a blow to morale at the Met. "We're all feeling so badly burned," Ms. Tarapor said. "We have a shellshocked institution but one that has acted with honesty throughout." The museum had not yet calculated the cost of the postponement, she said, but she estimated the show's original budget at around $1 million. Still, at least there was no need to cancel expensive catering arrangements because, with no outside sponsor to pay for a gala dinner, the Met had planned a low-key opening. Further, since installation had not begun in the now empty galleries, only Mr. Macarron's company has still to be paid.
Was the Met a victim, Mr. de Montebello was asked. "We are, no question, but so is Spain and Spanish art, which is not being represented in the premier museum of the Western Hemisphere," he replied. In Spain, in contrast, the postponement of the exhibition came as a surprise. "Perhaps it was not well organized from the first moment," a Culture Ministry spokeswoman said, adding "The ministry acted impeccably."
Despite all the misunderstandings surrounding this affair, both the Met and Spain's Culture Ministry seem eager to resume good relations. Noting that the two sides have a long record of close collaboration, Mr. Guirao said Spain is "always interested in participating in any exhibition at an institution like the Met." And in a letter to Mrs. Alborch thanking her for her help, Mr. de Montebello said that 1995, when the Met celebrates its 125th anniversary, would be a "propitious occasion" to hold the medieval art exhibition "on a scale that the subject and the works deserve."
Ms. Tarapor was also ready to look forward. "It will be the most glorious show of Spanish art ever," she said. "And, if Spain wants it to be, it will be."

TAKING BETTER CARE OF EARTH: [FIVE STAR EDITION]

Author: Edward Flattau Copyright Edward Flattau

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/taking-better-care-earth/docview/303776343/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Those who are skeptical of any danger from future global warning argue that as long as major scientific uncertainty shrouds this phenomenon, it's foolhardy to launch costly initiatives attempting to retard climate change. Their conviction that we need further information before we can justify taking steps to reduce humanity-generated greenhouse gas emissions is distinctly a minority view in the scientific community. Yet, even if we were to implement these approaches to perfection and reduce greenhouse emissions sufficiently to slow global warming and eventually stabilize it, there will be at least a century when temperatures around the Earth will increase several degrees because of past discharged pollutants concentrated in the atmosphere. Thus, the risk of a potentially disruptive period extends far into the future, no matter how successful our preventive maneuvers.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=TAKING+BETTER+CARE+OF+EARTH&amp;author=Edward+Flattau+Copyright+Edward+Flattau&amp;issn=&amp;title=St.+Louis+Post+-+Dispatch+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-12-03&amp;spage=07.C&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Edward Flattau, Washington, writes on environmental issues.
Those who are skeptical of any danger from future global warning argue that as long as major scientific uncertainty shrouds this phenomenon, it's foolhardy to launch costly initiatives attempting to retard climate change.
Their conviction that we need further information before we can justify taking steps to reduce humanity-generated greenhouse gas emissions is distinctly a minority view in the scientific community.
The consensus is that on the basis of what is already known, it would be prudent to take some precautionary actions. Given the immense scope of the worst potential scenario, there's more risk in standing pat than in investing in mitigating strategies that turn out to be unnecessary.
Even if you agree with the skeptics, however, their do-nothing approach (in deference to checkbook concerns) doesn't make much sense in the face of greenhouse emission abatement measures that would be beneficial for society (and the economy), even if there were no global warming.
These readily available strategies primarily consist of vastly improving the efficiency of energy use in every sector of society, and of launching massive reforestation efforts.
Yet, even if we were to implement these approaches to perfection and reduce greenhouse emissions sufficiently to slow global warming and eventually stabilize it, there will be at least a century when temperatures around the Earth will increase several degrees because of past discharged pollutants concentrated in the atmosphere. Thus, the risk of a potentially disruptive period extends far into the future, no matter how successful our preventive maneuvers.
With that in mind, the Office of Technology Assessment, Congress' research arm, has just completed a study that reviews relatively painless ways for humanity to mitigate any disagreeable effects from accelerated climate change.
The technology office calls these strategies "no regrets" or "low regrets" options because they too would benefit society in their own right, separate from any moderating effect they might have on global warming's consequences. It turns out that these options are pretty much one and the same with the reforms that the national environmental movement has long been seeking.
The technology office arrived at its conclusions by deducing that the healthier and more diverse an ecosystem was, the more resistant it would be to any stresses brought about by climate change.
Because of the possibility that the sea level could rise due to global warming, the technology office urged that the government enact regulations to discourage further development in hazardous, flood-prone areas. Financial disincentives would include higher flood-insurance premiums or no insurance at all for would-be occupants of hazardous zones. Erosion setback requirements for houses on or near vulnerable beachfront acreage would be another reform.
Even if significant greenhouse-induced sea-level rise doesn't materialize, the threats to life, limb and property from severe storms would be diminished by these steps.
The technology office recommended that fiscal incentives be introduced to encourage more efficient use and conservation of water, given the possibility that rainfall patterns could shift and areas previously strangers to drought would no longer enjoy that good fortune.
As far as agriculture is concerned, the technology office suggested that efforts be intensified to develop drought-resistant variations of staple crops, and that government subsidies guide farmers toward diversification and flexibility in their operations.
The science of restoring and creating wetlands must be perfected, since we now know their enormous value as well as the potentially serious menace to their survival (depending upon location) from accelerated climate change.
The technology office proposed that federal land acquisition programs focus wherever possible on purchasing corridors for publically owned natural areas so that flora and fauna adversely affected by climate change might be able to migrate to more favorable haunts.
Again, it's important to remember that these technology office recommendations would do us a world of good regardless of any ameliorating effect they might have on unpleasant global warming impacts.
So while the prospect of climate change may be unsettling, it could still work to our advantage if it drives home the lesson that the better we take care of Earth, the better Earth will take care of us.

A PRUDENT PLAN ON GLOBAL WARMING: [NORTH SPORTS FINAL EDITION]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/prudent-plan-on-global-warming/docview/283682215/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: President Clinton's long-anticipated climate change action plan, aimed at reducing the dispersal of so-called greenhouse gases, is reminiscent of the traditional wisdom about chicken soup: It won't hurt you, and it very well may be good for you. It is not, however, making everyone feel better. Environmental groups generally are disappointed, because with a few exceptions the plan does not contain a call for restrictions and regulations that would better ensure its success. But in opting for a largely voluntary approach with government-industry partnerships, the administration chose wisely. Though there is concern that the buildup of greenhouse gases, such as carbon dioxide and methane, could lead to calamitous overheating of the planet, there is by no means scientific consensus about the threat-or even the fact that global warming is underway. In the absence of certainty, Clinton-like George Bush before him-is leery of committing the nation to a series of unnecessary command-and-control measures that could inflict enormous, recessionary costs on business.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=A+PRUDENT+PLAN+ON+GLOBAL+WARMING&amp;author=&amp;issn=10856706&amp;title=Chicago+Tribune+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-11-22&amp;spage=14&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   President Clinton's long-anticipated climate change action plan, aimed at reducing the dispersal of so-called greenhouse gases, is reminiscent of the traditional wisdom about chicken soup: It won't hurt you, and it very well may be good for you.
It is not, however, making everyone feel better. Environmental groups generally are disappointed, because with a few exceptions the plan does not contain a call for restrictions and regulations that would better ensure its success. But in opting for a largely voluntary approach with government-industry partnerships, the administration chose wisely.
Though there is concern that the buildup of greenhouse gases, such as carbon dioxide and methane, could lead to calamitous overheating of the planet, there is by no means scientific consensus about the threat-or even the fact that global warming is underway. In the absence of certainty, Clinton-like George Bush before him-is leery of committing the nation to a series of unnecessary command-and-control measures that could inflict enormous, recessionary costs on business.
The Clinton approach does differ markedly from the Bush philosophy in one respect: It formally commits the United States to specific goals and timetables, reducing emissions of culprit gases to 1990 levels by the year 2000. In that, it also goes a step beyond the Bush-engineered climate agreement at last year's Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro.
Under any strategy, the principle is that we're better off with fewer of these gases in the atmosphere.
The emphasis in the 50-point Clinton plan will be on using American technological ingenuity to increase energy efficiency, thus decreasing dependence on gas-creating fossil fuels. The government will encourage better heating and cooling of buildings, more energy-efficient lighting and-through new standards-more efficient household appliances.
Partnerships also will permit private investment in federal hydroelectric dams and promote more use of natural gas and renewable energy sources. There will be regulations to reduce methane emissions from landfills, and the Agriculture Department will assist more tree planting and conservation, recognizing that trees absorb carbon dioxide and release oxygen.
In sum, these seemingly small steps-even such measures as promoting better inflation of tires-can have a cumulatively significant effect.
The plan also is notable for what it does not include: an energy tax once favored by the administration, higher fuel efficiency standards for cars and a scheme to include auto insurance premiums in the pump price of gasoline-all steps advocated by various environmental groups.
Industry groups, relieved that they will not be facing costly mandates, are supportive of the plan. And their general enthusiasm is an indicator that they will participate eagerly in the partnerships, extending the progress they already have made.
The risk is that the plan won't be tough enough to make a difference and the worst predictions about global warming will turn out to be true. But the administration has a stick amid the carrots-a vow to regularly review progress with a threat of future mandates. For now, however, the course is prudent and may be the only remedy necessary.

Subject: Global warming

Climate Change Makes Our Hurricanes Worse: [Letter]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-change-makes-our-hurricanes-worse/docview/429276708/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Their findings are not heartening. Lloyd's of London -- which suffered $4.33 billion in losses in 1990 -- discovered that from 1966 to 1987, no natural disasters topped $1 billion in insurance losses. But in the next five years alone, 10 catastrophic windstorms cost more than $15 billion in insured losses. Swiss Re, the world's largest reinsurance company, acknowledged that the increase could be "an initial consequence of climatic change."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Climate+Change+Makes+Our+Hurricanes+Worse&amp;author=&amp;issn=03624331&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-09-02&amp;spage=A.22&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: To the Editor:
Your Aug. 18 report on preparedness for the hurricane season on the East and Gulf Coasts in the aftermath of 1992's Hurricane Andrew and your Aug. 19 article on building a house to withstand a hurricane both neglect climate. Scientists and insurance companies alike are concerned that the increased intensity, frequency and cost of storms may be related to the climate changes caused by global warming.
As concentrations of carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases derived primarily from the burning of fossil fuels increase in the atmosphere, Earth's climate could change dramatically. As insured losses from hurricanes and cyclones mount, several international companies have established in-house groups to study climate change.
Their findings are not heartening. Lloyd's of London -- which suffered $4.33 billion in losses in 1990 -- discovered that from 1966 to 1987, no natural disasters topped $1 billion in insurance losses. But in the next five years alone, 10 catastrophic windstorms cost more than $15 billion in insured losses. Swiss Re, the world's largest reinsurance company, acknowledged that the increase could be "an initial consequence of climatic change."
While millions of dollars are spent to improve flood walls and levees, little is being done to employ the safe energy that would reduce our emissions of greenhouse gases. No doubt, better building codes and enforcement are necessary, but the great Midwest flood reminds us that the best engineering can be breached by rampaging weather. Real disaster prevention requires kicking our fossil fuel habit. KELLY QUIRKE Washington, Aug. 20, 1993 The writer is with Greenpeace's climate change campaign.

Subject: HURRICANES AND TROPICAL STORMS; Housing; Greenhouse effect; Climate change

Location: East coast

People: QUIRKE, KELLY

Climate Change Makes Our Hurricanes Worse

Author: Quirke, Kelly

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-change-makes-our-hurricanes-worse/docview/109194092/se-2?accountid=10226

Title: September 1, 1993 (Page 2 of 40)

Publication title: The Desert Sun (1954-); Palm Springs, California

BLACK DAY FOR ATLANTIC CANADA; The death of a way of life; Cod fishery's closure to kill 12,000 jobs: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/black-day-atlantic-canada-death-way-life-cod/docview/239760635/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: It virtually stopped Tuesday. Fisheries Minister Ross Reid said stocks had hit record lows, and announced the closure of most of what remained of the fishery. About 12,000 people will lose their jobs; scores of communities in Quebec and the Atlantic Provinces will lose their raison d'tre. The impact of the closures will be felt in southwestern Nova Scotia, Cape Breton, Prince Edward Island, northeastern New Brunswick, Quebec and western and southern Newfoundland. It's impossible to say which communities will be affected until processing companies decide which plants to close and fishermen tie up their boats. Color Photo; Graphic, Diagram; Wayne Cuddington, Citizen; What next? Residents in scores of communities will e left high and dry y the indefinite fishing ban CP, Citizen graphic; (Map locating fishing zones) (turn photo) File photo; No fishing: 12,000 fishermen lost their jobs Tuesday because of declining fish stocks on the East Coast

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=BLACK+DAY+FOR+ATLANTIC+CANADA%3B+The+death+of+a+way+of+life%3B+Cod+fishery%27s+closure+to+kill+12%2C000+jobs&amp;author=&amp;issn=08393222&amp;title=The+Ottawa+Citizen&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-09-01&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Almost 500 years ago, explorer John Cabot declared the seas off Newfoundland were "swarming with fish, which can be taken . . . in baskets let down with a stone," and the East Coast cod fishery began in earnest.
It virtually stopped Tuesday. Fisheries Minister Ross Reid said stocks had hit record lows, and announced the closure of most of what remained of the fishery. About 12,000 people will lose their jobs; scores of communities in Quebec and the Atlantic Provinces will lose their raison d'tre.
The job losses are in addition to the 30,000 people left unemployed by last year's ban on cod fishing off eastern Newfoundland and quota cuts imposed to save fish stocks devastated by overfishing and climate change.
"Whatever we do, we have to be realistic," Reid told a news conference in Halifax. "We can't deal with wishes and hopes and dreams."
If there was any comfort for those in the industry, it lay in the fact that the news was expected.
Peter Hennebury, a 46-year-old cod fisherman in Lord's Cove, Nfld., exclaimed "Gee whiz, no!" when asked if he was surprised by the move, noting there are few fish left to catch.
"It was a wise decision. We've had failures in our fishery since 1989."
Hennebury noted he was glad to have scraped up the 10 weeks' work required to qualify for unemployment insurance.
Reid announced a $190-million short-term compensation package, but admitted he didn't know how long it would be before things get better. He insisted, though, there will be a fishery of some sort in the future.
"It doesn't mean abandoning all our rural communities; it doesn't mean abandoning our very unique and important way of life. But it means recognizing that all of us can't be there forever."
In a community such as Lockeport, N.S., on a peninsula jutting into the Atlantic south of Halifax, the only real reasons to "be there" are to fish, or to work at a fish processing plant.
"Fishermen are going bankrupt, that's the pure and simple fact of the matter. We have the choice now as to what we do in the next year or two as to how many go bankrupt," said Lockeport fisherman Joe Richmond.
The impact of the closures will be felt in southwestern Nova Scotia, Cape Breton, Prince Edward Island, northeastern New Brunswick, Quebec and western and southern Newfoundland. It's impossible to say which communities will be affected until processing companies decide which plants to close and fishermen tie up their boats.
The valuable bottom-feeding species such as cod, flounder, haddock and pollock account for half the fish caught in the region and provided at least half the 140,000 jobs in the East Coast fishery.
Jim Barkhouse, Nova Scotia's fisheries minister, estimated that 3,000 fishing and processing jobs will be lost in his province and another 3,500 people in related industries such as ship repair and supply will also be without work.
Bernard Valcourt, federal human resources minister, said compensation and retraining funds will be extended to affected fishermen and plant workers.
But the aid package, which will redirect money from existing programs to avoid padding the federal deficit, only covers displaced workers until next May.
The compensation is an "ad hoc response to an emergency situation," said Valcourt, warning people to take advantage of training programs to get out of the fishery.
"Young Atlantic Canadians have got to come to face the reality of the future," he said.
But Earle McCurdy, president of the Fisheries, Food and Allied Workers' Union, said: "You can't just shut down the fishery and say 'here's an adult basic education course, good luck. . . . We're not going to convert New World Island into Silicon Valley."
Reid said the federal government will ask foreign countries to stop fishing just outside Canada's 200-nautical-mile jurisdiction. The Northwest Atlantic Fisheries Organization, which sets quotas outside the zone, will be asked to impose the ban at a meeting in Halifax next week.
East Coast fishermen have seven days to remove their nets from the newly affected areas, said Reid, who adopted the report of a blue-ribbon panel -- the Fisheries Resource Conservation Council -- that recommended the closure a week ago.
A year after the federal government closed Newfoundland's northern cod fishery, council members from industry, government and the scientific community reported the lowest numbers of groundfish ever observed in the Northwest Atlantic.
The council report said the spawning stock of mature fish -- those able to reproduce -- is also at an all-time low. Fish are growing slower, it said, and a high percentage are being killed by the fishery, predators such as seals and ocean conditions such as cold water.
(With files from CP, Southam News, and Citizen staff)

Prairies turn into wetlands Records set for rainfall

Author: Roberts, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/prairies-turn-into-wetlands-records-set-rainfall/docview/385300802/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: "Things are looking pretty tough," said John Waterer, a crop specialist for the Manitoba Agriculture Department. "The crops are in tough shape," he said. "The basic problem is an absence of heat. That, plus the fact the crops are sitting in lakes of water."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Prairies+turn+into+wetlands+Records+set+for+rainfall&amp;author=Roberts%2C+David&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-08-20&amp;spage=A.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Prairies turn into wetlands Records set for rainfall
Friday, August 20, 1993
DAVID ROBERTS
Winnipeg MB -- BY DAVID ROBERTS Manitoba Bureau WINNIPEG The summer of '93 has turned out to be a rain-sodden writeoff on the Prairies.
Yesterday, Regina broke the record for summer saturation. The Queen City has seen the wettest June, July and August since people began to measure such things in the 1880s.
As of midnight yesterday, 344.4 millimetres of rain had fallen on Regina since June 1, smashing the 1909 record of 330.7 mm. And it hasn't exactly been hot. Regina's mean temperature in July was 15.7, compared with the normal 19.1.
Winnipeg obliterated its 1962 record for summer rainfall last week. As of Aug. 15, the Manitoba capital had absorbed 427.8 mm of rain since June 1. The normal rainfall for the period is 231 mm. It rained much of yesterday. And the day before. And the day before that.
Quite a contrast to the weather in Ontario. So far this month, about 30 mm of rain have fallen on Toronto, compared with the normal 40. And it has been warm - a mean temperature of 22, about normal for this time of the year.
Basements in the Winnipeg suburb of South Transcona have been under water for a month. As soon as homeowners bail out from one deluge, another comes along. After sewers overflowed, the residents took to canoes, scooping human excrement out of flooded alleyways and basements.
Almost daily, radio stations interview climatologists, meteorologists, psychologists, hydrologists and civil engineers about the uncommonly wet weather.
Everyone is talking about the rain. Psychologists have reported seeing a lot of depressed people in raincoats and rubber boots, many of them farmers.
"Things are looking pretty tough," said John Waterer, a crop specialist for the Manitoba Agriculture Department. "The crops are in tough shape," he said. "The basic problem is an absence of heat. That, plus the fact the crops are sitting in lakes of water."
For the second consecutive year, the outlook for the wheat harvest is less than optimistic. Oilseeds such as canola thrive in wet conditions, but lentils and vegetables have been all but wiped out.
There is no dearth of theories to explain all the rain after several years of drought; a volcano in the Philippines, the residue of the 1991 Kuwaiti oil fires, auto emissions and a Pacific atmospheric inversion, the so-called El Nino effect, are all being blamed.
On Wednesday, environmentalists in Ottawa called a news conference to suggest that the torrential prairie rains - and withered trees in Vancouver - are hints of catastrophic climate change caused in part by 463,133 kilotons of carbon dioxide spewed into the atmosphere over Canada last year.
But Tim Ball, a University of Winnipeg climatologist, said it is about time that prairie dwellers woke up to the fact that they live in a harsh and changeable part of the planet.
He said he is puzzled by those who express surprise at this summer's rains, because long-term records show that the area is due for a period of usettled weather.
"We're returning to a period of more variability of weather, much like we had prior to the 1940s," he said. "From '40 to '80, we had relatively consistent weather . . . and what people came to think of as normal during that period is anything but normal."
Whatever the cause of the rainfall - farmers say it just evaporates from the ground, creates clouds and falls again - insurance adjusters say the damage is in the millions of dollars.
Welfare recipients have been pressed into duty for flood cleanup.
The Red River is still bulging at its banks, although flooding is caused by runoff problems in the valley's clay-like soil and not by the kind of massive spring runoff that created the Great Winnipeg Flood of 1950.
Still, David Christianson, a Winnipeg financial adviser, has twice pumped nearly a metre of water and raw sewage from his basement his summer. "Four years ago, I was watering the foundation around the house because of the drought," he said.
Psychologists and local officials say people should not let the weather get them down, and they insist that the Prairies remain a great place to live.
"I'd say people are starting to come to terms with it," said Gene Kaprowy, a Winnipeg psychologist.
One day in Winnipeg last week, the rain suddenly stopped. Thousands of vexed and clammy homeowners cranked up their vacuum pumps and headed downstairs to slurp sludge from their basements.
As the sun peeked out and dirty clouds disappeared, scores of nervous people emerged to welcome back an old friend: the wide blue canopy of prairie sky. Jackets were put away, and shorts and bikinis came back out.
But fidgety people attract mosquitoes.
And the vast pools of water surrounding prairie communities are exquisite breeding grounds for 40 different species of mosquito. The sun comes out and the larvae hatch.
Regina caught a record number of them in bug traps a few weeks ago, just around the time Prime Minister Kim Campbell paid a visit. Ms. Campbell said that although she applied insect spray before a late-night jog in Saskatoon, she still got a few bites.
Entomologists were finding more than 4,000 mosquitoes a trap in some parts of Saskatchewan. The same thing happened in Winnipeg earlier this month as mosquito levels approached the July, 1991, numbers of 4,600 a trap. This coincided with a full moon - around Aug. 2 - at which time, experts say, mosquitoes become especially thirsty.
But the mosquitoes soon will disappear, pest experts say. They will be replaced by next week with an infestation of sow bugs and carpenter ants that have been secretly thriving in wet basements.

Subject: Wetlands

Science diary

Author: Radford, Tim

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/science-diary/docview/293485282/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: CHEERFUL news for corn-cob lovers from the investigation into climate change by the prestige-laden quarterly The Geographical Journal, published by the Royal Geographical Society. Another 3C and farmers will be able to harvest maize in the western Highlands, it says. There is a downside or two, such as estuarial and coastal flooding, as sea levels rise. And also, according to Dr John Boardman of Oxford and a colleague, an erosion problem. He examined a field in the South Downs and predicted a 27 per cent increase in topsoil loss by 2050 because of extra rainfall. Meanwhile, the summers that triggered shrinkage in clays and subsidence in foundations also set the insurance market trembling. The insurers had presumed that baking hot summers such as that of 1976 - triggering clay shrinkage and subsidence - would happen perhaps once in every 1,000 years. But by 2050, according to John Doornkamp of Nottingham, such summers could occur every five years. Watch the premiums soar.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Science+diary&amp;author=Radford%2C+Tim&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-08-12&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: AN ECONOMIST from the US Environmental Protection Agency has forecast that by 2000, the cumulative retail value of pharmaceutical drugs based on plants will be $500 billion. This is about half of Britain's current GNP. A biodiversity-minded biologist points out that from now on every two years is likely to see the extinction of a plant with medicinal potential which he works out at a retail-market loss of $12 billion by 2050. Never mind the market, what about the value of lives saved? The OECD sets a value of $12.4 million on a human life, and plant-derived anti-cancer drugs save 90,000 lives a year (in the rich world). So the benefits of the plants used against cancer could be put at $1.1 million million. But most of these plants are found - and being extinguished - in the tropics, where the poor live and die. Glum sum: eventually, the Global Environment Facility of the World Bank might spend $1 billion a year on conservation.
MORE sums from the Americas: according to the Global Change Division of the US Environmental Protection Agency, if there were no controls on ozone destroying chemicals, there would be much more skin cancer from ultraviolet light the ozone layer is supposed to stop. The bill? Among Americans living now, or born by 2075, there would be an additional 354 million cases of skin cancer. The treatment of which, by 2165, would cost $40 million million. The cost of controlling the ozone-eaters however, is likely to total $36 billion, largely by 2000. Sounds like a healthy investment.
CHEERFUL news for corn-cob lovers from the investigation into climate change by the prestige-laden quarterly The Geographical Journal, published by the Royal Geographical Society. Another 3C and farmers will be able to harvest maize in the western Highlands, it says. There is a downside or two, such as estuarial and coastal flooding, as sea levels rise. And also, according to Dr John Boardman of Oxford and a colleague, an erosion problem. He examined a field in the South Downs and predicted a 27 per cent increase in topsoil loss by 2050 because of extra rainfall. Meanwhile, the summers that triggered shrinkage in clays and subsidence in foundations also set the insurance market trembling. The insurers had presumed that baking hot summers such as that of 1976 - triggering clay shrinkage and subsidence - would happen perhaps once in every 1,000 years. But by 2050, according to John Doornkamp of Nottingham, such summers could occur every five years. Watch the premiums soar.
In recent years, the villagers of Gourna in southern Egypt have made their money from tourism. But the recent slump, in the wake of terrorist attacks, has made archaeologists nervous that villagers will turn to grave robbing from the 420 ancient tombs in the vicinity to make ends meet. The Egyptian Antiquities Organisation has come up with a drastic solution: it wants Gourna dismantled and its inhabitants moved to another village. Villagers are resistant. One resident said the government need not worry about the treasures. "They've gone and they went long ago," he said.

Leading the global environmental fight: [Final Edition]

Author: Warden, Kathryn

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/leading-global-environmental-fight/docview/269693598/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Some say that's exactly why Elizabeth Dowdeswell, a former home ec teacher from rural Saskatchewan, is the right person to hold the world's top environmental job. While still in her early 30s, Ms Dowdeswell was made Saskatchewan's deputy minister of culture and youth. But she was fired when the Tory government came to power in 1982 and wanted its own stamp on cultural policy. When Mr. [Ian Wilson] retired in 1987, Ms Dowdeswell flew out from Ontario for the day to surprise him at his party.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Leading+the+global+environmental+fight&amp;author=Warden%2C+Kathryn&amp;issn=08390185&amp;title=The+Hamilton+Spectator&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-07-10&amp;spage=A.8&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: It takes a strong person to shake up a global organization that's become set in its ways over two decades.
But some say that's exactly why Elizabeth Dowdeswell, a former home ec teacher from rural Saskatchewan, is the right person to hold the world's top environmental job.
She's the first woman to head the United Nations Environment Program, an agency with a $120-million two-year budget.
"She won't 'She has a sort of religious fervor -- she feels she can make a difference in the world."
Others remark on Ms Dowdeswell's tenacity, her people skills, her public speaking ability and her dedication to saving the planet and building peace.
Six months into her four-year term, Ms Dowdeswell has served notice to her staff of 800 from roughly 60 countries she intends to cause "constructive damage to the status quo."
Sustainable development
In a recent interview from New York, she said UNEP must become a catalyst for sustainable development, not just environmental protection. For any country, rich or poor, a healthy environment and a sound economy are inextricably linked.
UNEP has to take a new direction, she said. "There have to be a number of changes in what we do and how we do it."
For instance, she strongly feels UN environmental programs should be aimed more at women.
"In many developing countries, it's really the women who make the daily decisions. The way in which household fuel and water is used is largely the result of the women."
As well, UNEP's management practices have to "come into the 21st century," she said.
As an undersecretary general to UN Secretary General Boutros Boutros-Ghali, Ms Dowdeswell, 48, is the highest-ranking Canadian and the most senior of only a handful of women in top UN positions.
"Too often I'm the only woman in the room and that's got to change," she said.
The latest figures, from 1991, showed there are only about 32 senior female managers in the UN secretariat. That's roughly one woman for every 10 men in those positions.
While the glass ceiling isn't gone, "It sure is cracking and she's evidence of that," said Joan Martin-Brown, a New York-based UNEP official.
She said Ms Dowdeswell's appointment in January was "particularly exciting" since it came on the heels of last year's Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro, which called women full partners in sustainable development and environmental management.
"The fact she's the boss is a strong signal that this wealth of capacity held by women cannot be left on the shelf."
Ms Dowdeswell is also executive director of Habitat, an agency which deals with problems of the world's cities and communities. She sees both agencies as "instruments of peace."
"It's disputes over natural resources that cause countries to go to war, and that triggers refugees to move from one place to another," she said.
"If we can make a dent in solving the problem of management of natural resources, then we have an opportunity to avoid conflict."
Though she is based in Nairobi, half her time is spent travelling. In one recent week, her job took her to four continents for meetings in New York, Nairobi, Paris and China.
That may seem like a long way from her roots in a string of small Saskatchewan towns.
Ms Dowdeswell's family moved to Canada from Northern Ireland when she was five. The eldest of eight children, she grew up with a sense of community and public service. Her father was a United Church minister and her mother a teacher.
She was active in 4-H and is remembered by former provincial 4-H supervisor Thelma Howard as an outstanding member who won many sewing awards and was "fun to have around." To this day, Ms Dowdeswell is said to design and sew her own clothes.
After graduating with a bachelor of science degree in home economics and a teaching certificate from the University of Saskatchewan, she worked in Swift Current for 10 years as a home ecconomics and drafting teacher, a guidance counsellor and adult education worker.
Vision, pragmatism
Those years gave her a combination of vision and pragmatism critical to her current work.
"With Saskatchewan people there is a vision that keeps you going, but a pragmatism about the day-to-day realities," she said.
In 1972, she earned a master's degree in behavior science at Utah State University. That landed her a job for a year teaching economics and marketing at the University of Alberta in Edmonton.
Ian Wilson, then Saskatchewan's deputy education minister, hired her as a consumer education consultant. She moved on to become the department's human rights ombudsman and was eventually put in charge of federal-provincial education matters.
Mr. Wilson, now chairman of the Saskatchewan Institute of Applied Science and Technology, says "Liz" was often highly disciplined and critical of herself, but could also laugh at herself.
Though often "scared out of her wits" having to publicly explain controversial policies, she came across as self-assured and dynamic.
While still in her early 30s, Ms Dowdeswell was made Saskatchewan's deputy minister of culture and youth. But she was fired when the Tory government came to power in 1982 and wanted its own stamp on cultural policy.
She headed to Ottawa, where she became a consultant to the Treasury Board, managing public inquiries such as those on water policy and weather services, as well as a royal commission on unemployment insurance.
"A lot of my life has been spent trying to bring the public into public policy-making," she said.
After directing the Ontario office of Environment Canada, Ms Dowdeswell moved up to assistant deputy environment minister in charge of the atmospheric environment service.
There she helped develop public services such as the ozone watch and the ultraviolet index. She was also Canada's representative to the World Meteorological Organization.
Mr. Wilson said Ms Dowdeswell's career moves have been motivated by "a sense of service and need. She has never really pushed or applied to do things. People ask, 'Would you do this?' And so she takes it on."
Key negotiator
She moved into the global spotlight as Canada's negotiator on the climate-change treaty signed at the Earth Summit.
"Everybody talked about Liz's ability to chair meetings," said Ms Bax. "She allowed each country to have its say, but ended up with a position that served everybody's interest."
Ms Dowdeswell can be "very cutting and very direct" but is not afraid to show a softer, caring side. "She would take the time to talk with staff when they're were having problems or grief over the loss of someone."
Despite her busy schedule, friends say she never forgets birthdays. And she's often generous and impulsive.
When Mr. Wilson retired in 1987, Ms Dowdeswell flew out from Ontario for the day to surprise him at his party.
Her marriage of almost 10 years broke up in the mid-1970s.
"Although she has lived alone, she is not a lonely person," said Mr. Wilson. She enjoys playing piano and singing with friends, but has little time for these interests now.
She has the "polish, sophistication and charm of a Jeanne Sauve" and would make a great ambassador some day, said Mr. Wilson.
But for now, Ms Dowdeswell has a job she loves.
"I feel I'm always learning. And I meet wonderful, wonderful people who really care about what's happening to this world."
