WHAT PRICE PROGRESS?: [FINAL EDITION]

Author: Spears, Tom

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/what-price-progress/docview/244253732/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: They`re dealing with greenhouse gases, the pollutants such as carbon dioxide that threaten to make the Earth`s climate hotter and shift patterns of rainfall in a few decades. Estimates range from 30 cents to $65 of damage from every tonne of the carbon in carbon dioxide effects such as drought in farmland, severe storms and higher sea levels that would flood coastlines. (The conference has heard that $20 a tonne is the likeliest range.) For instance, a vehicle that burns 30 litres of gasoline a week produces about a tonne of carbon (in carbon dioxide) a year.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=WHAT+PRICE+PROGRESS%3F&amp;author=Spears%2C+Tom&amp;issn=08281815&amp;title=Calgary+Herald&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-05-09&amp;spage=B.7&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Do you spend $25 to clean up $20 worth of pollution? That`s what the experts are debating
MONTREAL - Let`s say a tonne of pollution does $20 of damage to the Earth`s climate. You could prevent that pollution, but it would cost you $25. Is it worth the cost?
In Montreal last week, economists and climate experts from 100 countries wrestled with the nitty-gritty of financing a cleaner Earth.
They`re dealing with greenhouse gases, the pollutants such as carbon dioxide that threaten to make the Earth`s climate hotter and shift patterns of rainfall in a few decades.
The problem is that carbon dioxide comes from burning coal, oil, gasoline and natural gas things that most societies say they can`t do without.
This is the first meeting of a new working group in the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, the United Nations body tackling what may be the worst global environmental threat a harsher climate. Its report, due in 1995, will go to all the countries that signed the Climate Change Convention at last June`s Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro.
So far, the conference has been civil despite the controversial nature of debates about who should pay to prevent global pollution, and how much. "Just wait till the end of the week," predicted Jim Bruce, the Canadian who co-chairs the working group.
There are no answers yet in the conference`s big, garishly red and black auditorium on Sherbrooke Street just disquieting questions. These include:
* How much damage really results from carbon dioxide, the most important greenhouse gas?
Estimates range from 30 cents to $65 of damage from every tonne of the carbon in carbon dioxide effects such as drought in farmland, severe storms and higher sea levels that would flood coastlines. (The conference has heard that $20 a tonne is the likeliest range.)
Scientists say we`re adding about 1.6 billion tonnes of carbon to the air each year, a rate that would raise global temperatures between 1.7 and 3.8 degrees in the next century.
* Is future damage likely to occur gradually? Some say yes, while others say warmer temperatures might have no effect for a while, until suddenly something snaps in the world`s climate.
Big insurance companies believe the sudden-snap theory, says Bruce. That`s because there were no disastrous storms with $1 billion of insurance losses before 1987, while there have been 15 of them since then.
* What if the cost of reducing pollution is greater than the cost of living with pollution? Do we try to limit the carbon dioxide levels in our air at any cost?
* Should we be as worried about an amount of damage to the environment 100 years from now as we would be if it occurred tomorrow?
* Assume that a hotter, drier climate would wipe out many of a country`s farms. These represent just three or four per cent of that country`s gross domestic product. Do we sacrifice the farms to keep bigger economic sectors healthy, or save them at enormous cost because people need food?
* How would carbon taxes work? These are taxes on fossil fuels based on the amount of carbon they produce.
For instance, a vehicle that burns 30 litres of gasoline a week produces about a tonne of carbon (in carbon dioxide) a year.
The working group`s job isn`t to tell world governments what to do, but to say, "Here are different theories of how to control carbon dioxide emissions, along with their costs and benefits."
It`s also been assigned the task of figuring out how much carbon dioxide the world`s factories, cars, furnaces and electrical generating stations are likely to pump out in the future.
(Distributed By
SouthamStar Network.)

Is it worth it?: Experts debate costs of a cleaner Earth: [Final Edition]

Author: Spears, Tom

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/is-worth-experts-debate-costs-cleaner-earth/docview/239765940/se-2?accountid=10226

Mother Earth gets hto under the collar Storm warning: [AM Edition]

Author: Lambert, Shaena

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/mother-earth-gets-hto-under-collar-storm-warning/docview/436823994/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Hurricanes, cyclones, tornadoes and other wind storms could be the climate's first signals of discomfort at the onset of global warning. And if predictions are right, as the Earth heats up the message could get a lot nastier. In the last 200 years we've poured 500 billion extra tons of carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide into the air, creating a gaseous blanket around the Earth which soaks up light and heat. The resulting greenhouse effect has made the Earth's temperature rise half a degree, and most scientists expect it will shoot up a further 3 degrees Celsius by the year 2100. If this change seems small, it's not: the last ice age was caused by just a 5 degree dip in temperature. They fear what's known as a runaway greenhouse effect, a scenario where the planet overheats to the point that a host of dangerous side effects kick in, exacerbating the heating trend. This might occur if ice caps melted, releasing deep stores of frozen methane; areas of deep permafrost thawed releasing massive carbon stores; or phytoplankton, which plays a key role in absorbing carbon dioxide, was seriously depleted.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Mother+Earth+gets+hto+under+the+collar+Storm+warning&amp;author=Lambert%2C+Shaena&amp;issn=03190781&amp;title=Toronto+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-04-21&amp;spage=A.21&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: BEFORE LAST month's storm swept in from the Gulf of Mexico and deluged much of Eastern Canada and the United States, it struck the west coast of Florida, including a small barrier island where my friends and I were vacationing.
Hurricane-force winds tore down palm trees, smashed the concrete pool-side patio, shook the house and yanked sewage pipes open.
It felt at times as though the storm was directing a furious message at us, a premonition I discovered later may not be far off.
Climatologists and environmentalists now think that the recent surge in big storms may indeed be the Earth's attempt to tell us something.
Hurricanes, cyclones, tornadoes and other wind storms could be the climate's first signals of discomfort at the onset of global warning. And if predictions are right, as the Earth heats up the message could get a lot nastier.
In the last 200 years we've poured 500 billion extra tons of carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide into the air, creating a gaseous blanket around the Earth which soaks up light and heat. The resulting greenhouse effect has made the Earth's temperature rise half a degree, and most scientists expect it will shoot up a further 3 degrees Celsius by the year 2100. If this change seems small, it's not: the last ice age was caused by just a 5 degree dip in temperature.
Already there are worrying signs: Seven of the hottest years in human history have been in the last 10. There have been droughts; there have been food shortages; and there has been an upsurge in disastrous winds.
When Hurricane Gilbert devastated Jamaica and parts of Mexico in 1988, meteorologists said it could happen only once in a 100 years. Then came Hugo in 1989, also dubbed "hurricane of the century." Last September, both were topped by Andrew, which at more than $20 billion broke records in destruction.
Meanwhile, five unprecedented wind storms have swept over northwestern Europe, while four record-breaking cyclones have devastated Pacific islands.
To put this in human terms: in the 1960s and 1970s there were 14 natural disasters, each of which left more than 10,000 people homeless. In the 1980s, there were 70.
According to a science adviser at Canada's Atmospheric Environment Service, an upsurge in storms makes senses in a hotter world. It takes warm water (over 26 degrees) condensing and releasing moisture to fuel a tropical storm. So with oceans getting hotter, we can expect hurricanes to get bigger and for some to start further north.
This means Canadians may be in for bigger storms, especially in coastal areas. But the most noticeable short-term effect could be to raise insurance rates.
Munich Re, the world's largest re-insurance agency, recently warned: "For the first time in human history, mankind is about to change the climate significantly and possibly irreversibly, without having any idea of the consequences it will have."
It estimates global warming could cause natural disaster liabilities to rise from $20 billion to more than $100 billion a year. To compensate, some re-insurance corporations have hiked their rates by 300-500 per cent, while one company, the International Netherlands Group, has decided it can't afford to provide any insurance against natural disasters.
And massive wind storms may just be heralding worse to come.
A recent poll of 400 climate scientists conducted by the environmental group Greenpeace revealed that an alarming 45 per cent of them think that the end of the world, if not at hand, is a distinct possibility.
They fear what's known as a runaway greenhouse effect, a scenario where the planet overheats to the point that a host of dangerous side effects kick in, exacerbating the heating trend. This might occur if ice caps melted, releasing deep stores of frozen methane; areas of deep permafrost thawed releasing massive carbon stores; or phytoplankton, which plays a key role in absorbing carbon dioxide, was seriously depleted.
Nobody's saying these doomsday scenarios are certainties. As the Earth heats, climatologists are quick to point out they just don't know what will happen. The point is that they are all too real possibilities.
The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, the major international committee of scientists studying global warming, believes we must cut greenhouse gas emissions by at least 60 per cent to stop the warming trend.
Yet, as Kevin Jardine of Greenpeace points out, Canada continues to be among the nine largest producers of greenhouse gas. While the federal government has talked a good line, it hasn't acted on its promises, even its latest not to increase its output of carbon dioxide by the end of the century.
But even if Canada were to follow through on its commitments, it wouldn't be enough, according to Greenpeace and other environmental groups. The only long-term answer must be to invest in renewable energy alternatives - despite protests from the petroleum industry.
Faced with a crisis out of the realm of our experience, and almost our imagination, we can't say we haven't been warned. The message is being delivered by scientists, environmentalists, and recently by the Earth itself: The question is, how many more "storms of the century" will it take before we listen?
Shaena Lambert is a Toronto writer specializing in peace and environment issues.
Illustration Caption: Star photo: HURRICANE ANDREW: Satellite shows storm approaching gulf coast.

Top UN post was a shocker SUCCESS STORY " Elizabeth Dowdeswell still finds it hard to believe that she is the highest-ranking woman, and Canadian, at the United Nations

Author: Rusk, James

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/top-un-post-was-shocker-success-story-elizabeth/docview/385405436/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: "I pinch myself and say, 'Is this real?' " Ms. [Elizabeth Dowdeswell] said. "I grew up as a child with such respect for the United Nations, and I think that is true of so many Canadians. I can't believe I'm an undersecretary- general . . . at this moment in time when you can really make a difference."

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Top+UN+post+was+a+shocker+SUCCESS+STORY+%22+Elizabeth+Dowdeswell+still+finds+it+hard+to+believe+that+she+is+the+highest-ranking+woman%2C+and+Canadian%2C+at+the+United+Nations&amp;author=Rusk%2C+James&amp;issn=03190714&amp;title=The+Globe+and+Mail&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-04-21&amp;spage=A.3&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Top UN post was a shocker SUCCESS STORY " Elizabeth Dowdeswell still finds it hard to believe that she is the highest-ranking woman, and Canadian, at the United Nations
Wednesday, April 21, 1993
JAMES RUSK
BY JAMES RUSK Environment Reporter THREE months after becoming both the highest-ranking Canadian and the highest-ranking woman at the United Nations, Elizabeth Dowdeswell is still in a state of disbelief.
"I pinch myself and say, 'Is this real?' " Ms. Dowdeswell said. "I grew up as a child with such respect for the United Nations, and I think that is true of so many Canadians. I can't believe I'm an undersecretary- general . . . at this moment in time when you can really make a difference."
From a start as a home economics teacher in 1966 in southwestern Saskatchewan, she has risen to be a UN undersecretary-general in New York and executive director of the UN's two Nairobi-based agencies, the UN Environment Program (UNEP) and Habitat, which deals with the problems of the world's cities.
Her appointment appears to be characteristic of the changes that Secretary-General Boutros Boutros-Ghali is seeking to make in the organization.
All too often in the past, UN agencies such as UNEP have been headed by ex-politicians, who turned the agencies into personal fiefs in which they dispensed patronage and privilege and sought self-aggrandizement, all at the expense of both the UN purse and its ideals.
To stop this, Mr. Boutros-Ghali has sought out experienced public- sector managers as agency heads, leaders who can rebuild demoralized staffs, keep budgets in line and foster co-operation among international institutions, says David Runnalls of the Ottawa-based Institute for Research on Public Policy.
In tapping Ms. Dowdeswell, he turned to a seasoned public servant. The future undersecretary-general rose rapidly through the ranks in Saskatchewan and Ottawa after her marriage of almost 10 years broke up in the mid-1970s.
The daughter of a United Church minister in her native Saskatchewan, she grew up in a number of small towns throughout the province. After a brief stint as a teacher at the University of Alberta, she went to work for the Education Department in Saskatchewan, where she was in charge of rewriting the province's consumer-education curriculum. Later, she became the department's human-rights ombudsman, and finally was put in charge of federal-provincial educational matters.
After a period as deputy minister of culture and recreation, Ms. Dowdeswell moved to Ottawa and worked for the Treasury Board, was executive director of the Royal Commission on Unemployment Insurance and was director of the Ontario office of Environment Canada.
Her last post in Ottawa, before leaving for the United Nations, was as assistant deputy environment minister in charge of the atmospheric environment service, a post that brought her international attention as Canada's negotiator on the climate-change treaty signed at last June's Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro.
"I've never predicted most of the jobs I've been in," Ms. Dowdeswell said of her eclectic career. "I've been very fortunate."
Along the way, she was involved in a number of public inquiries, the most important being the Royal Commission on Unemployment Insurance in the mid-1980s, an experience she sees as vital in her current post.
The institutional processes around the Earth Summit were unique. The UN formally involved non-governmental groups with an interest in the issues in both the preparations for the meeting and the meeting itself, and Canada even made non-governmental representatives official delegates to the meeting.
As Ms. Dowdeswell sees from the paper that flows across her desk, this process is becoming entrenched in the UN system, and UN managers will need to be able to find ways of listening to the world's people, as well as its governments, as they seek solutions to apparently intractable global problems.
Ms. Dowdeswell, a silver-haired woman of great presence and dignity who, one Environment Canada employee said, is "one of those people who you know is in the room even though she hasn't said a word," said that in her work she must balance two sets of challenges - one managerial, one substantive.
"Fundamentally, I don't believe that the world is best served by one- person institutions. What we need to be able to do is to harness and to mobilize all the resources that we bring to bear. We can't do that if policy making is done by one person and the views of the experts aren't heard."
To do this, she hopes that after four years at UNEP ("I say four years deliberately because that is my term, and I don't want to lose that sense of urgency") the organization will be "lean and mean but with the accepted intellectual power to always be at the leading edge."
UNEP, which is 20 years old, has created a global awareness of environmental problems, but in the wake of the Earth Summit it has to take on a new role as a partner with a whole range of institutions and individuals, Ms. Dowdeswell said.
"One of the hard things for UNEP to learn is that it is not a one-man show, that it is not the only game in town," she said, explaining that her senior staff members have been told that their performance appraisals will reflect their ability to play an effective role in UN processes in which the agency is not the leader.
As for her other operational agency, Habitat, of which she has been head for less than a month, Ms. Dowdeswell said it was a logical fit with environment, as many of the problems of cities in the developing world are environmental.
That does not mean they should be combined, but she expects that she can bring some efficiencies to the two groups. There is, for instance, no need for them to maintain separate sets of conference and translation services.

Rare-breed ark: So all oureggs aren't in one basket: Conservancy is home toJacob, the four-hornedram, Pilgrim geese ...It's an act of insurance: [1* Edition]

Author: Read, Jeani

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/rare-breed-ark-so-all-oureggs-arent-one-basket/docview/267466286/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: What about the endangered cow? The endangered sheep? The endangered pig? The endangered . . . turkey? "I think people are more aware of the more exotic animals - the ones that capture the imagination," says Alan Regan, supervisor at Stanley Park's Children's Zoo, which includes a number of rare domestic breeds in its menagerie of horses, goats, sheep, geese, chickens, pigs, llamas and cows. Unquestionably, Regan's animal superstar is the startling, magical-looking Jacob four-horned ram. But others are here too: Dexter cattle, Pilgrim geese, Barbados Blackbelly sheep and this year, an honored guest: A rare - and pregnant - Tamworth pig.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Rare-breed+ark%3A+So+all+oureggs+aren%27t+in+one+basket%3A+Conservancy+is+home+toJacob%2C+the+four-hornedram%2C+Pilgrim+geese+...It%27s+an+act+of+insurance&amp;author=Read%2C+Jeani&amp;issn=&amp;title=The+Province&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-04-14&amp;spage=A.14&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Friday: How people help protect genetic diversity: Action, adventure and nurture.
I think we can safely say that most people have heard of the famous, endangered spotted owl of Pacific northwest old-growth forests.
And we probably all know about the endangered whale, bald eagle, Chinese panda and African elephant - and on and on.
But what about the endangered cow? The endangered sheep? The endangered pig? The endangered . . . turkey?
Yes, I'm afraid it's true, Virginia: Many turkeys ARE endangered. And no, it is definitely not a joke.
As with most of our planet's other resources, domestic farm animals have become victims of the high-production, short-term profit motive. And many are almost extinct: Just like the spotted owl.
"I think people are more aware of the more exotic animals - the ones that capture the imagination," says Alan Regan, supervisor at Stanley Park's Children's Zoo, which includes a number of rare domestic breeds in its menagerie of horses, goats, sheep, geese, chickens, pigs, llamas and cows.
The Children's Zoo belongs to the Rare Breeds Conservancy - which protects it from being simply a "zoo" (bad word). Now it's also a preserving and even, on a minor scale, breeding operation devoted to the survival of rare species (good words).
Unquestionably, Regan's animal superstar is the startling, magical-looking Jacob four-horned ram. But others are here too: Dexter cattle, Pilgrim geese, Barbados Blackbelly sheep and this year, an honored guest: A rare - and pregnant - Tamworth pig.
Why are creatures such as these important? "Genetically, we should not have all our eggs in one basket," says Regan with a smile. "As it were."
The rare breeds, you see, all have valuable characteristics it would be dangerous to lose. The Dexter cow, for example, is known as the "poor man's cow" because it can survive nicely on little pasture and food.
That Tamworth pig can rustle around, foraging for its own food. "That kind of adaptability - the lack of it - is one of the things you worry about with the established commercial breeds."
Basically, what happened was this: As recently as 30 years ago, a rich diversity of livestock breeds flourished on our lands. Modern agriculture, however, demanded specialization and the highest possible levels of productivity. Now a very few breeds fill all our needs - agriculture has become, in every sense of the word, "an industry".
Genetic diversity and flexibility ARE being lost. If climate change or a new disease wastes those few vulnerable breeds, we - as well as they - are in big trouble.
Preserving rare breeds is thus a survival strategy as well as an act of respect for our heritage: A kind of "insurance policy" to sustain and improve agriculture in the future.
The Rare Breeds Conservancy flyers and information newsletters point out that genetic conservation can be justified "on historical, cultural, aesthetic, recreational, biological, scientific, agricultural and economic grounds."
But there's something else, too. Something perfectly simple.
"It's nice to keep them anyway - for their own sake," says Regan. He surveys his little ark fondly. "You shouldn't always need an excuse to preserve things. It's a shame to let anything go."
Illustration Wayne Leidenfrost/ Alan Regan, with Jacob the ram, at the Stanley Park Child- ren's Zoo. (93-2458) ;

ZEITGEIST

Author: Bussmann, Tom

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/zeitgeist/docview/293324155/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Nice to see that Glen Ford, 76, The Fastest Gun Alive, is still alive. But a second look at the Hello! snaps of his wedding (number four) to Jeanne Baus, 33, raise some doubts. He appears to be wearing rather more make-up than his bride. That, and the Happy Childhood Smile that almost brings an expression to his face, look strangely like the work of Mr Joyboy, the cosmetic mortician in Evelyn Waugh's The Loved One. His suit, shirt and tie are of the kind that button down the back. Glen's not been too well, what with the pneumonia and ulcer-related blood-clotting, but now he's in good, if slightly long-finger-nailed, hands. His lovely young bride was once his nurse - "the professional attention she provided turned into affection and gradually grew into love" - and she's seen tenderly ministering to her ex-patient with a large slice of cake.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=ZEITGEIST&amp;author=Bussmann%2C+Tom&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-03-20&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: 1. CRIME TIME
So now we know. Or, rather proudly in this new era of less understanding, we don't. Watching television leads to crime. As opposed to unemployment, which doesn't. But then again, if people had jobs, they might watch less television, and therefore commit less crime. And what of the proven link between television and unemployment, particularly if you are (a) chairman of the BBC or (b) an actor in Eldorado?
2. EYE OF THE NEEDLE
The merciful Kenneth Clarke is to review the system of heavier fines for the better off. This after "a pensioner" (nice emotive word), Norman Smith, was convicted of drinking and driving and was fined pounds 3,200 for a first offence, while a less
financially-fortunate second offender was fined pounds 76 by the same court. The aggrieved Mr Smith "publicised his complaint through a full-page ad in his local paper", thereby further publicising his wealth . . .
3. NEEDLE'S EYE WIDENED, LATEST!
Mr Smith's problem is that he is not rich enough. Otherwise, all he needs do is invite himself (and a few other wealthy folk dissatisfied with the law as it stands) to dinner at No 10, leaving the odd pounds 17 million under the saucer as a tip. Not that this would have the slightest influence on future legislation . . .
4. PROFIT OF DOOM
Greenpeace may have finally found an argument that hits home. Wind storm damage has soared, along with global warming, accounting for 85 per cent of insurance losses of $53 billion. According to Jeremy Leggatt, scientific director of Greenpeace: "It is clear that a healthy insurance industry will not be possible in a world in the grip of human-induced climate change." Minor considerations, such as the loss of human life or possibly the planet, melt no ice caps in the minds of the rich and powerful. But the loss of money? Particularly all that once-painless Lloyds lucre? Now you're talking.
5. NOT TONIGHT, FATIMA
Researchers keen to pursue the missing link between video and vice should hit the road to Morocco forthwith, if not fifthwith. There, Chief Police Commissioner Haj Mohammed Mustapha Tabet has been sentenced to death after confessing to sex "with more than 1,600 women in three years". The commissioner's a goner, but what of the upright (take that as you will) judiciary? Prosecutor Riahi helpfully showed the court "118 pornographic videos of Mr Tabet and his friends having sex with 518 women and girls". Call it one hour per video, and let's assume an eight-hour day - that's a solid fortnight's viewing, plus overtime. By now the cream of the Casablanca legal system must be either (a) potential sex criminals or (b) off the idea for life.
6. PILLOW BITER
Talking of which, the Daily Mail chose to illustrate an article on non-performing husbands pleading a headache with a picture of Doris Day and - yes - Rock Hudson.
7. TAKE-AWAY
Chris Patten speaks, Li Peng replies and the Hang Seng index drops 5 per cent in a day. Keep this up for a month and we'll have to pay the Chinese to take Hong Kong off our hands. What a shame Mr Patten's talents are confined to the Colonies. He could certainly give Norman Lamont a run for our money.
8. UP THE WALL
While on Wall Street, the latest topic is inflation. Once thought as dead as the Eighties, it's back, and it's in business. Last week, 150 investment managers attended a symposium on the subject, at a cost per head of $525. Crikey, 10 years ago, it wouldn't have been half that . . .
9. SAY GOODBYE!
Nice to see that Glen Ford, 76, The Fastest Gun Alive, is still alive. But a second look at the Hello! snaps of his wedding (number four) to Jeanne Baus, 33, raise some doubts. He appears to be wearing rather more make-up than his bride. That, and the Happy Childhood Smile that almost brings an expression to his face, look strangely like the work of Mr Joyboy, the cosmetic mortician in Evelyn Waugh's The Loved One. His suit, shirt and tie are of the kind that button down the back. Glen's not been too well, what with the pneumonia and ulcer-related blood-clotting, but now he's in good, if slightly long-finger-nailed, hands. His lovely young bride was once his nurse - "the professional attention she provided turned into affection and gradually grew into love" - and she's seen tenderly ministering to her ex-patient with a large slice of cake.

Leading article: The bad side of a good move

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/leading-article-bad-side-good-move/docview/293298930/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Mr [Norman Lamont] introduced it, rather cunningly, as if it were something forced on a reluctant Chancellor by the need to reduce carbon emissions in fulfilment of pledges made last June to the UN Convention on Climate Change at Rio. For this reason, Greenpeace yesterday welcomed the tax increases - even though they didn't go far enough and despite its preference for a carbon tax of the kind proposed by the EC but rejected by Britain. The EC praised Mr Lamont's taxes on energy and car usage as moving in the same direction as Community environment policies. The step also marks a big leap in the harmonisation of EC VAT rates.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Leading+article%3A+The+bad+side+of+a+good+move&amp;author=&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-03-18&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WHICH measure in Norman Lamont's Budget achieved the unlikely miracle of uniting fiscal reformers, the European Commission and Greenpeace against the poverty lobby? His controversial decision to levy VAT of 17 1/2 per cent in stages on domestic gas and electricity. The move is expected to raise pounds 3 billion a year in revenue. And it is exactly the sort of measure which puts every liberal-minded person's prejudices into a mincing machine.
Mr Lamont introduced it, rather cunningly, as if it were something forced on a reluctant Chancellor by the need to reduce carbon emissions in fulfilment of pledges made last June to the UN Convention on Climate Change at Rio. For this reason, Greenpeace yesterday welcomed the tax increases - even though they didn't go far enough and despite its preference for a carbon tax of the kind proposed by the EC but rejected by Britain. The EC praised Mr Lamont's taxes on energy and car usage as moving in the same direction as Community environment policies. The step also marks a big leap in the harmonisation of EC VAT rates.
Fiscal reformers liked the Budget because it marked a further stage in the transfer of taxation from income to spending (as long as one ignores the increase in national insurance contributions). And anyway, increased taxation was inevitable for a country which has been living beyond its means (as witness a prospective budget deficit of pounds 50 billion) and paying its employed population much more than it has been earning for a number of years. Even now, at the end of the longest recession since the 1930s, average earnings are rising by almost five per cent - when all that is needed to maintain living standards is an increase of under one per cent. In the US, where unemployment is much lower, there has been hardly any increase in real earnings for a decade. Surely, argument blending with argument, the case for a tax increase is overwhelming?
Not quite. The trouble with putting VAT on energy is that it hits the poor dramatically more heavily than it hits the rich. As Fran Bennett points out on this page, the poorest fifth of households already spends more than twice as much of the weekly budget on fuel as the average household does. And, according to the Institute for Fiscal Studies, the poorest households (with less than pounds 60 a week of disposable income) spend 14 per cent of their income on fuel - compared with three per cent by the very richest (over pounds 800 per week). So the poor are disproportionately, even viciously, hit by Mr Lamont's VAT decision. True, he has diluted instant criticism by pledging that means-tested benefits will rise to reflect the change; but he doesn't say by how much. And, as the IFS points out, those just above the means-tested benefit level, including millions of pensioners, will not be helped.
The Government's track record is not one to inspire trust among the poor. The Conservatives have effected a massive redistribution of income from rich to poor in the past decade without any obvious impact on economic growth. There are lives and much misery at stake here. Mr Lamont has to be better than his word here. He must be monitored rigorously to ensure that the poor are not kicked while they're down by a reform which otherwise has a lot going for it.

Signs of climate change?: [FINAL Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/signs-climate-change/docview/306564486/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: As one who has devoted my life's work to the development of spirituality in my fellow humans and who unashamedly contends that organized religion is a most powerful tool for enhancing spiritual growth, I am highly distressed and saddened by recent events in Texas and Florida. More often than not, these misguided headline seekers believe they are special agents of the almighty with a unique calling that somehow makes OK the most atrocious and ungodly behavior imaginable. Please join with me in denouncing these recent cycles of terror and in exposing them for what they really are _ reprehensible acts of violence against both God and God's children. Wesley Vaughan, pastor South San Jose, Calif.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Signs+of+climate+change%3F&amp;author=&amp;issn=07347456&amp;title=USA+TODAY+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-03-16&amp;spage=08.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: THE EDITORIAL PAGE; LETTERS
From Hurricane Hugo to Hurricane Andrew to this past weekend's blizzard, we are amazed by the increasing severity of our weather.
Yet, Vice President Al Gore's book, Earth in the Balance, predicted that global warming would result in more severe storms.
It appears that he was right.
We must all work together now, ordinary people, businesses, schools and governments worldwide, to protect our planet and its environment.
Our way of life, maybe even our lives, depends on it. Arthur M. Keller Palo Alto, Calif.
Worst of century? Bull
Was last weekend's storm really the worst of the century? Not in New York, it wasn't. Maybe in other places it was, but not in New York. My God, it was only 12 inches.
I remember that back in 1947 we had, on Christmas Eve and Christmas Day, 26 inches of snow.
That was a storm. And we've had others _ 18, 19 inches. This was only 12.
It's the yuppies who run the media now; they're under 45 and have never seen a snowstorm or a northeaster in their lives.
They never went through what the old-timers went through in the '20s, '30s and '40s.
Also, the northeaster in December was like a thunderstorm compared with the 1938 hurricane in New York.
All the young people under 45 never have seen bad weather like we did in the old days.
I have to laugh when people say, "Oh, it's the worst I've seen." Charles Conran Great Neck, N.Y.
Target all bad habits
I was pleased to see the statistics regarding the general fitness of this country, printed in your Life section ("More of us losing battle of the bulge," Friday).
Why is it that while everyone can point to the burden smoking places on the system, no one seems to mention the 66% of the population that is overweight?
I was under the impression that arteries get clogged by improper diet and lack of exercise, which can lead to heart attacks, which result in angioplasty and bypass surgery.
I would guess these procedures would be expensive and are being covered by the health-insurance companies.
If the administration is going to add a "sin tax" to cigarettes and alcohol, I think we should tax McDonald's, Frito-Lay and Hershey's accordingly.
If we are to really solve our health-care problems, all aspects must be considered, not just the unpopular, easily targeted habits.
I am, by the way, a non-smoker. Pamela Hynes Rolling Meadows, Ill.
Terror in name of God
As one who has devoted my life's work to the development of spirituality in my fellow humans and who unashamedly contends that organized religion is a most powerful tool for enhancing spiritual growth, I am highly distressed and saddened by recent events in Texas and Florida.
With the dawning of each new day, one is bombarded with grisly headlines and horror stories about dreadful violence committed by deranged maniacs who actually claim to be acting on behalf of God.
More often than not, these misguided headline seekers believe they are special agents of the almighty with a unique calling that somehow makes OK the most atrocious and ungodly behavior imaginable.
This letter is an urgent plea to men and women of true faith and moderate temperament everywhere:
Please join with me in denouncing these recent cycles of terror and in exposing them for what they really are _ reprehensible acts of violence against both God and God's children. Wesley Vaughan, pastor South San Jose, Calif.

Greenpeace says insurers face global warming disaster

Author: Springett, Pauline; Brown, Paul

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/greenpeace-says-insurers-face-global-warming/docview/293407351/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Greenpeace, the environmental pressure group, in a study published today - Climate Change and the Insurance Industry - is urging insurers to lobby against the fossil fuel industry. Dr Jeremy Leggett, scientific director of Greenpeace, said insurers were worried about the heavy climate-related losses. Greenpeace argues that such change, caused by the build-up in the atmosphere of gases such as carbon dioxide from the burning of fossil fuels, is likely to induce weather pattern change including cyclones such as Hurricane Andrew which hit Florida last year.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Greenpeace+says+insurers+face+global+warming+disaster&amp;author=Springett%2C+Pauline%3BBrown%2C+Paul&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-02-03&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: UK INSURERS are being warned that they could face financial ruin if they fail to react to the dangers of global warming.
Greenpeace, the environmental pressure group, in a study published today - Climate Change and the Insurance Industry - is urging insurers to lobby against the fossil fuel industry.
Dr Jeremy Leggett, scientific director of Greenpeace, said insurers were worried about the heavy climate-related losses.
The insurance industry has reacted to the recent string of climatic abnormalities, such as hurricanes and droughts, by sharply pushing up rates, decreasing cover, and increasing deductibles. In some cases, cover has been withdrawn. In Hawaii it is no longer possible for new householders to insure against storm damage. In Samoa, a "cyclone proof" certificate is required before insurance inquiries can be accepted.
Dr Leggett said: "Healthy economics are impossible without a healthy international insurance industry. It is becoming clear that a healthy insurance industry will not be possible in a world in the grip of human-induced climate change."
Greenpeace argues that such change, caused by the build-up in the atmosphere of gases such as carbon dioxide from the burning of fossil fuels, is likely to induce weather pattern change including cyclones such as Hurricane Andrew which hit Florida last year.
In the UK, the four-year drought in the South-east, which caused subsidence and cracking in many buildings, has been followed by heavy rains, which insurers fear may begin to cause the opposite problem of "heave".
Insurers are also worried about huge areas of East Anglia in danger of flooding because they are below the five-metre contour level. Some areas are on alert from flood risk once every five years, and the National Rivers Authority is warning against new building. In some cases, new buildings in these danger zones will be uninsurable.
One leading Lloyd's underwriter, whose syndicate specialises in reinsurance, said the issue would become critical if the reinsurers, who tend to be badly hit first, start to withdraw cover. And he said that since 1989, the reinsurance rates for insuring companies in the US have shot up by 400 per cent, in the UK by 600 per cent and in Japan by 1,000 per cent.
"But I'm not too hopeful of the insurance industry being a good lobbier for change. It is too fragmented and too conservative," he said.
A spokesman for the Association of British Insurers said the industry was still assessing the impact of global warming. "Anyway, it has its upside - the warmer winters have meant fewer burst pipes."

Global warming: Threat to insurers?: [FINAL Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-threat-insurers/docview/251993884/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: LONDON (AP) - The environmental group Greenpeace said yesterday that global warming might have stirred the recent spate of hurricanes that have run up billions of dollars in losses for insurance companies. Greenpeace said in a report it hopes the insurance industry will become its ally in the global warming debate with the fossil fuels industry, which disputes environmentalists' claims that the "greenhouse effect" is dangerously heating up the planet. Greenpeace blames the burning of such fuels, including oil, for global warming. Greenpeace scientific director Jeremy Leggett presented his findings to a group of Lloyd's underwriters at their request last week.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+warming%3A+Threat+to+insurers%3F&amp;author=&amp;issn=26417634&amp;title=Pantagraph&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-02-03&amp;spage=D.2&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: LONDON (AP) - The environmental group Greenpeace said yesterday that global warming might have stirred the recent spate of hurricanes that have run up billions of dollars in losses for insurance companies.
One of those insurers, Lloyd's of London, agreed the idea makes sense. But one climatologist expressed strong doubts, saying all available 20th century data on hurricanes shows the biggest ones have come along in cooler years, while warm weather tends to bring a greater number of milder storms.
Greenpeace said in a report it hopes the insurance industry will become its ally in the global warming debate with the fossil fuels industry, which disputes environmentalists' claims that the "greenhouse effect" is dangerously heating up the planet. Greenpeace blames the burning of such fuels, including oil, for global warming.
Greenpeace scientific director Jeremy Leggett presented his findings to a group of Lloyd's underwriters at their request last week.
"They're beginning, I think, to panic," Leggett said in a telephone interview yesterday.
Lloyd's and other insurers, including many in the United States, have suffered staggering losses in recent years. A $20 billion blow came from Hurricane Andrew, which hit the Miami area last year.
Scientists disagree over whether global warming actually has begun, but nearly all scientists agree that the possibility of global warming is a serious threat.
Leggett acknowledged it is impossible to prove whether recent warmer temperatures are caused by the accumulation of such gases as carbon dioxide or merely a fluke fluctuation.
But if consumption of fossil fuels has indeed begun a trend of higher temperatures, the planet could be in for more wind storms that will cause increasing amounts of damage, he said.
"The insurance industry has started to wake up to the threat that climate change poses to its profitability," Leggett said in the report. "Some industry analysts are looking at the results of climate modeling and wondering how much of their current misfortunes can be put down to human-induced climate change."
Greenpeace found a receptive audience for its theories at Lloyd's, the huge insurance market.
"We're not experts on the weather," said a Lloyd's spokesman. "We note Greepeace says it could be changing for the worse and we know bills have been going up ... premiums have inevitably got to rise."
The Lloyd's spokesman declined to discuss any other factors that could have contributed to the big losses. He spoke on condition of anonymity.
Critics of Greenpeace's stance point out that coastal areas have become increasingly built up by developers, leading to an inevitable increase in damage whenever a hurricane strikes.

Subject: Global warming

Company / organization: Name: Greenpeace; NAICS: 813312, 813940; Name: Lloyds of London; NAICS: 524210; DUNS: 22-729-8270

The Insurers Bet on Global Warming: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Dyer, Gwynne

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/insurers-bet-on-global-warming/docview/406775241/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Then there was Cyclone Ofa in 1991, the biggest tropical storm ever seen in the South Pacific. And Cyclone Val a year later, which devastated both Western Samoa and American Samoa, and Cyclone Iniki last autumn, the strongest to hit Hawaii in over a century. The sea's surface has to be a minimum of 27 degrees Celsius, in fact. So if there are more and bigger tropical storms around than ever before, then more of the sea's surface must be at least 27 degrees, or the same area stays that temperature longer, or both. Then early last year Sir John Houghton, former head of the Meteorological Office and now chairman of the United Nations Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, released a report saying that things are probably even worse than they seem. Volcanic dust from the Mount Pinatubo eruption and sulphur dioxide from power stations, he suggested, are temporarily cooling the planet and masking the greenhouse effect.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+Insurers+Bet+on+Global+Warming&amp;author=Dyer%2C+Gwynne&amp;issn=19308965&amp;title=The+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-02-02&amp;spage=7.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: London. -- "The waves now are fantastically high on average," said Sheldon Bacon of the Institute of Oceanographic Sciences in England last August. "And why? Goodness knows. What pings around in the brain is the greenhouse effect."
Mr. Bacon was discussing the astounding fact that wave heights in the North Atlantic Ocean have risen by fully 25 percent over the past quarter-century, although average wind speeds have remained steady. That doesn't make any sense, and Mr. Bacon suspects that global warming is to blame.
But he can't prove it. The global interactions between sun, atmosphere, land areas and seas are so ferociously complex that nobody can even produce a reliable 30-day forecast of the weather, let alone a 30-year forecast of the climate.
Almost everybody fears that the extra carbon dioxide and other "greenhouse gases" released by human industrial and agricultural activities will change the global climate. But you can't even be certain what is happening in the present, given how much global temperature fluctuates from one year to another. Now, however, we have a different way of measuring warming: money.
The trillion-dollar global insurance industry takes a keen interest in weather patterns, because it naturally wants to limit large pay-outs to cover storm damage. The cost of Hurricane Andrew, which struck the U.S. Gulf Coast last year, for example, was around $20 billion.
Hurricane Andrew, by traditional standards, was a "100-year storm," a once-in-a-lifetime event. But so was Hurricane Gilbert in 1988, the strongest-ever Caribbean storm, and Hurricane Hugo in 1989 ($6 billion insured losses).
Then there was Cyclone Ofa in 1991, the biggest tropical storm ever seen in the South Pacific. And Cyclone Val a year later, which devastated both Western Samoa and American Samoa, and Cyclone Iniki last autumn, the strongest to hit Hawaii in over a century.
Last year Munich Re, the world's largest reinsurance company, commissioned a report on windstorm damage. It found its worst suspicions confirmed. Total economic losses from windstorm damage in the '80s were three times greater than in the '60s. Insured losses were five times greater. "We definitely have a trend which, without exaggeration, may be regarded as dramatic," concluded the report's authors.
What does this have to do with global warming? Like the mysteriously bigger waves in the North Atlantic, more and bigger storms give us no direct proof of temperature change. But tropical storms require a tremendous amount of energy, which means that they can only form over warm water.
The sea's surface has to be a minimum of 27 degrees Celsius, in fact. So if there are more and bigger tropical storms around than ever before, then more of the sea's surface must be at least 27 degrees, or the same area stays that temperature longer, or both.
The actual change in sea temperature may be slight, but the results are already quite dramatic. Not only are traditional hurricane regions getting an unprecedented battering, but Western Europe has been hit repeatedly by near-hurricane storms in the past few years.
The Munich Re report predicts that it will just go on getting worse, with full-scale hurricanes visiting Western Europe and the Mediterranean for the first time in recorded history. If the insurance industry does not change the way it assesses storm damage risk, it concludes, "catastrophe" payments will rapidly escalate from $20 billion to $100 billion a year.
What the insurance industry cannot tell us, unfortunately, is how much warming, and how fast: it's like a canary in a coal-mine. And the specialists are still confused and divided among themselves.
In 1989, for example, the British Meteorological Office predicted that global warming would amount to 3 degrees Celsius over the next 70 years. But in 1991 the same group of meteorologists revised their forecast downwards by half, predicting that it would be only 1.5 degrees warmer by 2060.
Then early last year Sir John Houghton, former head of the Meteorological Office and now chairman of the United Nations Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, released a report saying that things are probably even worse than they seem. Volcanic dust from the Mount Pinatubo eruption and sulphur dioxide from power stations, he suggested, are temporarily cooling the planet and masking the greenhouse effect.
But when the volcanic dust washes out with the rain, and sulphur dioxide emissions are cut by the scrubbers that power stations everywhere are now having to install, we may suddenly discover that the planet got a lot hotter while we weren't looking. "It will be like a curtain being drawn back to let in the sunlight," one scientist said.
How much warmer? How fast? Who knows? But a poll of climate scientists taken a year ago revealed that 13 percent now believe that an unstoppable "runaway" greenhouse effect is likely, and another 32 percent think it is possible. We live in interesting times.
Gwynne Dyer syndicates a column on world affairs.

Subject: Global warming

People: Bacon, Sheldon

WAITING FOR THE HURRICANE; Indications are that global warming offers the world a stormy future: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Dyer, Gwynne

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/waiting-hurricane-indications-are-that-global/docview/432391610/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Then there was Cyclone Ofa in 1991, the biggest tropical storm ever seen in the South Pacific. Full text:   "The waves now are fantastically high on average," said Sheldon Bacon of the Institute of Oceanographic Sciences in England last August. "And why? Goodness knows. What pings around in the brain is the greenhouse effect."
Bacon was discussing the astounding fact that wave heights in the North Atlantic have risen by fully 25 per cent over the past quarter-century, although average wind speeds have remained steady. That doesn't make any sense, and Bacon suspects that global warning is to blame.
But he can't prove it. The global interactions between sun, atmosphere, land areas and seas are so ferociously complex that nobody can even produce a reliable 30-day forecast of the weather, let alone a 30-year forecast of the climate.

Total economic losses from windstorm damage in the 1980s were three times greater than in the 1960s. Insured losses were five times greater.
"We definitely have a trend which, without exaggeration, may be regarded as dramatic," concluded the report's authors.
What does this have to do with global warming? Like the mysteriously bigger waves in the North Atlantic, more and bigger storms give us no direct proof of temperature change. But tropical storms require a tremendous amount of energy, which means that they can only form over warm water.
The sea's surface has to be a minimum of 27 degrees Celsius, in fact. So if there are more and bigger tropical storms around than ever before, then more of the sea's surface must be at least 27 degrees C, or the same area stays that temperature longer, or both.

The Munich Re report predicts that it will just go on getting worse, with full-scale hurricanes visiting western Europe and the Mediterranean for the first time in recorded history.
If the insurance industry does not change the way it assesses storm damage risk, it concludes, "catastrophe" payments will rapidly escalate from $20 billion to $100 billion a year.
One implication of all this is that you should think twice before buying coastal property below the five-metre contour line. Insurance costs may rise very rapidly in coming years, if indeed insurance coverage is available at all. The other, larger implication is that global warming is indeed under way.
What the insurance industry cannot tell us, unfortunately, is how much warming, and how fast: it's like a canary in a coal-mine. And the specialists are still confused and divided among themselves.
In 1989, for example, the British Meterological Office predicted that global warming would amount to 3 degrees C over the next 70 years. But in 1991 the same group of meterologists revised their forecast downwards by half, predicting that it would be only 1.5 degrees warmer by 2060.
Then early last year Sir John Houghton, former head of the Meterological Office and now chairman of the United Nations Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, released a report saying that things are probably even worse than they seem.
Volcanic dust from the Mount Pinatubo eruption and sulphur dioxide from power stations, he suggested, are temporarily cooling the planet and masking the greenhouse effect.
But when the volcanic dust washes out with the rain, and sulphur dioxide emissions are cut by the scrubbers that power stations everywhere are now having to install, we may suddenly discover that the planet got a lot hotter while we weren't looking. "It will be like a curtain being drawn back to let in the sunlight," one scientist said.
The whole issue remains highly controversial, which is hardly surprising given the scientific complexity of the question and the immense amounts of money at stake. But the insurance industry is a usefully impartial source of evidence, and it suggests that warming is already a fact.
How much warmer? How fast? Who knows? But a poll of climate scientists taken a year ago revealed that 13 per cent now believe that an unstoppable "runaway" greenhouse effect is likely, and another 32 per cent think it is possible.
We live in interesting times.

RESOURCES CHANGES IN ENERGY, ENVIRONMENTAL POLICIES WILL BE SUBSTANTIAL, IF NOT DRAMATIC Series: CLINTON: TOUGH CHOICES RESOURCES Second of five parts: [FIVE STAR Edition]

Author: Bill Lambrecht Post-Dispatch Washington

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/resources-changes-energy-environmental-policies/docview/303697600/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: "We are optimistic that this administration will take environmental issues very seriously and not present confrontation between environmental and economic goals, as in the past," said Christopher Flavin, vice president of Worldwatch Institute, an organization that advocates some new approaches. As a senator, Gore became an environmental advocate. But President-elect Clinton will be calling the shots, and he may not be so ambitious. As governor of Arkansas, he seldom put a priority on energy and the environment. Indeed, Arkansas' land and rivers suffered during Clinton's 12-year stewardship. Clinton won praise from pollution-control advocates for picking [Carol M.] Browner as EPA administrator and Bruce Babbitt as interior secretary. Browner fought to protect the Everglades as Florida's chief environmental officer; Babbitt, a former governor of Arizona, has headed the League of Conservation Voters.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=RESOURCES+CHANGES+IN+ENERGY%2C+ENVIRONMENTAL+POLICIES+WILL+BE+SUBSTANTIAL%2C+IF+NOT+DRAMATIC+Series%3A+CLINTON%3A+TOUGH+CHOICES+RESOURCES+Second+of+five+parts&amp;author=Bill+Lambrecht+Post-Dispatch+Washington&amp;issn=&amp;title=St.+Louis+Post+-+Dispatch+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-01-14&amp;spage=01.C&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: LIFE WILL BE "greener" under the Bill Clinton-Al Gore administration when it comes to energy and the environment.
But dramatic shifts in policy may not occur swiftly because of vexing choices and built-in resistance awaiting the new government.
By many accounts, this is a turning point: The shifting global economy, climate change and destruction of the atmosphere from pollution could compel a new, more sustainable way of living.
Some of the changes brewing could be as profound as those accompanying the end of the Cold War: weaning ourselves from foreign oil, shifting to solar power and renewable energy, eliminating waste in factories and in the home.
Vice President-elect Gore has talked of phasing out the internal combustion engine one day. Much sooner, he wants a Marshall Plan, or massive program, to clean up environmental messes around the world.
Clearly, the new generation in Washington has created many expectations.
"We are optimistic that this administration will take environmental issues very seriously and not present confrontation between environmental and economic goals, as in the past," said Christopher Flavin, vice president of Worldwatch Institute, an organization that advocates some new approaches.
Some of those who could feel the sting of new approaches are resigned to their inevitability.
Referring to stricter rules on pesticides, Chet Dickerson, Monsanto Co.'s director of agricultural affairs in Washington, said: "Threat might not be the right word. It may be long-term reality."
But questions remain as to how quickly things will, and should, change:
Clinton's Cautious Style
As a senator, Gore became an environmental advocate. But President-elect Clinton will be calling the shots, and he may not be so ambitious. As governor of Arkansas, he seldom put a priority on energy and the environment. Indeed, Arkansas' land and rivers suffered during Clinton's 12-year stewardship.
Rather than commanding change, Clinton is more likely to use market forces, such as urging industries to trade pollution credits to reduce industry-wide pollution. He is expected to consider tax incentives for energy conservation.
Carol M. Browner, Clinton's choice to head the Environmental Protection Agency, worked during her confirmation hearing this week to mollify critics.
"The EPA should promote and encourage rewards for businesses that take the lead in developing pollution prevention and recycling strategies," she said.
Pollution-control advocates don't know what to expect. Clinton "may turn out to behave more like a moderate Republican, at least at first," said Ken Cook, vice president of the Center for Resource Economics, which deals with environmental issues in farming. "And that might be OK, since we have a lot of catching up to do."
Mixed Signals
Clinton won praise from pollution-control advocates for picking Browner as EPA administrator and Bruce Babbitt as interior secretary. Browner fought to protect the Everglades as Florida's chief environmental officer; Babbitt, a former governor of Arizona, has headed the League of Conservation Voters.
But Clinton's choice of Hazel R. O'Leary as secretary of the Energy Department is a different story. As an executive of Northern States Power Co. of Minnesota, she has pushed coal-fired and nuclear power plants.
Her company was a partner in an ill-fated plan last year to build the coal-fired plant in Florida near Lake Okeechobee - a plan that Browner helped kill.
O'Leary has angered American Indians in Minnesota for campaigning personally to store more nuclear waste at her company's Prairie Island nuclear plant. The plant is adjacent to Sioux Indian land on an island in the Mississippi River.
"We really question why they would appoint someone who has been involved with the nuclear industry like this," said Tom Goldtooth, environmental coordinator for the Red Lake Band of Chippewa Indians in Minnesota.
New Ideas, Same Game
The administration and many members of Congress may be new. But the real power on Capitol Hill has changed little. Likewise, the lobbying that accompanies the perennial battles over natural resources has changed little.
If Clinton gets around to pushing higher fuel economy requirements for vehicles, he will have to contend with Rep. John D. Dingell, D-Mich., chairman of the House Energy and Commerce Committee and protector of the auto industry.
When it comes to limiting oil use, Sen. J. Bennett Johnston, D-La., will have a lot to say. Likewise, Clinton's treasury secretary, Sen. Lloyd Bentsen, D-Texas, did not look benevolently on providing tax incentives for renewable energy sources when he was chairman of the Senate Finance Committee.
"The danger is getting bogged down in some of the same old fights," remarked Flavin.
Little Money; Other Priorities
With another huge budget deficit looming, the Clinton-Gore administration will be strapped as far as innovations.
The EPA alone received $110 million this year from what was requested. And the price tag for cleaning up radioactive messes at defense plants around the country has grown by hundreds of millions.
Blake Early, a Sierra Club lobbyist, said that he is preparing to rely on more favorable interpretations of old laws rather than expecting new programs.
"I don't expect this administration to be lobbing out a lot of new initiatives," Early said.
Chet Dickerson, Monsanto Co.'s director of agricultural affairs in Washington, believes that the economy and health care will consume the brunt of attention in the early going.
"I don't think that the Clinton administration will want to jump into some of these controversial areas," Dickerson said. "`Next year? Our jobs could be tougher."
Even before taking office, Clinton's administration has signaled some of the likely changes. Natural gas is likely to be seen more favorably as an alternative to oil, coal and other fossil fuels, judging by Clinton's associates.
Lawyers who work for natural gas interests held influential positions in Clinton's transition planning. Clinton's chief of staff, Thomas F. "Mack" McLarty, until recently was chairman of Arkla Inc., a natural gas company.
Some environmental advocates have come to prefer clean-burning natural gas over some of the other fuels. But they worry that Clinton's administration might become so dependent on natural gas that it fails to provide sufficient incentives to speed the era of renewables.
Attitudes toward oil might be affected by the devastating spill this month in the North Atlantic that damaged the Shetland Islands. The accident occurred even as the oil industry was negotiating to water down hull standards for tanker ships.
"The disaster underscores our continuing vulnerability as a nation in relying on shipments of imported oil," observed Jack Doyle, an analyst with Friends of the Earth, an international organization.
Early in Clinton's term, energy policy may well be tax policy. As a presidential candidate, Clinton opposed a gasoline tax. But he recently said that he might accept a hike in the 14-cent federal gas tax if it were accompanied by tax breaks for the middle class.
Changing Decisions
Government agencies expect new ways of doing business and are making decisions that might be different two weeks from now.
Last week, the EPA approved a test-burn at a new hazardous waste incinerator in East Liverpool, Ohio. Gore has made few waves during the transition. But he has asked for an investigation on why the government allowed the incinerator to be built.
Shortly after the November election, the Department of Agriculture relaxed rules covering outdoor testing of genetically engineered crops. And in November, the EPA dropped some of the rules for testing the effects of pesticides on birds and other creatures.
These decisions foreshadow struggles on the horizon. The biotechnology industry - which uses gene-splicing to create products - escaped new statutes that would govern it during the Reagan-Bush era. Instead, existing laws were modified to cover areas that were quite different from what Congress had in mind.
The industry now worries about more regulation from the Food and Drug Administration and less protection of patents under the new administration.
"Biotechnology is at a crucial stage in its development," Stephen A. Duzan, chairman of an industry trade association, wrote in a letter to Clinton this month.
Rules governing pesticides could be in for drastic overhaul. The new administration will be looking closely at a federal court decision in California that could require banning of many pesticides. In the so-called Delaney case, a federal court ruled that pesticides cannot be used if they have been shown to cause cancer in lab animals.
While chemical manufacturers could suffer, waste disposal operators may prosper under Clinton. The $1.5 billion air pollution control industry alone could grow 8 to 14 percent in the next few years, analysts say.
Waste disposal companies worry about curbs on waste burning and stricter rules for landfills and transportation. But they are optimistic about the business that may come their way under an administration concerned about pollution.
"What they're going to do is going to be a plus for us and anybody else who gets revenues dealing with environmental problems," said William Y. Brown, a vice president of Waste Management Inc., the biggest disposal company.
Stronger Enforcement
While taking it slow on new programs, Clinton's administration is likely to enforce regulations that have been delayed or ignored.
For instance, federal farming law requires those who farm erodable land to have conservation plans by next year in order to partake of subsidies, crop insurance and many federal programs. The provision has particular bearing in the hills of Missouri, where crops are planted on sloping land.
Similarly, advocates of sustainable farming are frustrated at roadblocks to shifting away from heavy chemical use. Another federal program that was established to certify organic farms couldn't operate very effectively last year: It received $15,000 from the administration of President George Bush.
Betty Bailey, director of the North Carolina-based Rural Advancement Fund, said that she was hoping for new policies that look to the future.
"Many things could be changed in the first 100 days," she said.
Wilderness advocates are breathing easier these days. They no longer worry about threats to millions of acres of wetlands contained in a proposal to change definitions. They are shifting now from defense to offense, planning for strong language in a newly drawn Endangered Species Act.
And Clinton, following through on a campaign promise, is preparing to hold a forest summit in the Northwest soon after becoming president. He will be stepping into a long-running fight about cutting trees.
Scott Feierabend of the National Wildlife Federation said he sees a new atmosphere along with the new approach that the Clinton-Gore team is bringing to town. Many industries fighting to protect jobs and subsidies might not agree.
Feierabend said, "There seems to be a whole new mentality of cooperation rather than combativeness."
Illustration PHOTO; Caption: Photo of containers filled with glass bottles.

Our principles and goals

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/our-principles-goals/docview/333600789/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Promote innovation in education and demand greater accountability by educators for pupil performance, particularly in Milwaukee Public Schools, with emphasis on closing the achievement gap between white and minority students. Encourage greater parental involvement in schools. Demand wholehearted efforts to improve discipline and cut truancy and dropout rates. Support the renewal of neighborhoods and improve Milwaukee's downtown and lakefront, while preserving open spaces and historically significant buildings. Promote cooperation by Milwaukee and its suburbs to improve mass transportation, housing and job opportunities. Encourage land-use policies that stem urban sprawl and keep development in sync with public services. Encourage more aggressive US leadership on environmental issues, including the fight against ozone depletion and global warming. Insist that international lending policies promote environmentally sensitive development. Pursue resource conservation, recycling and the elimination of toxic substances from air, land and water. Back tougher policies to prevent agricultural and urban runoff into waterways.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Our+principles+and+goals&amp;author=&amp;issn=10524452&amp;title=Milwaukee+Journal&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1993-01-03&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Today The Journal Editorial Board reaffirms its priorities on local, state, national and worldwide issues. We offer them as a clear statement of what The Milwaukee Journal stands for.
We are strongly independent, bearing no allegiance to political party or special interest. Our aim is tough-minded realism, not orthodoxy. We believe in civil liberties, representative government and equal opportunity. We support both free enterprise and sensible government action. We emphasize responsibility
civic as well as personal.
We are well aware that achieving such goals requires the sustained labor of many diverse forces. The Journal adds its voice to those efforts.
Local and state
Crime control
Support appropriate funding for law enforcement, criminal prosecution and punishment of criminals in both community and institutional settings. Provide testing and treatment of all criminals for alcoholism and other drug abuse. Encourage greater concern for crime victims, including the broader use of restitution orders to compensate victims. Campaign for tighter gun controls. Work to restore neighborhoods as safe places to live and conduct business.
Gambling
Draw the line on gambling in Wisconsin. Beef up enforcement of gambling laws. Expand help for addicted bettors. Promote other forms of economic development on Indian reservations.
Tax reform
Support affordable approaches to local property-tax relief, including greater state funding of public schools and other mandated services; a reduction in tax exemptions; the elimination of inefficient spending; greater sharing of services and cooperation among governments; and the enactment of tax alternatives, such as county sales taxes and surcharges on entertainment tickets.
Race relations
Confront personal prejudices, discourage stereotyping and promote tolerance. Encourage integration of housing, schools and employment, with public service jobs and affirmative action programs where necessary. Prod lenders to root out bias and step up financing of minority-owned firms. Support and enforce strong civil rights laws. Promote self-help efforts among minorities.
Jobs
Encourage economic expansion to create family-supporting jobs. Foster a business commitment to retraining and education to keep the work force productive. Improve job training for minorities and push to locate more state employment in Milwaukee's inner city.
Education
Promote innovation in education and demand greater accountability by educators for pupil performance, particularly in Milwaukee Public Schools, with emphasis on closing the achievement gap between white and minority students. Encourage greater parental involvement in schools. Demand wholehearted efforts to improve discipline and cut truancy and dropout rates.
Quality of life
Foster and safeguard high-quality-of-life features in the Milwaukee metropolitan area, such as first-rate artistic, sports and recreational opportunities. Support the construction of a new stadium for the Milwaukee Brewers and protect such major community assets as Milwaukee's public museum, zoo, symphony and ballet.
Balanced urban development
Support the renewal of neighborhoods and improve Milwaukee's downtown and lakefront, while preserving open spaces and historically significant buildings. Promote cooperation by Milwaukee and its suburbs to improve mass transportation, housing and job opportunities. Encourage land-use policies that stem urban sprawl and keep development in sync with public services.
Nation and world
Domestic needs
Reallocate unneeded military funds so as to increase investment in education, health, housing, police and infrastructure, especially in cities with decaying environments and widespread poverty.
Campaign reform
Reduce the impact of big money and interest groups on public policy by limiting campaign spending and boosting public funding of elections. Support reforms to enable any citizen, regardless of means or connections, to run for office.
Health
Replace the nation's shamefully inadequate medical care system with an equitably financed plan for national health insurance that (1) assures everyone of a decent minimum level of coverage, irrespective of income, and (2) curbs the rapid growth of health care costs.
Welfare reform
Overhaul welfare programs from top to bottom, on the premise that the poor want jobs and job training, not handouts. Relieve state and local welfare costs by shifting most funding to the federal government. Eliminate incentives for recipients to relocate by setting uniform benefits.
Child protection
Renew efforts to improve the health, education and basic welfare of every American child, regardless of race or economic situation. Encourage government policies that strengthen families and foster parental responsibility.
Sexual responsibility
Encourage responsible attitudes toward sexuality, including abstinence from sex except within a committed relationship. Support aggressive efforts to reduce adolescent pregnancy and sexually transmitted disease.
Economic and tax policy
Reduce the federal deficit through (1) cuts in non-essential programs, (2) aggressive collection of unpaid taxes and (3) where necessary, a boost in income taxes on an ability-to-pay basis. Encourage balanced economic growth, stressing greater savings, investment and productivity.
Balanced transportation
Urge more efficient use of existing facilities. Promote mass transit and van- and car-pooling. Give priority in highway projects to the elimination of safety hazards. Resist excessive construction likely to encourage wasteful land use.
International leadership
Promote the worldwide development of democratic governments that respect human rights and permit self-determination by their peoples. Foster cooperative efforts by industrial countries to help Third World nations help themselves. Support the United Nations as the chief arbiter of international disputes and coordinator of international health and economic assistance.
Trade
Promote trade that is both free and fair. Eliminate artificial barriers and encourage productive investment abroad, particularly development that serves the needs of Third World peoples.
Energy policy
Promote greater energy conservation to reduce dependency on foreign oil, rather than expanded energy production in environmentally sensitive areas. Beef up research into energy alternatives.
Environment
Encourage more aggressive US leadership on environmental issues, including the fight against ozone depletion and global warming. Insist that international lending policies promote environmentally sensitive development. Pursue resource conservation, recycling and the elimination of toxic substances from air, land and water. Back tougher policies to prevent agricultural and urban runoff into waterways.
Military preparedness
Review the purposes and funding of the US armed forces. Clearly define strategic American interests in the post-Cold War era and maintain military flexibility sufficient to their defense. Abandon wasteful and unnecessary arms programs, seeking particularly to dismantle unneeded nuclear weapon systems in the wake of the Soviet Union's collapse. Oppose the proliferation of nuclear arms technology. Support international restrictions on the sale of conventional arms.

Subject: Open spaces; Agricultural runoff; Urban sprawl; Global warming; Neighborhoods; Waterways; Resource conservation; Suburbs; Land use; Climate change; Ozone depletion; Toxic substances; Schools; Urban agriculture; Urban runoff; Housing
