Wrecking global havoc Europe's scrap cars contribute around three million tons per year to refuse dumps. The problem is growing, of course, but how do you tackle it? KEN ROGERS looks at the options

Author: Rogers, Ken

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/wrecking-global-havoc-europes-scrap-cars/docview/293364220/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: When it comes to recycling, Porsche is a unique and certainly esoteric case. Since nobody ever scraps a Porsche, there would appear to be little incentive to make them as recyclable as a mass-market product. The company's Dr Dusan Gruden recently pointed out, that of the 900,000 Porsche sportscars built since 1948, more than 600,000 are still in use. Even so, Porsche claims to be treading the pathway towards easier recyclability. Currently, Europe's scrap cars contribute around three million tons per year to refuse dumps. If their lifespan could be extended from ten to 12 years, says Gruden, this could be reduced by 17 per cent. As long ago as 1974, Porsche pointed out the importance of extended vehicle life-spans as a way of protecting the earth's resources. There is a catch. In order to reduce the sulphur dioxide emissions of diesel, which are greater than petrol, EC legislation is calling for its progressive "desulphurisation". Cleaner diesel will in fact hit the forecourts in January next year - and there could be an additional desulphurisation standard in 1995/1996. But, the additional energy required for this desulphurisation entails a considerable increase in carbon dioxide emissions from the refinery. For every tonne of sulphur retained in the refinery, some 21.5 tonnes of additional C02 are emitted.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Wrecking+global+havoc+Europe%27s+scrap+cars+contribute+around+three+million+tons+per+year+to+refuse+dumps.+The+problem+is+growing%2C+of+course%2C+but+how+do+you+tackle+it%3F+KEN+ROGERS+looks+at+the+options&amp;author=Rogers%2C+Ken&amp;issn=02613077&amp;title=The+Guardian+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-10-19&amp;spage=NOPGCIT&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: As the heady days of the late eighties are succeeded by the lacklustre nineties, Europe's car-makers are increasingly conscious of the murky environmental waters sloshing around their wheels.
The Germans made most of the early running, prompted by a government which suggested the industry cleaned-up its act voluntarily rather than being forced by legislation. So, the buzz-word became "recycling", with most of the major manufacturers clamouring to show that even if they couldn't yet conjure a silk purse out of a sow's ear, they could at least make boot-linings out of all the old dashboards.
The problem of disposing of the 12 million wrecks that Western European motorists scrap each year is a formidable one. In Germany alone, some two million cars are disposed of annually, most in land-fill sites, others just surreptitiously dumped. With its present car population of three million and with an average car lifespan of ten years, by the turn of the century, Germany will have three million scrap cars each year.
The pattern set by the Germans is starting to form in the UK too - mainly instigated by importers of German products. For example, BMW opens its first UK-based recycling plant in the UK this month, and plans nine more by 1994. Its dealers are also part of a programme to recycle waste oil and other polluting by-products of the motor trade.
Volkswagen is initiating a waste disposal programme for its dealers here and has announced it will take back old Series Three Golfs and Ventos for recycling at the end of their useful lives. It is also changing the way it makes cars so that they can be scientifically taken apart when they reach the end of the road, their components re-used, recycled and re-made into new cars.
The most likely scenario, certainly in Germany, is that eventually, licenced salvage firms will be established which, on receiving a scrap car, will endorse the last owner's documents so that there is no longer a requirement to pay tax or insurance on the vehicle.
So far, BMW, PSA Group (Peugeot-Citroen) and Volkswagen, have set up pilot recycling projects, to determine ways of making cars that are more easily taken apart and their components more readily recyclable. Most recently, Rover Group announced plans to set up a joint-venture car recycling facility with materials reclamation specialists, Bird Group.
At these plants, scrap cars are systematically drained of waste fluids, dismantled and all materials separated into individual storage areas. The objectives are either to recondition components or re-use materials to recycle them chemically or mechanically, or to use them as a source of energy.
About 75 per cent of the average car consists of steel, which is relatively easy to recycle after it has been separated from the other materials and fluids it consists of. The most effective way is to feed the scrap shell into a crusher - or "shredder" - which pulverises it into re-usable walnut-sized pellets.
After removal, old plastic bumpers can be granulated then re-made into new components. Although the use of plastic in the construction of cars is on the increase - because of its weight-saving properties - the number of different types of plastics is being reduced, to ease eventual sorting and recycling. For the same reason, plastic components are being stamped with codes to show the specific type of material they are made from.
When it comes to recycling, Porsche is a unique and certainly esoteric case. Since nobody ever scraps a Porsche, there would appear to be little incentive to make them as recyclable as a mass-market product. The company's Dr Dusan Gruden recently pointed out, that of the 900,000 Porsche sportscars built since 1948, more than 600,000 are still in use. Even so, Porsche claims to be treading the pathway towards easier recyclability.
Currently, Europe's scrap cars contribute around three million tons per year to refuse dumps. If their lifespan could be extended from ten to 12 years, says Gruden, this could be reduced by 17 per cent. As long ago as 1974, Porsche pointed out the importance of extended vehicle life-spans as a way of protecting the earth's resources.
"Our study on the theme of the 'long-life automobile' was a logical sequence of the Porsche development and production philosophy," he says. "Although the vehicle was never built, many considerations from this development flowed into Porsche production cars." The company's hot-dip, galvanised body and ten-year warranty against rust perforation stem from this development.
Porsches are expensive, low-volume specialist cars, but a mass-market "long-life automobile' could be good news for the environment - and perhaps, the customer. But it could have decidedly worrying shortcomings from the motor trade and industry's long-term viewpoint . . .
The other area concerning environmentalists is vehicle emissions. In Europe, there is a growing move towards cutting consumption of fossil fuels and thus the generation of "greenhouse gas" carbon dioxide, but the Americans seem solely concerned with "localised" pollution problems. Here, diesel finds increasing favour with environmentalists.
But there is a catch. In order to reduce the sulphur dioxide emissions of diesel, which are greater than petrol, EC legislation is calling for its progressive "desulphurisation". Cleaner diesel will in fact hit the forecourts in January next year - and there could be an additional desulphurisation standard in 1995/1996. But, the additional energy required for this desulphurisation entails a considerable increase in carbon dioxide emissions from the refinery. For every tonne of sulphur retained in the refinery, some 21.5 tonnes of additional C02 are emitted.
It comes down to which problem you want to address most. Is it to be "local' air pollution and acidification, or global warming and climate-change? In the end, it is the womb-to-tomb, crude-oil to tailpipe life-cycle that matters.
Ken Rogers is a freelance writer.

Quayle to fire back at `Murphy Brown': [FINAL Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/quayle-fire-back-at-murphy-brown/docview/306562726/se-2?accountid=10226

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Quayle+to+fire+back+at+%60Murphy+Brown%27&amp;author=&amp;issn=07347456&amp;title=USA+TODAY+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-09-18&amp;spage=04.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: CAMPAIGN '92;ELECTION '92
The script for Murphy Brown's season opener Monday remains TV's hottest secret, but Vice President Quayle is ready to ``blast'' back with a response.
Quayle spokesman David Beckwith confirmed Thursday an upcoming TV Guide report that Quayle's office received two advance copies of Monday night's script from ``someone in Hollywood.''
``The script we saw shows Hollywood twisting the vice president's words - as usual - into something he never said,'' Beckwith says. ``That's why he wants to respond, to correct what the show is saying.''
Details of the script remain unavailable, although some versions have been leaked. Executive producer Gary Dontzig says the show, starring Candice Bergen, is ``not an attack.''
Adds executive producer Steven Peterman: ``Part of this whole phenomena was that once the Republican staffers got a hold of this material, I think they became more worried by the way we handled it. They were hoping we'd be vicious and shoot ourselves in the foot.''
Beckwith tells the Sept. 26 TV Guide, ``If this is the correct script, then it's a real shot at the vice president and it's unfair.'' But he adds one kudo: ``Our original point was that Murphy Brown glamorized single motherhood. The script we saw makes a big deal out of de-glamorizing single motherhood, showing how tough it is. This is enormous progress.'' Needless to say, stay tuned.
DRAFT ISSUE LINGERS: Bill Clinton spent another day Thursday deflecting questions about the draft, this time refusing to respond to new charges by a retired Arkansas ROTC leader. Col. Eugene Holmes, who headed the University of Arkansas ROTC program in 1969, says he believed Clinton ``deceived'' him in order to avoid being drafted. The affidavit by Holmes, who has previously questioned Clinton's statements on the draft, seemed to contradict Holmes' own statements back in 1978, when he praised Clinton.
But in his new statement, Holmes said Clinton didn't tell him he opposed the Vietnam War and had already received a draft notice when he applied in 1969 for an ROTC slot.
Dee Dee Myers, Clinton press secretary, asked: ``What's new about it? What's to say about it? This guy has changed his story. It's curious that 23 years after the fact and 48 days before the election, he's changing his story.''
White House spokesman Marlin Fitzwater said Holmes statement ``goes to the heart of why Bill Clinton should not be president. He was deceitful and he still cannot tell America the facts.'' He labeled Clinton ``Mr. Doublespeak.''
BUSH ATTACK: With attention focused on chaos in European money markets, President Bush linked Bill Clinton's economic ideas to European-style socialism. Speaking in Enid, Okla., Bush warned a crowd of about 2,000 supporters that the Oxford-educated Clinton was influenced by the social democracy and communism that has dominated Europe since World War II.
``Those old social engineers advocated large-scale government ownership to give the state the leading role in the society and economy ... to create a welfare state,'' Bush said. ``I stand with the private sector, the risk taker.''
Bush later traveled to Atlanta, where he announced a plan to roll back regulations in order to cut the cost of insurance on 15-year mortgages backed by the Federal Housing Administration. Housing Secretary Jack Kemp estimates the change will reduce mortgage insurance premiums by an average of $650.
IN BRIEF: Economist Arthur Laffer, the founding father of Reaganomics, said Bush's economic performance has been a disaster and said he would vote for Clinton. ... A group of Little Rock police officers endorsed President Bush as Republican party official Samuel Skinner said in Arkansas that ``governors like'' Clinton are to blame for increases in the nation's crime rate.

Al Gore - Today, testifies to Congress on climate change; Baltimore, College Park, Md. Saturday, Sandy Hook, N.J.; Wilmington, N.C.; Atlanta. Sunday, Washington. Republicans: George Bush - Today, Basking Ridge, N.J. Saturday-Sunday, Camp David, Md. Dan Quayle - Today, Washington. Saturday, Ravenna, Ohio. Sunday, Washington.

Earth Summit: The road from Rio // Bush deserves high marks for summit stance: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Shanahan, John

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/earth-summit-road-rio-bush-deserves-high-marks/docview/306541492/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: More important, most climate-change researchers dispute the theory of catastrophic global warming. Proponents base their predictions on computer models that are fatally flawed. In fact, one model predicted warming by effectively moving the Earth's orbit 2 million miles closer to the sun. The USA is legitimately concerned that finances might not be used for environmental protection. Deterioration in the South often has been caused by the governments themselves. Also, many developing countries are ruled by dictators with a history of lining their own pockets. In the final analysis, President [Bush] gets high marks for his positions at the summit. He braved the jeers of the press and the thunderous applause for Fidel Castro, communist dictator of pollution-ravaged Cuba. The president stood firm in support of principles that protect both the environment and jobs, reaffirming that our nation is ``second to none.''

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Earth+Summit%3A+The+road+from+Rio+%2F%2F+Bush+deserves+high+marks+for+summit+stance&amp;author=Shanahan%2C+John&amp;issn=07347456&amp;title=USA+TODAY+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-06-15&amp;spage=13.A&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: COMMENT;RIO;John Shanahan says the president stood firm for principles that protect both the environment and jobs.;John Shanahan is environmental policy analyst for the Heritage Foundation.
Well, the Earth Summit is over.
Throughout the conference, the American press criticized the Bush administration's positions.
Scandal erupted when a classified memo from U.S. delegation head William Reilly to the White House was leaked to the press.
Germany attempted to undermine the United States by revisiting the terms of the painfully negotiated Global Climate Accord.
Environmental activists characterized the USA as the ``evil empire'' for refusing to provide enormous sums of money to Third World governments without restrictions on its use.
Meanwhile, the president told the world that the U.S. environmental record is ``second to none.''
The important question is: What have we learned from the hoopla and rhetoric of the last two weeks?
In reflection, there are two lessons.
First, the president was right to insist that we study climate change further before mandating drastic measures to curb greenhouse gases, especially since such measures could throw hundreds of thousands of Americans out of work.
More important, most climate-change researchers dispute the theory of catastrophic global warming. Proponents base their predictions on computer models that are fatally flawed. In fact, one model predicted warming by effectively moving the Earth's orbit 2 million miles closer to the sun.
While models predict that global warming already should have occurred, the Earth's average temperature is the same as during World War II, before most man-made gases were produced.
Ironically, the temperature actually dipped from 1945 to 1975, provoking hysterical warnings of an inevitable ice age.
Although the risk of global warming is minimal, the USA is intelligently buying insurance against its occurrence. We will spend $1.4 billion on global climate-change research - more than all other countries combined.
Other countries have talked a good game but have been unwilling to invest in meaningful research.
The second lesson is that this summit was not about the environment. It was about money.
Discussions focused on transfer payments from the Northern developed countries to the governments of the Southern developing countries.
At first glance, the South's demands seem quite reasonable. As one Southern delegate stated, ``You want us {to stop cutting} our trees so you can drive your cars.''
The South fears that its development will be limited, so it demands wealth transfers of $125 billion annually. But without use restrictions.
The North, however, led by the USA, insists on some control over how any transfer payments are used.
The USA is legitimately concerned that finances might not be used for environmental protection. Deterioration in the South often has been caused by the governments themselves. Also, many developing countries are ruled by dictators with a history of lining their own pockets.
In the final analysis, President Bush gets high marks for his positions at the summit. He braved the jeers of the press and the thunderous applause for Fidel Castro, communist dictator of pollution-ravaged Cuba. The president stood firm in support of principles that protect both the environment and jobs, reaffirming that our nation is ``second to none.''
Illustration GRAPHIC;b/w,Marcy Staimer,USA TODAY(Drawing)

The road from Rio: Bush maneuvering for a carbon tax: [City Edition]

Author: Warsh, David

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/road-rio-bush-maneuvering-carbon-tax/docview/294699665/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Certainly the fact that he didn't surrender to the sentimental chorus of good intentions that rose in Rio has made him a symbol of tough-minded resistance to the latest international fad. It was Fidel Castro who received the lusty ovation from the gathered throng, after all; [George Bush] collected the boos. Quite aside from the slogans -- he was defending American jobs, he was defending American sovereignty -- it was just plain caution and good sense that Bush displayed, and it will probably do him much more good than harm in the November election. No economic adviser is more closely associated with President Bush than Professor [Martin Feldstein], who is best remembered for his falling-out in 1983 with President Reagan over the significance of the exploding deficit. Feldstein served as chief adviser to Bush in the 1988 campaign, then propelled his associates, including chief economic adviser Michael Boskin, into key jobs after the election. It is an almost certain thing that Feldstein was speaking of matters that are on the president's mind. Somewhat less well known, in the United States at least, is the swivet into which the carbon tax threw the European Community in May, in the days before the Rio meeting. First the EC's Executive Committee voted for a new tax on oil, gas and coal, starting at $3 a barrel and rising to $10 a barrel by century's end. This was no mere recommendation; it constituted draft legislation for the 12 member nations. The politicians who run the EC prepared to go to Rio to set the whole world an example, by levying a tax on Europe so long as the United States and Japan followed suit.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=The+road+from+Rio%3A+Bush+maneuvering+for+a+carbon+tax&amp;author=Warsh%2C+David&amp;issn=07431791&amp;title=Boston+Globe+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-06-14&amp;spage=81&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   Economic Principals / DAVID WARSH
Poor George Bush. He's really been taking it from the commentators down in Rio. The conventional wisdom is that his performance range was either downright wicked, intransigent or inept. For example, Sen. Al Gore, leader of the US congressional delegation, says "the White House gave the impression of a Keystone Kops-type operation." The same president who did such a good job in rallying support for the Persian Gulf War, the critics marvel, has been such a failure in building consensus on the environment.
In fact, Bush probably has charted what is more or less the proper course -- again.
Certainly the fact that he didn't surrender to the sentimental chorus of good intentions that rose in Rio has made him a symbol of tough-minded resistance to the latest international fad. It was Fidel Castro who received the lusty ovation from the gathered throng, after all; Bush collected the boos. Quite aside from the slogans -- he was defending American jobs, he was defending American sovereignty -- it was just plain caution and good sense that Bush displayed, and it will probably do him much more good than harm in the November election.
What hasn't been noted is that, behind the scenes, his administration has been quietly laying the basis for a concrete deal on global greenhouse gas emissions. With a little luck, it will lead within a year or two to serious international controls.
How so? Well, at the height of the highly public shadow-play in Brazil, the Harvard economist Martin Feldstein called for the adoption of a world carbon tax. "The idea of a carbon tax levied by all of the major industrial countries may not be on the current agenda," Feldstein wrote in an article published on the editorial page of The Wall Street Journal, "but it could turn out to be the most important result of the Earth Summit."
No economic adviser is more closely associated with President Bush than Professor Feldstein, who is best remembered for his falling-out in 1983 with President Reagan over the significance of the exploding deficit. Feldstein served as chief adviser to Bush in the 1988 campaign, then propelled his associates, including chief economic adviser Michael Boskin, into key jobs after the election. It is an almost certain thing that Feldstein was speaking of matters that are on the president's mind.
A carbon tax, were it uniformly levied by the industrial nations, would be the financial engine of a system of tradeable carbon dioxide emission permits, designed to keep global emissions at, say, 1990 levels by whatever methods cost the least. Nations that wanted to emit more greenhouse gases would pay those that emitted less; rich countries would pay poor countries to clean up the Earth.
Such a market-based system would consitute the relatively low-cost "insurance" against the more troubling possibilities of a warmer Earth that the most senior boards of specialists in the last few years have recommended buying. Such a tax would hit oil and gas producers hardest, though it would hardly put most of them out of business; households would scarcely notice it at all. A study in the new Brookings Papers on Economic Activity estimates that such a tax would cost the US economy barely half a percent of gross domestic product over the next 20 years.
A little background: It is well known how, in the days during which the agenda for the Rio meeting was being prepared, the United States persuaded the other industrial nations to abandon a showy agreement that would have been the greenhouse-gas equivalent of a balanced-budget amendment -- a treaty requiring them to stabilize the emission of greenhouse gases at 1990 levels within the next 10 years, without specifying any way of going about it. The United States held out for a treaty without targets, saying all the while that a carbon tax was the right first step.
Somewhat less well known, in the United States at least, is the swivet into which the carbon tax threw the European Community in May, in the days before the Rio meeting. First the EC's Executive Committee voted for a new tax on oil, gas and coal, starting at $3 a barrel and rising to $10 a barrel by century's end. This was no mere recommendation; it constituted draft legislation for the 12 member nations. The politicians who run the EC prepared to go to Rio to set the whole world an example, by levying a tax on Europe so long as the United States and Japan followed suit.
The EC's Executive Committee quickly found it couldn't make stick its bold initiative. Member nations were divided sharply on the measure, with Germany, the Netherlands and Denmark enthusiastically supporting it, and Spain, Portugal and Greece opposing it. The committee thereupon withdrew the measure -- and the EC's environmental commissioner stayed home from Rio to protest. The White House, which had argued to the Europeans that now was not a propitious moment to enact the tax, breathed a sigh of relied.
Welcome to the politics of the New World Order! As economist Feldstein notes, the European commissioners should have understood that no European parliament could enact such a major tax without an extensive national debate; once again, the bureaucrats got out ahead of the politicians. But if the difficulty of passing such a measure was great in Europe, it could only be greater in the United States, where the battle cry "no new taxes" evokes passions that run almost as high as the conviction that something should be done about greenhouse emissions.
It should have been obvious, Feldstein wrote, that "the US could contemplate such a massive tax increase only in the context of a general budget agreement and therefore that it could not be considered until after the election." Now this is really quite an interesting glimpse of the thinking apparently going on at high levels in the Bush administration, even if it does come from a man without official portfolio. It gives considerable insight into the the pressures on the president in this three-way election year: a measure to help balance the US budget and save the planet cannot be mentioned by the president in an election year, because of pressure from his right wing.
Nevertheless, behind the scenes, the administration team is systematically going after the carbon tax. EPA chief William Reilly was baiting America's G-7 allies last week in Rio, telling them that the United States wants a "prompt start" on a climate change convention. Such a meeting would, in effect, be the beginning of a new international regulatory agency, whose work would be to contemplate the emission monitoring, the distribution of emission rights and tax negotiating that is a necessary prelude to beginning the great atmospheric cleanup.
In the end, the international environmental regulatory apparatus will resemble nothing so much as the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade -- the bare-bones, little-understood Geneva-based agency that for 40 years has overseen world trade. The Bush administration is actively putting it together. The president isn't getting much credit for it in most of the simple-minded analysis that has been emanating from Rio, though. That is just another aspect of the greatly increased complexity of politics in the early days of the New World Order.

Earth Summit starts with debate over what can be accomplished

Author: Klein, Gil; Media General News Service

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/earth-summit-starts-with-debate-over-what-can-be/docview/268027771/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: The Earth Summit is about presidential politics and microwave ovens. It's about rich nations and impoverished nations. It's about landfills, nuclear power, oil, wind energy, solar power, coal, natural gas, spotted owls, electric cars, miles per gallon, rising seas and declining rain forests. A U.N. study, which is the foundation for the Earth Summit, warned that without cutting such greenhouse gases as carbon dioxide and methane, the global mean surface temperature will rise by 0.4 to 0.9 degrees each decade, reaching a total of 4.5 degrees by the end of the 21st century. The Earth Summit is much more than a discussion of carbon dioxide emissions in the United States. It also addresses the relationship between developed and developing countries in the aftermath of the Cold War.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Earth+Summit+starts+with+debate+over+what+can+be+accomplished&amp;author=Klein%2C+Gil%3BMedia+General+News+Service&amp;issn=&amp;title=Colorado+Springs+Gazette+-+Telegraph&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-05-31&amp;spage=E.5&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: Label: WORLD
WASHINGTON - Trying to define this week's Earth Summit is like trying to define, well, the Earth.
More than 20,000 people, including at least 100 national leaders, will converge on Rio de Janeiro, Brazil, beginning Wednesday, and nearly every one of them will have a different idea of how to save the Earth from global climate change.
Out of it is expected to come a treaty that will pledge the industrialized countries to limiting emissions of what are known as greenhouse gases.
And they will promise to help developing countries with technology and money to help them improve their economies without adding to the pollutants that are believed to be warming the Earth's atmosphere.
But from there, the sky's the limit.
The Earth Summit is about presidential politics and microwave ovens. It's about rich nations and impoverished nations. It's about landfills, nuclear power, oil, wind energy, solar power, coal, natural gas, spotted owls, electric cars, miles per gallon, rising seas and declining rain forests.
It's about jobs, jobs, jobs. American jobs, Nigerian jobs, Australian jobs, atmospheric scientists' jobs and politicians' jobs.
The summit is based on the supposition that mankind's pollution is altering the Earth's atmosphere in such a way that more and more heat from the sun will be trapped near the Earth. Temperatures will rise.
This is known as the greenhouse effect.
From there the argument goes all over the map and becomes bitter as scientists question one another's motives, as environmentalists battle industry groups and as politicians angle for the high ground.
A U.N. study, which is the foundation for the Earth Summit, warned that without cutting such greenhouse gases as carbon dioxide and methane, the global mean surface temperature will rise by 0.4 to 0.9 degrees each decade, reaching a total of 4.5 degrees by the end of the 21st century.
The World Resources Institute warns that along with inundating part of the coastline, a significant temperature increase would make coastal storms more frequent and fierce, allow tropical diseases and parasites to move north, destroy the natural habitat for many species and turn fertile land into desert.
But a vocal minority of atmospheric scientists disagree with the United Nations' study and the World Resource Institute's predictions. They contend that the study is based on complex computer models of the atmosphere that cannot possibly contain all the factors that determine climate.
The problem, said Richard Grundy, a senior Republican staff member on the Senate Energy and Natural Resources Committee, is that it will be "10 to 30 years before science can confirm what we are debating today."
That may be true, said Philip Barnett, a leading House Democratic environmental staff member, "but if we wait 10 years to do anything, we may have to have a crash program that would be more damaging to the economy than a reasoned program started now."
Spending money now to combat global warming, he said, would be like buying a homeowner's insurance policy for the world.
But the Earth Summit is as much about politics as science.
President Bush looked at the uncertainty among scientists and decided that he would not go to Rio unless provisions were removed from the treaty that set time- tables and reduction rates to eliminate greenhouse gases.
He agreed with business interests that said the treaty could undercut the U.S. economy if the United States had to cut power production.
Bush said he would agree to work toward the United Nations' goal of cutting carbon dioxide emissions to 1990 levels by the year 2000, but he would not agree to having the country required to do so.
Even though all of the other industrialized countries had agreed to specific reduction, U.S. negotiators were able to get them removed from the treaty. Bush announced that he would go to Rio.
Democrats have been quick to castigate the president.
"It was thrilling to see the rest of the world come to the United States and ask us to lead," said Sen. Tim Wirth, D-Colo. "But instead of leading, the United States reached for the lowest common denominator in protection of the global climate."
How much combating global warming would cost the United States is even more uncertain than the science supporting the theory. Estimates range from its destroying the U.S. economy by pushing up the price of energy to helping the economy by promoting energy efficiency.
But the Earth Summit is much more than a discussion of carbon dioxide emissions in the United States. It also addresses the relationship between developed and developing countries in the aftermath of the Cold War.
The developing countries in Africa, Asia and South America are seeing interest in their economic development disappear now that they are no longer factors in an East-West struggle.
They are demanding that if the developed countries want them to maintain their rain forests, which absorb carbon dioxide as well as provide opportunities for pharmaceutical discoveries, then investment must flow to them.
And they argue that an economy struggling at a subsistence level gives off more pollution than one that is thriving because it cannot invest in such niceties as pollution control.
As with so much else with the Earth Summit, how much that will cost developing countries is uncertain.
"We're still not at a stage of defining costs," said Jonathan Pershing, a State Department official working on the Rio treaty. "We're still in the stage of deciding how we are going to prepare reports to study the problem. We are not at the stage of implementing strategy."

People: Bush, George (President)

Full text: Next week's meeting in Brazil will test whether `haves' and `have nots'agree on ecological goals.

Title: Summit aims to protect Mother Earth EARTH SUMMIT '92

Publication title: Las Vegas Review - Journal; Las Vegas, Nev.

Pages: 1a

Number of pages: 0

Publication year: 1992

Publication date: May 28, 1992

Publisher: Las Vegas Review - Journal

Place of publication: Las Vegas, Nev.

Country of publication: United States, Las Vegas, Nev.

Publication subject: General Interest Periodicals--United States

ISSN: 10971645

Full text: OLYMPIA & YORK FACES potentially crucial talks with bankers this week in what may be a last-ditch attempt to avoid financial collapse amid $12 billion of debt.
---
Hafnia spurned Skandia's friendly offer of $504 million in a board-room rebellion, plunging Scandinavia's insurance scene into turmoil.
---
U.S. manufacturing continued to expand in April, but at a slower pace than the strong growth posted in February and March, a survey of purchasing managers indicated.
---
Hungary is the first of the former Warsaw Pact countries to be dropped from a list of countries subject to strict export controls by the U.S. and its allies.
---
Germany is expected to come under pressure from other EC members to back a measure limiting the EC work week even if this means overruling Britain.
---
Lloyds Bank promised to improve the service it offers small businesses, as it and HSBC Holdings stepped up their fight over Midland Bank.
---
Shell Oil's net rose 55% in the first quarter but operating profit plunged 96% as Shell struggled to climb out of a company and industry morass.
---
London share prices gained on Friday, helped partly by expectations of an imminent interest-rate cut by the Bank of England. The FT-SE 100 share index added 5.7 points to 2659.8.
Sterling bonds rallied as the Bank of England appeared to signal it wants lower money-market rates. The National and Provincial Building Society's #150 million issue of five year bonds rose about 0.20 point.
---
The U.S. has indicated a softening of its opposition to international efforts to curb greenhouse gas emissions by the turn of the century. But it is unclear whether this shift can provide the basis for a treaty on climate change in time for the June Earth Summit in Rio.
---
Delft Instruments said its 1991 loss widened to $26.8 million because of sanctions for violating U.S. curbs on exports to Iraq shortly before the Persian Gulf war.
---
Krupp Stahl expects the current year to be difficult. The German steelmaker posted a 43% drop in 1991 operating profit as sales fell 1.7%.
---
Nintendo of America's apparently successful defense of an antitrust suit by Atari is as much a stinging rebuke for Atari, which called the ruling "outrageous," as a vindication for the Japanese-owned concern.
---
The dollar's course in the near term hinges on U.S. employment data due Friday. Traders and economists link the resumption of a rally in the U.S. currency partly to improvement in the weak labor market.
---
Tokyo's market is seen trading in a narrow range in a week shortened by a two-day holiday. Stocks were mixed Friday, with the index falling 87.32 points. But advancers led decliners. Hong Kong stocks may retreat following Friday's surge.

THE CLIMATE CLASH As debate over global-warming heats up, some things are known, but many are not Series: THE WARM WAR. Global Warming: Conflict Over Climate-Third in a Series: [NASSAU AND SUFFOLK Edition]

Author: By Robert Cooke. STAFF WRITER

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/climate-clash-as-debate-over-global-warming-heats/docview/278490392/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: Whether the increase in carbon dioxide has started warming the world's climate, however, is still being debated, more than warmly. Climatologist James Hansen, for one, thinks warming has begun, and that it will be characterized in the 1990s by events such as droughts and floods. Hansen is director of NASA's Goddard Institute for Space Studies in New York City. Climate specialists agree, generally, that global temperatures have risen by about half a degree in the past century. But, while some point to the increase as a sign of global warming, others note that it's less of an increase than predicted by the computerized global-warming scenarios. The increase is also well within the range of natural fluctuations; a half-degree of warming could be just a normal bump in the climate's ordinary ups and downs. Water vapor. It is known that water vapor is itself a potent Greenhouse Gas, and as more moisture gets pumped into the air, warming might be accentuated. But excess water vapor could also condense into clouds, increasing the planet's reflectivity, tending to cool the atmosphere. Oceans. Most of the planet's surface is covered with water, and the oceans are known to be huge "heat sink," a storage system for solar energy. It is not known how the oceans would change, or how rapidly they could change, in repsonse to global warming.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=THE+CLIMATE+CLASH+As+debate+over+global-warming+heats+up%2C+some+things+are+known%2C+but+many+are+not+Series%3A+THE+WARM+WAR.+Global+Warming%3A+Conflict+Over+Climate-Third+in+a+Series&amp;author=By+Robert+Cooke.+STAFF+WRITER&amp;issn=&amp;title=Newsday&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-04-14&amp;spage=59&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text:   See also sidebars- 1) A `Real World' Model 2) Cloudy Calculations
THE REAL AWAKENING began when scientists got their first good, closeup views of other planets. At Mars, for example, the Mariner 9 spacecraft dropped into orbit in 1971 just as a planetwide dust storm was building. Mars' red landscape was completely obscured for weeks until the dust gradually settled.
At Venus, other spacecraft found a greenhouse gone wild, a planet being cooked in its own atmosphere. So much heat is trapped in Venus' dense shroud of gas that everything volatile has been vaporized, creating a perfect picture of hell. Venus' surface temperature is about 900 degrees Fahrenheit, hot enough to melt lead. And the thick, sluggish atmosphere is loaded with sulfuric acid.
While exciting, the discoveries also raised alarm. They provide stark evidence that global extremes can occur, sometimes quickly, sometimes in awesome ways. The lessons, applied to Earth, suggest that earlier concerns about atmospheric changes are worth some worry.
Although it was known as early as 1896 that carbon dioxide, when added to air, traps extra heat, serious concern about the consequences hasn't been widespread until recently. As expanding industry has accelerated use of fossil fuels, the carbon dioxide in air has increased by 25 percent since the Industrial Revolution began. By the year 2050, it may be double what it was in 1860. The change may boost global temperatures anywhere from 2 to 9 degrees Fahrenheit.
Whether the increase in carbon dioxide has started warming the world's climate, however, is still being debated, more than warmly. Climatologist James Hansen, for one, thinks warming has begun, and that it will be characterized in the 1990s by events such as droughts and floods. Hansen is director of NASA's Goddard Institute for Space Studies in New York City.
Other scientists, such as meteorologist Richard Lindzen, at Massachusetts Institute of Technology, disagree, arguing that global warming is not a sure thing. And most researchers think the subtle signs of warming have not become evident within the natural fluctuations of world climate and weather.
Knowns and Unknowns
There is no doubt, of course, that the greenhouse effect is real - and, so far, beneficial. It is greenhouse warming - but in moderate form - that makes the Earth habitable. The "natural" greenhouse effect caused by "normal" amounts of carbon dioxide raises the temperature enough, about 35 degrees (C) higher than it would be otherwise, to make the planet comfortable for life.
The main problem is that even though everyone agrees the air's chemical balance is being changed, no one can say accurately what, if anything, will happen. But there are signs - such as shrinking glaciers, bleached coral reefs, recent severe droughts in Africa and North America and a string of extra-warm summers during the 1980s - hinting that the climate may be warming because of greenhouse gases such as carbon dioxide, methane, nitrous oxide and the chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs).
Those who disagree argue that the string of hot summers was probably a natural fluctuation in continuing weather patterns, with little further impact. Glaciers have long been known to wax and wane. Droughts occur frequently. And a experts even suspect the earth may get cooler rather than warmer because of increased cloud cover and possibly more snowfall.
This wide range of predictions hints at how little is really known about an extraordinarily complex subject, global climate. Because of the atmosphere's extreme complexity, computer models trying to mimic world climate are handicapped by the assumptions they're based on, which suffer from a lack of critical data.
The influence of the oceans, for example, is generally unaccounted for in computer models. And cloudiness of the sky is only roughly accounted for, even though clouds play critical roles in the planet's energy budget.
In addition to carbon dioxide, of course, emissions of the other greenhouse gases have been accelerating as industry expands. These gases include methane (from natural gas and the intestines of cows, sheep and other grass-eating animals, plus termites), nitrous oxide, and the very stable industrial gases called CFCs.
All such factors increase the complexity of the problem, making it harder to create accurate, reliable climate models.
"My own feeling," said atmospheric scientist Ronald Prinn, is that the current climate models "are very uncertain, but I can't rule them out. I realize there are a lot of missing or poorly modeled processes" in the computer simulations. "But since I can't rule out the results being correct, I'm very concerned. I think it's too dangerous to sit and say let's wait until science has all the predictions nailed down.
"We can't wait. I don't think we can wait to move to quickly discussing all the possible ways to do something about rising {levels of} greenhouse gases," Prinn said. "Some of the results are worrisome. Some of the predictions you'd like to rule out, you can't rule out."
Defensive Options
Doing something about greenhouse gases, however, is both difficult and expensive. The most effective policy option - greatly reducing the use of fossil fuels - would hamper development in Third World nations and perhaps cause severe unemployment in the energy, automobile, aircraft and other fuel-dependent industries.
Other options include emphasizing energy conservation, cutting fewer trees and planting more trees. Trees, like all vegetation, absorb carbon dioxide. The use of CFCs is already being reduced, and may soon be phased out, but not to prevent global warming. These chemicals are the culprits thinning the stratospheric ozone that blocks out most of the sun's dangerous ultraviolet rays.
Prinn, recently named director of the new Center for Global Change at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, sees an urgent need for action. "In my mind," he said, "it's time for intensive study of all of these policy options, their costs, and also the costs of doing nothing. It is urgent that this be done with the science fully integrated into the policy-making process."
But, he added, "I would not like to see drastic policies put into place before the fully-integrated study is done."
Lindzen, also at MIT, argues that too little is known to start putting any policies in place. "Anytime you're ignorant, it only means you're ignorant," nothing else, he warned. The numbers in a computer are just that, he says, numbers that don't represent real climate with great accuracy. "And," Lindzen warned, "it could be very bad to legislate on the basis of ignorance."
Pressure to Act
Despite the obvious gaps in understanding of world climate, political pressure is mounting to do something soon about global warming. The Earth Summit conference scheduled for Rio de Janeiro in June, for example, is likely to increase demand to limit greenhouse gases, especially the 30 billion tons of carbon dioxide produced annually by human activities.
But Lindzen, for one, sees no clear need.
"As a scientist," he said, "I find no substantive basis for the warming scenarios being popularly described." And "since we don't see warming in the data, there really is no basis for the hysteria."
Even if warming does result, he said, the increase in temperature will probably be so small "there would be little difficulty adapting."
The real danger, he added, is that "once you commit yourself to a scenario" and try to meet its consequences, it's hard to change course, "even if the evidence is to the contrary."
Atmospheric scientist Michael McElroy, at Harvard University, agreed that "there are deficiencies in the {computer} models. There is no doubt about that. But I feel the question is whether they are good enough; is there any serious reason to suspect that adding greenhouse gases to the atmosphere will not give us a warmer climate?"
Even without computer models, McElroy said, there is strong evidence that the Earth's climate has varied dramatically in the past, being so warm at one time that "alligators were swimming in Alaskan waters. That tells me that the philosophical view that climate is stable and resists change is not correct."
And, McElroy said, "since we are changing the composition of the atmosphere, and since we're making those changes occur very, very rapidly - like in a human lifetime - I think the onus on the policy side is to do what we can responsibly to slow down the pace of change."
Climate `Insurance'
Climatologist Stephen Schneider also thinks the danger of global warming is real - even if the data are incomplete. Because of what he sees as the ominous potential, Schneider advocates changing some human activities. Because of the climate uncertainties, he argues, the changes should be those that are needed anyway, to curb air pollution and acid rain, for instance.
This is often called the "no regrets" approach. Prinn also thinks it is wise, even though he thinks the label is wrong because such changes will cause some problems.
Schneider, at the National Center for Atmospheric Research, in Boulder, Colo., explained in The Bulletin of the American Meteorological Society:
"It is obvious that the things to do first are the things that make the most economic and social sense, regardless of whether global warming materializes.
"Using energy efficiently will not only reduce the prospect of rapid climate change, but will also reduce acid rain, will reduce pollution in cities, will reduce the balance of payments deficit and, in the long run, can improve product competitiveness."
Schneider characterized such proposals - aimed at perhaps a 20-percent cut in carbon dioxide emissions - as climate insurance. Points of Agreement
Points of agreement do exist between supporters and critics of global-warming scenarios:
Global climate is highly variable, even without industrial gases being released into the air. Ice ages - the growth and decline of glacial ice - for example, occur regularly.
The air is changing. Since industrial activity began in earnest, the amount of carbon dioxide in air has gone up, from 280 parts per million in the 1800s to 355 parts per million now. At the present rate of release, and with expected industrial expansion, the amount of carbon dioxide in air may double from the 19th Century figure, reaching 560 parts per million, by the year 2050, modelers estimate.
What remains unclear, however, is how some other factors, such as atmospheric circulation, the oceans as a "heat sink," the polar ice caps, evaporation of water, dust in the air, volcanic eruptions, and cloud formation complicate the picture.
Climate specialists agree, generally, that global temperatures have risen by about half a degree in the past century. But, while some point to the increase as a sign of global warming, others note that it's less of an increase than predicted by the computerized global-warming scenarios. The increase is also well within the range of natural fluctuations; a half-degree of warming could be just a normal bump in the climate's ordinary ups and downs.
At present, Schneider added, an international group of scientists projects "a further warming, from a moderate 0.5 degrees (C) up to a potentially catastrophic 5 degrees (C), or greater, before the end of the next century." Possible Consequences
Schneider noted that several lines of evidence - including ice cores extracted from deep within the thick glaciers of Greenland and Antarctica, indicate that even a few degrees' change in average temperature might be important. "The Earth has not been more than one to two degrees {C} warmer during the 10,000-year era of human civilization," he noted. Also, he wrote in a recent issue of Monitor, a monthly publication of the Christian Science church, that "the previous ice age - in which mile-high ice sheets stretched from New York to Chicago - was only 5 degrees (C) colder than now."
Thus it seems likely that an increase of 5 degrees, if it occurs, could have strong global impact, Schneider said.
"Changes of this magnitude could dramatically alter accustomed climatic patterns, affecting agriculture, water supplies, disease patterns, ecosystems, endangered species, severe storms, sea level and coastal flooding," he noted.
He also explained in the Meteorological Society's bulletin that a 5-degree increase "is the magnitude of global warming that occurred between 15,000 and 5,000 years ago - the end of the last ice age to our present inter-glacial epoch."
One important difference between then and now, however, is that the large natural temperature changes apparently took a long time to occur, something like 5,000 to 10,000 years. Any warming induced by human activities will probably come on much faster, perhaps in 100 years. Species that need time to adapt - tree species gradually shifting northward, for example - might find their comfortable habitat moving north, without the ability to follow.
Most of the crops that humans rely on, however, could be gradually moved to more suitable regions, scientists say. Farmers routinely rotate different crops onto the same land, even to improve the soil, new crops are bred constantly and seeds are easily transported around the world.
Moving tree crops - such as fruit and nut orchards - would be more difficult, obviously, but could still be done in a few years' time
Lindzen, in discussing reasons to view climate-warming scenarios with skepticism, explained why:
All of the complex, computer-based climate models disagree with each other, further underlining the uncertainties.
Recent measurements of the sun's brightness taken by satellites show that the amount of energy delivered to the Earth varies slightly, in step with the sun's 22-year sunspot cycle. Such fluctuations could account for changes seen in global temperature.
Carbon dioxide is not accumulating in the air as rapidly as computer models have predicted. This suggests that a sink - something down which the gas disappears - exists somewhere. One possibility is that living things consume more carbon dioxide than expected.
The carbon dioxide picture is also complicated by the fact that increased amounts of the gas can cause plant growth to accelerate, increasing the demand for carbon dioxide, pulling more from the air.
The greenery is especially important because trees, along with phyto-plankton in the sea, absorb tremendous amounts of carbon dioxide, using the carbon to build tissues in the sun-powered process called photosynthesis. As trees are cut down and the land is turned to agriculture or left barren, a major "sponge" capable of soaking up excess carbon dioxide disappears.
Expected changes in sea level are far from certain. Now it seems that sea level will probably not rise as dramatically as once predicted; coastal cities might be spared from flooding. Lindzen said there is general agreement now that the water will rise only an inch or two - not tens of feet - "even if we get all of the warming" predicted by various models.
In fact, how the oceans might react to global warming may be the biggest joker in the deck. It is thought that the seas' reaction to global change will be lethargic, since it takes about 500 years for a given amount of water to complete a trip around the globe in the deep and powerful ocean currents.
But geologist Wallace Broecker, at Columbia University's Lamont-Doherty Geophysical Observatory, suspects the oceans can react faster than generally expected. The huge ocean conveyor system - which starts with cold, dense, salty water sinking toward the seafloor near Iceland - is what brings warm surface water back to the North Atlantic. Heat released from northward-flowing water is what gives northern Europe its relatively benign climate.
According to Broecker, there is evidence that this huge ocean circulation system can switch abruptly to a different pattern, causing significant climatic changes.
No Winning Argument
According to Broecker, at Columbia, neither side in the argument over global warming has firm enough evidence to present a winnable case.
"If the reality of global warming were put on trial," Broecker wrote recently in Natural History magazine, "each side would have trouble making its case."
The case that Broecker makes is that an expanding human population is the real problem. As population grows, and as demand for fossil fuels grows in step, "annual emissions of greenhouse gases are likely to increase," he said.
So, he added, "I hope the concern about global warming will force us to develop a broader perspective on our planet's future - one that will include the reality of the population bomb." **** Climatic Puzzle: The Odd Pieces
Some major uncertainties remain in the effort to sort out how the climate will respond to rapid increases in carbon dioxide and other Greenhouse Gases:
Cloud dynamics. It is still not known what clouds will do in response to warming. As more water evaporates into the air, cloud cover may increase, blocking sunlight, causing cooling. If clouds form at the correct altitude, however, they tend to block radiation of heat away from the Earth, causing further warming. Or, the two effects may balance, causing no change.
Water vapor. It is known that water vapor is itself a potent Greenhouse Gas, and as more moisture gets pumped into the air, warming might be accentuated. But excess water vapor could also condense into clouds, increasing the planet's reflectivity, tending to cool the atmosphere. Oceans. Most of the planet's surface is covered with water, and the oceans are known to be huge "heat sink," a storage system for solar energy. It is not known how the oceans would change, or how rapidly they could change, in repsonse to global warming.
Sun. Firm evidence has been found showing that the sun's energy output - its luminosity - varies slightly along with the sun's 22-year sunspot cycle. It is also known that long-term, gradual variations in the Earth's distance from the sun alter the amount of energy the planet receives. Such orbital changes are linked to the growth and decline of glacial ice - ice ages.
Particles. Dust, sea salt and other airborne particles act as nuclei on which ice and water collect, forming cloud droplets. The amount of particles in the air can influence how long clouds persist, how long they hold the moisture that condenses to form rain.
Illustration Newsday Cover Illustration by Bob Newman-Man in greenhouse with the earth in the background. Newsday Color Chart by Steve Madden-Climatic Puzzle: The Odd Pieces-See end of text

Who'll pay for our ecological crimes?: [AM Edition]

Author: McLeod, Bruce

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/wholl-pay-our-ecological-crimes/docview/436598309/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: "To anticipate global changes and act in advance, before a potential crisis develops." That's the challenge, Harvard and M.I.T. professors Eugene Skolnikoff and Abram Chayes told 300 U.N. delegates struggling for consensus last week. As for scientists, Union of Concerned Scientists chairman Henry Kendall told the U.N. delegates "we scientists are cautious by nature, hesitant to sound the alarm until we are confident of our findings." The cautious U.N. scientists themselves refuse to draw straight line connections between gas emissions and currently warming temperatures (1990 and 1991 recorded the highest averages in 6,000 years) and rising seas (four islands in the low-lying Maldives have already been evacuated). Unequivocal proof, they say, won't come for 10 years. ("Waiting for the proof will kill us," a Maldivian said.)

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Who%27ll+pay+for+our+ecological+crimes%3F&amp;author=McLeod%2C+Bruce&amp;issn=03190781&amp;title=Toronto+Star&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-03-06&amp;spage=A.23&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: WHEN I HOLD my youngest granddaughter, I touch a future I won't see. When she's my age, the 21st century will be over half gone. Decisions on global warming made today - or not made - touch her world as surely as my arms touch her.
"To anticipate global changes and act in advance, before a potential crisis develops." That's the challenge, Harvard and M.I.T. professors Eugene Skolnikoff and Abram Chayes told 300 U.N. delegates struggling for consensus last week.
The trouble is, said astronomer Carl Sagan, we respond better when the crisis is actual, not potential. It's hard to raise public concern about "invisible gases . . . when the damage happens in the time of our children and our grandchildren."
People don't plan that far ahead. Politicians calculate beyond the next election as easily as corporate planners look beyond their company's bottom line.
As for scientists, Union of Concerned Scientists chairman Henry Kendall told the U.N. delegates "we scientists are cautious by nature, hesitant to sound the alarm until we are confident of our findings."
Yes, he said, there is "broad" scientific consensus on the "likely" range and consequence of global warming. For 150 years, human-caused gas emissions (particularly carbon dioxide from the burning of gasoline, oil and coal) have cumulatively skewed the delicately balanced envelope of air that filters sunlight to the Earth.
Unchecked, the planet's expected to warm 0.3 degrees every decade. The problem's not so much the warming, but the unprecedented rate which leaves the startled Earth no time to adapt.
Possible effects on my granddaughter's world include seas rising 30 to 100 centimetres (warmed water takes more space), uprooted peoples, flooded croplands, prairie deserts, dried tundra, and the tearing of eco-systems on which the web of life depends.
But the breakneck rate of climate change for Earth (confirmed by analysis of eons-old air bubbles in Arctic ice-cores) seems gradual to us.
More than once at the U.N. last week, we were told a frog, dropped in boiling water, will leap out if it can; left in a slowly heating pot, it waits quietly until its boiling death.
There were voices that counselled quiet waiting. A few scientists (like those who once spoke for tobacco companies) were assembled by an industrial consortium including the Canadian Coal Association and Saudi Arabian oil exporters. Referring to "substantial scientific uncertainties," they said, "we don't share the assumption that global warming is a proven immediate threat."
The cautious U.N. scientists themselves refuse to draw straight line connections between gas emissions and currently warming temperatures (1990 and 1991 recorded the highest averages in 6,000 years) and rising seas (four islands in the low-lying Maldives have already been evacuated). Unequivocal proof, they say, won't come for 10 years. ("Waiting for the proof will kill us," a Maldivian said.)
"Shall we wait until the evidence is absolutely compelling?" asked Sagan. "Or shall we take prudent steps beforehand in case the vast majority of scientists are right?"
Kendall said two years ago he took an appeal from 55 American Nobel Laureates and 700 members of the National Academy of Sciences to U.S. President George Bush. "Uncertainty is no excuse for complacency," the appeal said. "Only by taking action now can we assure that future generations will not be put at risk."
The issue's beyond national or company interests. It's about insurance for my granddaughter's world. No global warming policy is adequate which fails to reckon her an argument.
* Bruce McLeod is a minister at Toronto's Bellefair United Church.

Global warming viewed as threat to output of food: [Final Edition]

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-warming-viewed-as-threat-output-food/docview/275278258/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: "The impact of global warming on agriculture may indeed be serious, especially if warming is at the upper end of the range projected," says the report produced for the UN intergovernmental panel on climate change. Some scientists have argued that global warming will help farmers because plant growth rates increase when there is more carbon dioxide in the air, and because warmer temperatures bring longer growing seasons in northern countries. Thawing of the permafrost in the sub-polar regions may release large quantities of methane gas, a powerful greenhouse agent. This would accelerate the warming process.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Global+warming+viewed+as+threat+to+output+of+food&amp;author=&amp;issn=&amp;title=Kitchener+-+Waterloo+Record&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-02-14&amp;spage=F.1&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: (CP) - Global warming may seriously affect world food production, says a report by a UN-sponsored committee of top climate scientists.
The unpublished report, a copy of which was obtained by The Canadian Press, raises serious questions about the ability of farmers to adapt to climate change.
"The impact of global warming on agriculture may indeed be serious, especially if warming is at the upper end of the range projected," says the report produced for the UN intergovernmental panel on climate change.
Average global temperatures are expected to rise in coming decades due to the accumulation of heat-trapping gases such as carbon dioxide in the atmosphere.
Some scientists have argued that global warming will help farmers because plant growth rates increase when there is more carbon dioxide in the air, and because warmer temperatures bring longer growing seasons in northern countries.
Optimists also say biotechnology will produce new crops that can thrive in a greenhouse world.
But the UN report warns such benefits may be offset by drought, pollution, and more frequent insect infestations.
"While recent reviews have emphasized the possibilities of adaptation to climate change, it is important to remember that adaptation may not be complete, and that adaptation may have detrimental effects of its own.
"For example, modifying the types of crops does not guarantee equal levels or nutritional quality of food production or equal profits for farmers."
The report says the improvements brought by biotechnology likely won't be available to farmers in developing countries, and predicts greater desertification and famine in Africa.
Other findings:
The impact of global warming on the forest sector may be severe, especially in countries heavily dependent on forestry.
Snow cover in the Northern Hemisphere has been at record low levels since the middle of 1987.
Thawing of the permafrost in the sub-polar regions may release large quantities of methane gas, a powerful greenhouse agent. This would accelerate the warming process.
The construction and insurance industries may be significantly affected by soil shrinkage and swelling of clay-rich soils.
Computer models developed in the former Soviet Union indicate that the agricultural production in that region would drop five to 10 per cent with a doubling of carbon dioxide levels.
Diseases such as malaria, river blindness and dengue fever could increase or be re-introduced in many countries.
Credit: CP

Unfriendly Skies: [FINAL Edition]

Author: Mathews, Jessica

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/unfriendly-skies/docview/307489500/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: First, the bare facts. A NASA-led team using satellites and aircraft has discovered unexpected, extremely high levels of the most potent ozone-destroying chemical, comparable to, and even higher than levels found in the Antarctic ozone hole. Beneath them, however, were not penguins and phytoplankton, but London, Brussels, Berlin, Moscow and northern Maine. The results mean that the atmosphere is much less able than scientists previously thought to fend off the ozone-destroying effects of chlorofluorocarbons. The discovery greatly increases the likelihood that an ozone hole will soon develop in the Arctic. Unlike the Antarctic hole, which stays put over the South pole, an Arctic hole will slither around, periodically extending as far south as 45 degrees north latitude, north of which lies most of Europe and the former Soviet Union, Canada and several U.S. states. The new findings confirmed earlier predictions that sulfates tossed into the stratosphere in the eruption of Mt. Pinatubu would accelerate ozone loss. This is another instance of a seemingly unrelated man-made change (chlorine in the atmosphere) turning an uncontrollable and otherwise relatively benign natural event (a volcanic eruption) into a severe result. Trees bathed in acid rain succumb to natural outbreaks of pests and disease they would otherwise resist. Deforestation on steep hillsides in the Philippines turned a moderate typhoon last year into a human catastrophe of death by mudslide. On this crowded planet even acts of God are becoming acts of man.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Unfriendly+Skies&amp;author=Mathews%2C+Jessica&amp;issn=01908286&amp;title=The+Washington+Post+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-02-09&amp;spage=b.07&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: More bad news from the upper atmosphere. What is one to make of it?
First, the bare facts. A NASA-led team using satellites and aircraft has discovered unexpected, extremely high levels of the most potent ozone-destroying chemical, comparable to, and even higher than levels found in the Antarctic ozone hole. Beneath them, however, were not penguins and phytoplankton, but London, Brussels, Berlin, Moscow and northern Maine. The results mean that the atmosphere is much less able than scientists previously thought to fend off the ozone-destroying effects of chlorofluorocarbons.
The discovery greatly increases the likelihood that an ozone hole will soon develop in the Arctic. Unlike the Antarctic hole, which stays put over the South pole, an Arctic hole will slither around, periodically extending as far south as 45 degrees north latitude, north of which lies most of Europe and the former Soviet Union, Canada and several U.S. states.
In years when the hole lasted for a long time, pockets of very high ozone depletion would persist into May and June, when the sun is strongest, exposing heavily populated areas to very high levels of ultraviolet radiation, raising rates of melanoma and cataracts. Effects on non-human species are less clear but possibly worse for them, and indirectly, for us.
The new findings confirmed earlier predictions that sulfates tossed into the stratosphere in the eruption of Mt. Pinatubu would accelerate ozone loss. This is another instance of a seemingly unrelated man-made change (chlorine in the atmosphere) turning an uncontrollable and otherwise relatively benign natural event (a volcanic eruption) into a severe result. Trees bathed in acid rain succumb to natural outbreaks of pests and disease they would otherwise resist. Deforestation on steep hillsides in the Philippines turned a moderate typhoon last year into a human catastrophe of death by mudslide. On this crowded planet even acts of God are becoming acts of man.
Happily, something can be done. Under the current terms of the Montreal Treaty, ozone loss will continue to grow until 2000. But revised terms requiring an accelerated phaseout of chlorofluorocarbons and tighter controls over related ozone-depleters can cut the inevitable loss significantly. Meanwhile, maximum diplomatic pressure needs to be exerted on India, the chief treaty holdout, to overcome its habitual antipathy to global problem solving and join the effort.
There is a moral here for policy makers. The findings confirmed a hunch scientists had about a recently discovered mechanism of atmospheric chemistry. There are lots and lots (hundreds, maybe even thousands) of things of comparable significance that we don't know about this planet, because it's study is a scientific frontier. Also, man is creating conditions that natural systems have never before encountered, which may trigger unpredictable shifts. For the next few decades, therefore, surprise will be a constant backdrop to decision making. It has to be expected - and accommodated.
Waiting for scientific certainty, the administration's constant plea, is obviously a non-starter. By that standard there would be no Montreal Treaty and no action possible even now. The only option is to learn to act in the presence of substantial doubt. The sensible way to do that is by taking relatively low-cost steps to reduce the risk of highly unpleasant outcomes - in the same spirit that we buy insurance.
The Senate reacted to the ozone news immediately, requiring tighter controls on the culprit chemicals by a vote of 96-0. The White House went so far as to withdraw its previous opposition, but it has further catching up to do. Regulations on CFCs required by Congress more than a year ago are overdue. Some have not emerged from EPA, others have disappeared into the regulatory black hole of the Quayle Council.
The news should give the administration pause as it reconsiders its posture on greenhouse warming. Without the 1987 Montreal agreement - which involved scientific uncertainty comparable to that surrounding climate change - the world would now be facing a truly frightening situation. As with ozone depletion, there are low-cost options for reducing greenhouse risks, principally through an energy policy that forcefully promotes efficiency. The energy bill now wending its way through Congress takes modest steps in the right direction and should pass this year now that the president has withdrawn his veto threat.
By all accounts, though, internal battles are raging. Skinner is looking for ways to soften the Sununu greenhouse policy. The right wing, encouraged by the president's gimmicky 90-day regulatory moratorium, is beating the drums for no action. The No. 1 item on the Heritage Foundation's agenda of "environmentally sound, free-market goals" for the coming Earth Summit is not to discuss greenhouse warming. It would be funny if it were not so self-defeating.
The Democratic candidates have said little about climate change, though they did electronically attend last week's candidate forum on the subject. Neither Bush nor Buchanan could find the time. Long-term issues are never salient in a presidential campaign, least of all in the midst of a recession. Still, advancing ozone loss is a useful reminder that candidates' thoughts - Republican no less than Democratic - on the future habitability of the planet are worth hearing this year.
The writer, vice president of World Resources Institute, writes this column independently for The Post.
