Pollutants May Slow Warming Of Earth ... But Scientists Say Sulfur Effect Is Short-Term: [L5 Edition]

Author: William Allen Post-Dispatch Science Writer

http://ezproxy.cul.columbia.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/pollutants-may-slow-warming-earth-scientists-say/docview/303525660/se-2?accountid=10226

Abstract: CHICAGO - Sulfur pollutants linked to acid rain may help block global warming in the short run, but so-called greenhouse gases such as carbon dioxide should overcome the cooling effect of sulfur in the long term, scientists said Friday. Recent computer calculations by U.S., German and Swedish scientists showed the "rather surprising" result that acidic sulfur particles in the atmosphere may be partly masking greenhouse warming, said Robert Charlson, an atmospheric scientist at the University of Washington. He and other scientists discussed recent findings on global warming and the greenhouse effect at the annual meeting here of the American Association for the Advancement of Science. The greenhouse effect is a natural process in which carbon dioxide, water vapor, methane and other gases trap heat in the atmosphere. Without the greenhouse phenomenon, the planet would maintain constant below-zero temperatures.

Links: http://rd8hp6du2b.search.serialssolutions.com/directLink?&amp;atitle=Pollutants+May+Slow+Warming+Of+Earth+...+But+Scientists+Say+Sulfur+Effect+Is+Short-Term&amp;author=William+Allen+Post-Dispatch+Science+Writer&amp;issn=&amp;title=St.+Louis+Post+-+Dispatch+%28pre-1997+Fulltext%29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;date=1992-02-09&amp;spage=6.D&amp;id=doi:&amp;sid=ProQ_ss&amp;genre=article

Full text: CHICAGO - Sulfur pollutants linked to acid rain may help block global warming in the short run, but so-called greenhouse gases such as carbon dioxide should overcome the cooling effect of sulfur in the long term, scientists said Friday.
Recent computer calculations by U.S., German and Swedish scientists showed the "rather surprising" result that acidic sulfur particles in the atmosphere may be partly masking greenhouse warming, said Robert Charlson, an atmospheric scientist at the University of Washington. He and other scientists discussed recent findings on global warming and the greenhouse effect at the annual meeting here of the American Association for the Advancement of Science.
The greenhouse effect is a natural process in which carbon dioxide, water vapor, methane and other gases trap heat in the atmosphere. Without the greenhouse phenomenon, the planet would maintain constant below-zero temperatures.
But the burning of coal, gas and other carbon-based materials is producing unprecedented amounts of additional carbon dioxide and other pollutants - enough that many experts believe the atmosphere is gradually warming, with potentially dangerous consequences.
By the middle of the next century, they say, the warming could make the Midwestern climate hotter and drier and trigger other major climate changes.
Critics say there's no evidence of that warming or any of its consequences.
At the science meeting, the researchers said that major uncertainties still exist on the question of how climate change is affected by clouds, snow, ice, ocean circulation and sulfur pollution.
The acidic sulfur particles that concern Charlson are not a component of the greenhouse gases. These particles, known as sulfates, form in the atmosphere as a haze from smelters and coal-burning power plants.
They are the root of acid rain, which has been damaging forests, lakes and other natural ecosystems downwind from the pollution source, scientists said.
Charlson and other researchers a decade ago had dismissed the importance of sulfates. But they examined the role of the particles after realizing that the Northern Hemisphere, where sulfate-producing industry is concentrated, seemed to be warming more slowly than the Southern Hemisphere.
The man-made sulfuric haze cools large parts of the industrial world by reflecting sunlight from the planet's surface, Charlson said. By keeping sunshine from reaching the ground, the haze partly counters the warming effect of carbon dioxide pollutants and other greenhouse gases.
But sulfur pollution should not be thought of as an ally against global warming, Charlson said. And new clean air regulations will lessen the amount of sulfur pollution released over North America and Europe.
"Sulfates last only a few days and are limited to the areas that produce them, but they come from the same pollution sources as carbon dioxide, which remains in the atmosphere for decades," he said.
"When we cut back on fossil-fuel burning, either by policy or because we've exhausted oil, the cooling effect of sulfates will taper off."
Thomas Karl, a scientist with the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, said his recent analysis showed significant warming over many continents in the past 40 years.
Karl traced the history of daily high and low temperatures at various locations in the continental United States, Alaska, Canada, China and the former Soviet Union. He avoided data taken at weather stations near cities, where a "heat island effect" can distort regional temperature measurements.
Since 1951, he said, the average low has risen by about 1 degree Fahrenheit. The average high has risen by only about a third of that.
An accurate picture of how much the planet will warm in the coming decades is important because it will affect how trillions of dollars in global resources are used to address the problem, said Michael Schlesinger, a climate specialist at the University of Illinois.
Schlesinger said a 10-year delay in decision-making would have little environmental effect or ultimate cost. Such a delay would enable nations to more accurately respond to the global warming problem, he said.
(Following text ran in the 3* Edition only)
Other scientists say steps aimed at stemming global warming would be a good insurance policy as well as beneficial to a cleaner environment and energy conservation even if global warming doesn't occur.
